==PAGE:1==
BARRIERS TO A 
CIRCULAR ECONOMY?
2021
AN ASSESSMENT OF THE BARRIERS 
PREVENTING DANISH HOSPITALS FROM 
INCREASING THE RECYCLING RATE OF 
THEIR RESIDUAL WASTE
J. SUND
==PAGE:2==
==PAGE:3==
ABSTRACT
Hospitals are generating more waste than ever before, which has become especially apparent during
the Covid-19 pandemic, due to the increasing use of medical single-use plastics (SUPs). This is
especially the case for high-income countries such as Denmark, which has even been accused for
consuming a much higher degree of SUPs compared to many of its neighbouring countries. Today
the vast majority of Danish hospital waste is incinerated. In fact, low recycling rates are a common
issue among hospitals worldwide. If we are to successfully move towards a circular economy, it is of
great importance that the potential barriers preventing such a transition are identiﬁed and assessed.
This master thesis seeks to assess the barriers that prevent Danish somatic hospitals from increasing
the recycling rate of their residual waste. In order to do so, it has made use of an iterative method,
where three research areas were explored, namely; (1) the application of circular economy in EU and
Danish legislation; (2) the management of hospital residual waste within the ﬁve Danish regions;and
(3) the average composition of their residual waste, including the expected avoided environmental
impacts that are associated with the recycling of the recyclables observed in the residual waste.
The information regarding these research areas, was obtained from literature and interviews, which
were conducted with relevant representatives from across the Danish healthcare sector. In addition, a
Life Cycle Analysis screening model was used to calculate the expected avoided impacts of diverting
the recyclable materials found in the residual waste, from incineration to recycling. Lastly, surveys
were conducted to rank the identiﬁed barriers according to their perceived prevalence.
This study found that although a range of initiatives have been put forth relating to the circular econ-
omy, on both the EU and national level, none were found to be aimed directly at Danish hospitals.
However, several were found to have an indirect impact, such as the required implementation of an
Extended Producer Responsibility (EPR) scheme for packaging. This is expected to have a positive
impact on the packaging waste fraction of the residual waste, which was found to be one of the largest
recyclable material fractions. According to the LCA screening model, its diversion from incineration
to recycling, was also associated to the largest avoided CO2-equivalent emissions among any of the
other recyclable materials. However, with the lack of restrictions regarding polymers in SUPs, the
fraction contained many different types of plastics, of which many were difﬁcult to recycle, and thus
an EPR scheme may assist the hospitals in realizing this potential. However, about 68% of the waste
was considered non-recyclable, of which a large portion was also composed of plastics. With a lack
of EU and national targets speciﬁcally focusing on product manufacturing and design, it seems that
hospitals may face issues meeting the EU recycling targets.
A total of 13 different social, organizational, regulatory and technical barriers were identiﬁed, that cur-
rently prevent Danish hospitals from increasing the recycling rate of their residual waste. However,
Danish hospitals were found to have a very decentralized approach to waste management, where
each hospital is largely responsible for its own sorting schemes. In addition, no formal communication
was identiﬁed between the regions, with respect to waste management. This study argues that these
factors could make it difﬁcult to implement nation wide initiatives related to barrier elimination, so as
to increase resource efﬁciency. Therefore, it proposes an alternative model for a more centralized
approach to waste management, which aims to obtain cheaper costs, efﬁcient decision-making pro-
cesses, smoother implementation of visions and increased transparency. However, this study has not
assessed any of the ﬁnancial aspects associated to transitioning to an alternative model, and thus no
conclusion can be made in terms of whether or not it would be able to sustain itself in practice.
==PAGE:4==
SAMMENFATNING
Sygehuse genererer mere affald end nogensinde før, hvilket er blevet særlig tydeligt under Covid-
19-pandemien. En af hoved˚arsagerne til dette er den stigende mængde af engangsplast der har
vundet indpas p˚a sygehusene. Dette er især tilfældet for højindkomstlande som Danmark, der endda
er blevet beskyldt for at forbruge en endnu større mængde end mange af dets nabolande. I dag
forbrændes langt størstedelen af dansk sygehusaffald. Faktisk er lave genanvendelsesprocenter et
almindeligt problem blandt sygehuse over hele verden. Hvis vi med succes skal bevæge os hen
imod en cirkulær økonomi, er det yderst vigtigt, at de potentielle barrierer, der forhindrer en s˚adan
overgang, identiﬁceres og vurderes.
Dette speciale bestræber sig p˚a at vurdere de barrierer, der forhindrer danske somatiske hospitaler i
at øge genanvendelsesgraden af deres restaffald. For at gøre dette, har den gjort brug af en iterativ
metode, hvor tre overordnede emner er blevet udforsket, nemlig; (1) anvendelsen af cirkulær økonomi
i EU og dansk lovgivning (2) h˚andtering af restaffald fra de danske regioner og (3) den gennem-
snitlige sammensætning af deres restaffald, herunder de forventede undg˚aede miljøp˚avirkninger, der
er forbundet med en genanvendelse af de genanvendelige materialer.
Den viden der blev opn˚aet vedrørende disse emner, blev hentet fra litteratur samt interviews. Deru-
dover blev der anvendt en livscyklusanalyse screeningsmodel til at beregne de forventede undg˚aede
klimap˚avirkninger ved en omledning af de genanvendelige materialer i restaffaldet, fra forbrænding
til genbrug. Endelig, ved brug af spørgeskemaer, blev den opfattede forekomst af hver af de identiﬁ-
cerede barrierer undersøgt.
Dette studie har vist, at selvom der er fremsat en række initiativer, der vedrører den cirkulære økonomi,
b˚ade p˚a EU og nationalt niveau, blev ingen fundet direkte rettet mod danske sygehuse. Imidlertid blev
ﬂere fundet at have en indirekte indvirkning, s˚asom udivdet producentansvar til emballering. Dette
forventes at have en positiv indvirkning p˚a emballageaffaldet, som viste sig at være en af de største
genanvendelige fraktioner af sygehus restaffald. I henhold til LCA-screeningsmodellen var dets aﬂed-
ning fra forbrænding til genbrug ogs˚a forbundet med de største undg˚aede CO2 -ækvivalente emis-
sioner blandt nogen af de andre genanvendelige materialer. Men manglen p˚a begrænsninger p˚a
polymerer i engangsplastprodukter bidrager til forekomsten af mange forskellige typer plast, hvoraf
ﬂere kan være vanskelige at genanvende. Dette vil udvidet producentansvar forh˚abentlig lave om p˚a.
Imidlertid blev ca. 68 % af affaldet betragtet som ikke-genanvendeligt, hvoraf en stor del ogs˚a var
sammensat af plast. Med mangel p˚a EU- og nationale m˚al, der speciﬁkt fokuserer p˚a produktfrem-
stilling samt design, ser det ud til, at sygehuse kan f˚a svært ved at opfylde EU’s genanvendelsesm˚al.
I alt blev 13 forskellige sociale, organisatoriske, lovgivningsmæssige og tekniske barrierer identiﬁ-
ceret. Imidlertid viste det sig, at danske sygehuse havde en meget decentral tilgang til deres af-
faldsh˚andtering, hvor hvert hospital stort set er ansvarlig for sine egne sorteringsordninger. Derudover
blev der ikke identiﬁceret nogen formel kommunikation mellem regionerne med hensyn til sparring
vedrørende affaldsh˚andtering. Derfor argumenteres der for, at det kan blive vanskeligt at gennemføre
landsdækkende initiativer relateret til eliminering af barrierer. Derfor foresl˚ar denne afhandling en
alternativ model for en mere central tilgang til affaldsh˚andtering, der sigter mod at opn˚a billigere
omkostninger, effektive beslutningsprocesser, jævnere implementering af visioner samt øget gen-
nemsigtighed. Det skal dog p˚apeges, at denne afhandling imidlertid ikke har vurderet nogen af de
økonomiske aspekter forbundet med overgangen til en alternativ model, og der kan s˚aledes ikke
drages nogen konklusion med hensyn til, om den ville være i stand til at opretholde sig selv i praksis.
==PAGE:5==
PREFACE
This master thesis has been conducted by Joseﬁne Sund at the Technical University of Denmark, in
the Department of Environmental Engineering. It represents the completion of the Master of Science
degree in Environmental Engineering. The thesis and its defense accounts for 32.5 ECTS points, and
was prepared in the period from the 4th of January 2021 to the 18th of June 2021. It has been written
under the proﬁcient supervision from Associate Professor Alessio Boldrin and Senior Researcher
Anders Damgaard from the Department of Environmental Engineering.
The concept of the thesis was developed in collaboration with Steffan Vaaben and Simon Graasbøll,
consultants at COWI A/S, in connection with an internship in the autumn of 2020.
The outcome of the internship was an internship report, which will be referred to as (Sund, 2020). The
report documented the ﬁndings of a waste audit, which was conducted in connection with a contracted
agreement between COWI A/S, the Region of Southern Denmark and Hospital Sønderjylland. The
waste audit assessed the segregation potential of the residual waste produced at Hospital Sønderjylland.
COWI A/S also produced a report that presented the intended deliverables, which had been agreed
upon in the contract. However the ﬁndings presented by Sund (2020) provide a more detailed descrip-
tion of the methodology, the observed hospital departments and analysis of the waste composition.
Joseﬁne Sund, 152303
Kongens Lyngby, 2021

ACKNOWLEDGEMENTS
I would like to sincerely thank my supervisors Associate Processor, Alessio Boldrin, and Senior Re-
searcher, Anders Damgaard, for providing me with their proﬁcient guidance and feedback throughout
the preparation of this master thesis.
Moreover, I would also like to thank Stefan Vaaben and Simon Graasbøll, consultants at COWI A/S,
for somehow managing to persuade me to assist them on my ﬁrst ever waste audit, of which was
to be conducted on residual hospital waste, in the midst of a global pandemic. Your positive and
helpful nature, made sorting through over 200kg of residual hospital waste a both fun and insightful
experience. Without it, the concept of this thesis would not have emerged.
In addition, I would like to extend my sincerest gratitude to all of the individuals who have taken the
time out of their busy schedule, and contributed to the report, by sharing with me their knowledge and
experience from working in the ﬁeld. Thereby, I would like to thank Thyge Morten Nielsen, Thomas
Madsen and Ole Gerner Jacobsen from the Capital Region; Lærke Møller Toftlund and Jesper Lam-
bert Nielesen from Region Zealand; Birgitte Bagge, Anders Krabbe Nielsen, Marianne Mortensen
and Christina Rasmussen from the Region of Southern Denmark; Maria Gaden, Susanne Backer,
Thomas Dejbjerg Pedersen and Anne Riis from the Central Denmark Region; Torben Kronborg Pihl
from the North Denmark Region; Lucas Absalon and Tiffany Marilou Ramos from Roskilde University;
Gitte Buk Larsen from Aage Vestergaard Larsen A/S; and ﬁnally, Line Geest Jakobsen and Trine Lund
Neidel from COWI A/S. Without your extensive insights, and your willingness to share them with me,
this report could not have been written.
Last but not least, I would like to express my deepest appreciation to my family for their endless
support, and to Hanne Dalsgaard Nicolaisen, Janus Sonne Nielsen and Sebastian Møller Rasmussen
for their revision and feedback on the report, and for providing me with a sounding board, even at the
latest of hours.

1
1
INTRODUCTION
The global generation of waste has increased massively in recent decades, and is showing no signs
of slowing down (Tiseo, 2020). In fact, Hoornweg et al. (2013) have reported that waste is generated
faster than any other environmental pollutants, including greenhouse gases. Although the populations
of high-income countries only account for 16% of the global population, they are responsible for
generating 34% of the total amount of waste produced globally (Kaza et al., 2018). It is thus of utmost
importance, that countries such as Denmark seek to reduce their generation of waste, and increase
their recycling rates in cases where a reduction is not possible.
According to the Ellen MacArthur Foundation (2020b), the increasing waste generation has led world
leaders to embrace the concept of closing the loop in our linear economy, thereby using the waste
to build capital rather than reduce it. The idea of creating a circular ﬂow for resources is not new,
however, the Ellen MacArthur Foundation published one of the ﬁrst ever reports evaluating the busi-
ness and economic opportunities coupled to such a transition (Ellen MacArthur Foundation, 2013).
Since then, terms such as “zero waste” and “circular economy” have become buzzwords among
government- and business policymakers, both in Denmark and on a global scale. To transition to a
circular economy, products should be designed to be disassembled and regenerated, thereby replac-
ing the ‘end-of-life’ concept with restoration (Ellen MacArthur Foundation, 2013). However, depending
on the industry, a transition to a circular economy can be met with a range of different barriers.
One industry that is facing difﬁculties in transitioning to a circular economy, is the healthcare industry,
which has become especially apparent during the Covid-19 pandemic. The reason for this is that
hospitals rely heavily on the use of single-use plastics (SUPs) which are used for countless products,
including latex gloves, intravenous (IV) bags and tubes, face masks, sutures, syringes, surgical linen,
instrument trays and protective gowns. And in order to secure sterility, and both patient and employee
safety, these items are delivered in double-wrapping and complex composites. According to Singh
et al. (2020), the city of Wuhan generated nearly six times more hospital waste than before the virus
hit, at the peak of the pandemic. Of this waste, the composition was greatly inﬂuenced by disposable
plastic-based personal protective equipment and other SUPs. Much of the waste that is generated
from hospitals is often considered hazardous and thus either landﬁlled or incinerated. However, ac-
cording to the World Health Organization (2018b), about 85% of average hospital waste is actually
non-hazardous. Nevertheless, low recycling rates are a common issue among hospitals worldwide.
According to the European Commission (2017) and Ellen MacArthur Foundation (2015), signiﬁcant
direct cost savings for hospitals and health care services can be achieved from the implementation of
circular economy actions, by maximizing their resource efﬁciency and minimizing their waste genera-
tion, through both prevention and recycling. In fact, the Ellen MacArthur Foundation (2015) conducted
a case study on hospitals in Denmark, where they highlighted considerable potential savings from the
adoption of two key circular economy opportunities; (1) the use of performance models in procure-
ment (i.e., where the customer pays for the use of a product rather than the product itself), and (2)
increased recycling and waste reduction through training programmes and through the creation of
national guidelines and/or targets.
Currently, Danish hospitals produce an immense amount of waste, the majority of which is incinerated
(Godt Sygehus Byggeri, nd).
In fact, according to Godt Sygehus Byggeri (nd), Danish hospitals
consume a much higher degree of SUPs than neighbouring countries such as Sweden and Germany.
The hospitals in the Capital Region alone send up to two tonnes of waste to incineration per hour
==PAGE:15==
1.1
OBJECTIVES
2
(Godt Sygehus Byggeri, nd). However, with the adoption of the Danish Government’s Climate Plan
for a Green Waste Sector and Circular Economy in 2020, both the public and private sector will be
required to collect an increased amount of recyclable fractions. In this regard, several hospitals are
making attempts to assess their residual waste fractions, in order to map the existing segregation
potential. In addition, they are assessing the many different products that they use, with the purpose
of producing a list of requirements for their suppliers. With these requirements, they hope to avoid
unnecessary products, and reduce the amount of materials that end up in their residual waste in the
ﬁrst place.
Sund (2020) conducted a waste audit in the Autumn of 2020, in connection with an internship at COWI
A/S, at a hospital in the Region of Southern Denmark. The audit concluded that the hospital had an
average segregation potential of roughly 27% of their residual waste. Nevertheless, upon inspecting
a number of different departments within the hospital, several different barriers were observed, which
could prevent the hospital from realizing their full potential (Sund, 2020). It was therefore considered of
the utmost importance to assess these potential barriers, to assist the hospital in making the required
alterations to achieve an increased level of sorting. In this regard, the general objective for this thesis
emerged. However, instead of limiting the assessment to merely focus on one hospital, the scope
was broadened, so as to conduct an assessment on the national level.
1.1
OBJECTIVES
The general objective of this study is to assess the barriers that prevent Danish somatic hospitals from
increasing the recycling rate of their residual waste. A somatic hospital deals with matters relating to,
or affecting the body. Therefore, psychiatric hospitals and other healthcare services are not included
in this study. Barriers can arise due to many different reasons. Therefore this study seeks to conduct
a broad assessment, taking into consideration the (1) regulatory incentives that currently exist, (2) the
different approaches to waste management that are evident in the Danish hospitals, and (3) the types
of materials that are found in their residual waste. A list of speciﬁc objectives have been established,
with the purpose of achieving the totality of the general objective. Thus, this study seeks to:
1. provide an overview of the current circular economy-related targets, initiatives and policies
that may have a direct or indirect impact on the residual waste production from Danish
hospitals, on both the EU and national Danish level;
2. provide an overview of the waste management systems currently in place among the ﬁve
Danish regions;
3. compare the current recycling rates of the ﬁve regions and their distance to EU targets;
4. assess the composition of residual waste from a number of Danish hospitals and identify
the most prevalent items;
5. propose an average composition of residual waste from Danish hospitals;
6. compare the avoided environmental impacts associated to diverting each of the recyclable
material fractions from incineration to recycling;
7. investigate which social, organizational, technical and regulatory barriers that currently
prevent the Danish regions from realising the segregation potential of their residual waste
fraction;
8. propose possible methods of barrier elimination.
==PAGE:16==
1.2
REPORT STRUCTURE
3
1.2
REPORT STRUCTURE
This study makes use of an iterative approach to identify the barriers faced by Danish hospitals. In
connection with this, three main research areas have been identiﬁed as especially relevant to assess.
Therefore, it has been considered appropriate to deviate from the standard report structure, and to
divide the report into ﬁve main sections, where the following areas will be discussed in detail:
Circular Economy:
An overview of the circular economy concept is presented along with
a description of its application in EU and Danish legislation.
The Regions:
In Denmark, the healthcare sector is managed by ﬁve different re-
gions; the Capital Region, Region Zealand, North Denmark Region,
Central Denmark Region, and the Region of Southern Denmark. A
description is provided of the regional management of hospital resid-
ual waste, based on interviews conducted with employees from each
of the ﬁve regions.
Residual Waste:
An assessment is made of the anticipated general composition of
residual waste from hospitals in Denmark based on three waste au-
dits, which were conducted at four different hospitals. In addition, the
most common types of products that are found will be discussed.
Case study:
A life cycle analysis (LCA) screening model is conducted to as-
sess the environmental impacts that are associated to eight different
waste management scenarios at an average Danish hospital. The
study will be used to gain a deeper insight into the waste that should
be prioritized in terms of diversion from incineration to recycling.
Barriers:
A summary of the barriers preventing Danish hospitals from increas-
ing the recycling rate of their residual waste is provided, based on
the ﬁndings obtained from the previous sections.
KEY FINDINGS
Conclusively for each section, the key ﬁndings that have been discussed throughout that sec-
tion will be summarised. The key ﬁndings will be presented in these text boxes.
OPSUMMERING
Da dette studie ogs˚a henvender sig til danske hospitaler samt beslutningstagere, vil de vigtigste
pointer fra hver af de ovenst˚aende afsnit blive oversat til dansk.
Within each of the sections presented above, the ﬁndings from that given section will be discussed.
However, a ﬁnal discussion will be presented at the end of the report, taking into consideration the
ﬁndings from each section.
==PAGE:17==
1.3
SUSTAINABLE DEVELOPMENT GOALS
4
1.3
SUSTAINABLE DEVELOPMENT GOALS
Figure 1: Sustainable Develop-
ment Goal 12.
Source: United Nations (nd).
In 2015, the United Nations adopted 17 Sustainable Development
Goals that are intended to be achieved by the year 2030. They
”provide a shared blueprint for peace and prosperity for people
and the planet, now and into the future” (United Nations, nd).
This study primarily seeks to mitigate the Sustainable Development
Goal number 12, which deals with responsible consumption and
production, see Figure 1. It emcompasses 11 different targets, of
which this study mainly aims to aid Danish hospitals in:
• achieving a sustainable management and efﬁcient use of nat-
ural resources (Target 12.2) and
• reducing their waste generation (Target 12.5).
1.4
DEFINITIONS
This section will provide deﬁnitions to key terms from the study.
Hospital waste:
All of the different types of waste that are produced by a hospital.
Residual waste:
Waste that does not pose any particular biological, chemical, ra-
dioactive or physical hazard (World Health Organization, 2018a).
Packaging waste:
Any packaging or packaging material covered by the deﬁnition of
waste laid down in Article 3 of Directive 2008/98/EC.
Waste management system:
In this study, the waste management system refers to the various
schemes that are in place, which manage and dispose of resid-
ual hospital waste. This includes any tasks related to sorting,
collection, transportation, and disposal of the waste.
Purchasing agreement:
A binding legal contract between two parties that obligates a
transaction between a buyer and a seller (Chen, 2021). In this
study the purchasing agreement refers to the legal contract be-
tween the hospital or region and the waste company.
Procurement:
The act of obtaining goods or services, typically for business
purposes. Procurement is most commonly associated with busi-
nesses because companies need to solicit services or purchase
goods, usually on a relatively large scale (Young, 2020).
Segregation potential:
The amount of waste that potentially could be segregated if all
generation units, e.g. citizens involved in the program, partic-
ipated 100% and followed the sorting guideline 100% (Chris-
tensen, 2011).
Extended Producer Respon-
sibility:
A policy approach under which producers are given a signiﬁcant
responsibility, which is either ﬁnancial and/or physical, for the
treatment or disposal of post-consumer products (OECD, nd).
==PAGE:18==
METHOD
2
==PAGE:19==
6
2
METHOD
This study identiﬁes the different barriers that currently prevent Danish hospitals from increasing the
recycling rate of their residual waste. It has made use of an iterative approach to explore a number of
different research areas relating to the topic. The iterative approach has been illustrated in Figure 2,
which has been divided into three overall phases; the (1) initial phase, (2) barrier identiﬁcation phase
and (3) barrier assessment phase. The barrier identiﬁcation phase consists of three subphases; (2.1)
planning, (2.2) research, and (2.3) review. All of the phases were conducted during the production of
this thesis, with the exception of the initial phase. This section will provide a general overview of each
phase. However, more detailed descriptions of the speciﬁc tasks which were carried out in connection
with each research area, will be provided within each of the respective sections.
2.1
INITIAL PHASE: CONCEPT
As can be seen in Figure 2, the overall concept of the study was initially established during a waste
audit, which was conducted at Hospital Sønderjylland, prior to the commencement of the thesis. The
ﬁndings from this waste audit have been reported by Sund (2020) and will be referred to throughout
the report. The purpose of the audit was to assess the hospital’s residual waste fraction, and estimate
its average segregation potential. However, during the audit several barriers were observed, that
could prevent the hospital from actually obtaining the full potential, which was identiﬁed (i.e., the total
amount of recyclable materials). In this regard, the aim of the thesis was established.
2.2
BARRIER IDENTIFICATION PHASE
2.2.1
PLANNING
Based on the observations made at the hospital, and the information obtained from the hospital staff,
literature was reviewed with the purpose of deﬁning relevant research areas, which could contribute
to a general understanding of the topic, and thereby, provide an understanding of the barriers. Here,
three research areas where identiﬁed as especially relevant, see Table 1.
Table 1: List of research areas, their descriptions and their primary data sources.
Research area
Description
Data source(s)
Circular economy
The overall concept, and the regulatory incen-
tives, which have been put forth, by both the EU
and Danish Government, that to some degree
have an impact on the residual waste produc-
tion or management of Danish hospitals.
Literature
Regions
The residual waste management practices
which are currently in use by the ﬁve Danish
regions, who are responsible for managing the
public hospitals in Denmark.
Interviews
Literature
Residual waste
The average composition of residual hospital
waste, and the avoided impacts associated to
recycling the recyclable material fractions.
Interviews
Literature
LCA
==PAGE:20==
2.2
BARRIER IDENTIFICATION PHASE
7
Figure 2: The iterative approach behind the barrier identiﬁcation process.
==PAGE:21==
2.3
BARRIER ASSESSMENT PHASE: ANALYSIS
8
2.2.2
RESEARCH
The research phase was divided into three subphases, consisting of one research area each. As can
be seen in Table 1, each of the research areas were explored in different ways. The approach which
was taken, depended on the amount of information which was readily available. Initially, literature
was reviewed regarding each research area. If an insufﬁcient amount of information was available,
relevant interviewees were identiﬁed, in order to obtain the missing information. Interviewees were
typically selected based on their background, and experience working with the given topic of interest.
The interviews were carried out in both an unstructured and semi-structured manner, depending on
the type of information that was to be obtained.
Unstructured interviews:
Involve initially preparing a set of goals, and referring to them
when asking the questions during the interview. The questions
are typically open-ended, which allow for the interviewee to lead
the dialogue.
Semi-structured interviews:
Involve preparing a set of speciﬁc pre-planned questions prior to
the interview. However, this approach also seeks to pursue a
more casual format, like in the unstructured interview, and thus,
some open-ended questions are also prepared.
In connection with the third research area, ’residual waste’, a case study was also carried out in the
form of an LCA screening model. This was done in order to determine the avoided impacts associated
with recycling the different recyclable material fractions that were found in the residual waste.
Each research area provided a different insight, and understanding of the types of barriers, which are
currently prevalent to some degree. Thus, the initial list of barriers, which were observed during the
waste audit by Sund (2020), were reviewed and reﬁned as each research area was investigated.
2.2.3
REVIEW
Upon obtaining a thorough understanding of each of the research areas, the last subphase of the
barrier identiﬁcation phase was carried out, and a ﬁnal list of barriers was prepared. This list was
reviewed and presented for a selected group of representatives, with the purpose of gaining their
opinion of the barriers, and potentially identifying any redundant or missing barriers. This led to the
termination of the barrier identiﬁcation process, and a ﬁnalized list of barriers had been achieved.
2.3
BARRIER ASSESSMENT PHASE: ANALYSIS
Within the ﬁnal phase, the barriers were ranked in terms of their perceived prevalence. This was
done by use of surveys. Here, multiple candidates were selected to participate, with the purpose of
assessing their perceived degree of prevalence of each barrier. Thereafter, the median scores for
each barrier were used as an indication of the central tendency of perceived prevalence.
In addition, the ﬁndings from the LCA were used to identify the main barriers that prevent the recycling
of the materials, which when recycled, were coupled to the greatest avoided environmental impacts.
==PAGE:22==
CIRCULAR 
ECONOMY

==PAGE:23==
10
3
CIRCULAR ECONOMY
Over the recent years, the ’circular economy’ concept has gained recognition and popularity all over
the world, along with increasing prominence in policy development at the national, EU and global
level (World Health Organization, 2018a). Countries are now striving to develop methods of possible
waste minimization and prolonging product lifetime to close the loop in our linear economy. However,
with an expansive amount of circular economy-related strategies, goals and policies being set forth by
both the EU and the Danish Government, it can be difﬁcult to discern which initiatives actually have
an impact on certain industries.
This section aims at providing a general understanding of the circular economy concept, so as to
better understand the extent of which it is possible to adapt the healthcare industry to meet the needs
of such a system economy. In addition, it will provide a general overview of the measures being made
to catalyze such a transition, that are related to the Danish hospital residual waste production and
management. The measures presented in this section will be limited to the EU- and Danish national
level. For descriptions of the regional level measures, see Section 4.
3.1
THE CONCEPT
In a circular economy, products and the materials they contain are kept in use for as long and efﬁ-
ciently as possible, whilst reducing waste to a minimum (Bourguignon, 2016). The Ellen MacArthur
Foundation, a British non-proﬁt organization and one of the main advocates for promoting the circu-
lar economy (Geisendorf and Pietrulla, 2017), has developed a system diagram that illustrates the
essence behind the circular economy, see Figure 3. They refer to it as the Butterﬂy Diagram due
to the two cycles of technical and biological materials. According to the Ellen MacArthur Foundation
(2017a), the circular economy is deﬁned by the following three guiding principles:
1. regeneration of natural systems;
2. keeping products and materials in use; and
3. designing out waste and pollution.
As can be seen in the ﬁgure, renewable raw materials are separated from limited resources. The
renewables are sent through biological cycles (green), and the limited resources are sent through
technical cycles (blue). In the circular economy, a product must be able to be easily separated so that
it is possible to send each of its components into their respective cycle (Ellen MacArthur Foundation,
2017a). What distinguishes these two cycles is that biological cycles regenerate natural systems, as
renewable materials have the ability to be degraded when returned to nature, whereas the limited
resources are ﬁnite and cannot be degraded. Therefore, materials circulating in the technical cycles
are recovered and restored through different strategies.
The production of individual components and the production of the product itself are separated in
order to promote uniformity among the components and to better facilitate their maintenance and
remanufacture. In addition, services are provided to users or consumers, rather than products to
buyers. This is to promote producer responsibility, where product quality, modular design and product
durability are in focus (Ellen MacArthur Foundation, 2017a). In the ﬁgure, this is depicted in the
processes presented between the extraction of resources and the user or consumer of the resources,
in the center of the diagram.
These processes are referred to as ‘parts manufacture’, ‘products
manufacture’ and ‘service provider’.
==PAGE:24==
3.1
THE CONCEPT
11
Figure 3: The Ellen MacArthus Foundation’s Butterﬂy Diagram.
Source: Adapted from the Ellen MacArthur Foundation (2017b).
Figure 4: The Waste Manage-
ment Hierarchy.
The loops that are closest to the center of the diagram, depict
the methods of life extension that are most desirable according to
the Ellen MacArthur Foundation (2017a). Essentially, the diagram
follows the same ranking in terms of preference regarding waste
management as the Waste Management Hierarchy, as shown in
Figure 4. Biological nutrients can safely re-enter the natural world,
once they have gone through one or more cascades (e.g., use
cycles), where they will biodegrade over time and return the em-
bedded nutrients to the environment (Ellen MacArthur Foundation,
2017a). The lifetime of technical nutrients, on the other hand, can
be extended through either:
• Maintainence: ﬁrst and foremost, products and materials are kept in use by extending their
service life for as long as possible through durable design as well as maintenance and re-
pair. This eliminates the need to introduce new products made from virgin materials (Ellen
MacArthur Foundation, 2017a).
• Reuse/redistribution: if maintenance is not an option for the user, products and materi-
als can be distributed to new users in their original form or with few or minor changes or
improvements (Ellen MacArthur Foundation, 2017a).
==PAGE:25==
3.2
EU INCENTIVES
12
• Refurbishment/remanufacture: if products and materials can no longer be either main-
tained or reused, their value can be restored by either disassembling them to the component
level and rebuilt (where components are replaced where it is necessary) to a new condition
with the same warranty as a new product; or by repairing the product as much as possible
without disassembly and replacement of components (Ellen MacArthur Foundation, 2017a).
• Recycling: if none of the previous options are possible, products can be reduced back to
their basic material level, whereby these materials (or a portion of them) can be transformed
into new products (Ellen MacArthur Foundation, 2017a).
Lastly, through the application of the circular economic model, system leakages are minimized (i.e.,
the amount of resources that e.g., are sent for incineration with energy recovery or landﬁll) as prod-
ucts, components and materials are circulated in the system economy for as long as possible. This
can be seen at the bottom of the diagram.
Although the circular economy concept proposes a promising approach to tackling the global eco-
logical crises, its terms are determined by the technologies available, the environmental impacts
associated to the recycling processes and the market conditions (Sørensen et al., 2019).
3.2
EU INCENTIVES
3.2.1
CIRCULAR ECONOMY ACTION PLAN
In 2015, the EU launched its ﬁrst Circular Economy Action Plan, followed by an updated version in
2020 as part of the European Green Deal1.
The ﬁrst action plan provided 54 measures focused mainly on supply-side measures aimed at ad-
dressing negative impacts of products, services and production, and on dealing with materials that
become waste (Pantzar and Suljada, 2020). It also provided revised legislative proposals on waste.
Most of the measures were to be adopted and implemented at the EU level, but certain measures
have encouraged the adoption of circular strategies at the national and regional levels across Europe
(Ellen MacArthur Foundation, 2020a). According to the European Commission (2019b), all 54 actions
have now been completed or are being implemented.
The revised legislative proposals on waste establish a long-term path for waste management and
recycling (World Health Organization, 2018a). Key targets according to the European Commission
(2015) include:
• 60% of municipal waste and 70% of packaging waste is recycled by 2030;
• 65% of municipal waste is recycled by 2035; and
• landﬁll is reduced to a maximum of 10% of municipal waste by 2035.
The new action plan contains 35 different legislative and non-legislative measures, offering a contin-
uation of existing efforts, initiated in the ﬁrst action plan (European Commission, 2020). In addition,
the European Commission (2020) presents several measures related to tackling the rising amounts
of packaging waste, plastic waste and SUPs. These measures will be introduced in the following
sections.
1The Green Deal was ﬁrst presented in 2019, and acts as an overarching package of measures that aim to transform Europe
into the world’s ﬁrst climate-neutral continent by 2050 (European Commission, nda)
==PAGE:26==
3.2
EU INCENTIVES
13
3.2.2
PACKAGING AND PACKAGING WASTE DIRECTIVE
Figure 5: Distribution of packaging waste gener-
ated by packaging material in the EU.
Source: (Eurostat, 2018)
The amount of materials used for packaging is
growing continuously (European Commission,
2020), which within the EU, amounted to 174
kg per inhabitant in 2018 (Eurostat, 2018). Cur-
rently, the main types of materials that consti-
tute European packaging waste are paper and
cardboard, followed by plastic, glass and wood,
see Figure 5. In response, the new action plan
communicated that the Packaging and Pack-
aging Waste Directive (Directive 94/62/EC27)
should be reviewed to ”prevent the production
of packaging waste, and promote the reuse, re-
cycling and other forms of recovering of pack-
aging waste, instead of its ﬁnal disposal, thus
contributing to the transition towards a circular
economy”.
The reviewed directive states that the Member States were required to transpose the directive into
national law by the 5th of July 2020, and that they should ensure that Extended Producer Responsibil-
ity (EPR) schemes are established for all types of packaging by no later than with the entry into 2025.
In addition, it sets speciﬁc recycling targets for packaging waste to be reached before 2025 and 2030,
see Table 2:

it has not been possible to calculate the actual recycling rates (Miljøstyrelsen, 2019, 2020).
==PAGE:27==
3.3
DANISH NATIONAL INCENTIVES
14
3.2.3
STRATEGY FOR PLASTICS IN A CIRCULAR ECONOMY
The EU action plan includes minimizing plastic waste among its key priorities. According to Plastic-
sEurope (2020), 29.1 million tonnes of plastic waste was generated in Europe in 2018, and only just
over 30% of it was recycled (European Commission, 2018). Of this amount, the Commission states
that a signiﬁcant share leaves the EU to be treated in third countries, where different environmental
standards may apply. Thus, in 2018 the EU adopted a “strategy for plastics in a circular economy” to
protect the environment from plastic pollution.
Some of the measures outlined by the strategy include:
• all plastic packaging placed on the EU market must be reusable or recyclable in a cost-
effective manner by 2030;
• changes must be made to production and design of products to enable higher plastics
recycling rates;
• quality standards must be developed for sorted plastic waste and recycled plastics; and
• 10 million tonnes of recycled plastics ﬁnd their way into new products on the EU market by
2025 (European Commission, 2018).
In addition, the strategy outlines the need for a legislative proposal on SUPs. This resulted in the
launch of the Directive on SUPs, which entered into force in 2019, with the intention of gradually
phasing out their use (European Commission, ndb). The directive targets some of the most common
single-use products, of which many are witnessing a complete ban as of July 2021. This means that it
will no longer be possible to import or manufacture such products in the EU (European Commission,
ndb). Targeted products include the following single-use products made of either oxo-degradable
plastics or expanded polystyrene; cutlery, chopsticks, plates, drink stirrers, cups and other beverage
containers, take away food and beverage containers, balloons sticks, straws and cotton bud sticks.
However, the straws and cotton buds, which are used for medical purposes, will not be subject to the
ban (European Commission, ndb).
Other measures of the Directive include ensuring that certain products are marked with instructions for
proper waste management, separate collection for recycling, or that EPR schemes are established.
3.3
DANISH NATIONAL INCENTIVES
3.3.1
STRATEGY FOR CIRCULAR ECONOMY
In 2018, the Ministry of Environment and Food and the Ministry of Industry, Business and Financial
Affairs launched a Strategy for Circular Economy, based on recommendations by an Advisory Board
for Circular Economy, to be implemented in the period 2018-2022 (Danish Government, 2018b). The
strategy encompasses ﬁfteen initiatives within six thematic areas; ranging from strengthening enter-
prises, improving data, digitalization and circular design, to working towards changing consumption
patterns, creating a proper functioning market for waste and recycled materials, and getting more
value out of buildings and biomass.
In the strategy, the Danish Government (2018b) communicates the need for more circular procure-
ment. They state that procurers often lack knowledge about the economic and environmental beneﬁts
associated with procuring circular solutions, and how to incorporate relevant requirements for suppli-
ers. They state that one of the reasons behind this is a lack of tools making it easier for procurers to
==PAGE:28==
3.3
DANISH NATIONAL INCENTIVES
15
set the requirements. To combat this issue, several measures are mentioned with the aim of making
it as easy as possible to procure sustainably, including:
• further developing the portal ”The Responsible Procurer2”;
• conducting an analysis of environmental and economic consequences of procuring in a
circular manner in various ﬁelds; and
• facilitating knowledge sharing and better cooperation between different forums and organi-
zations working with sustainable procurement.
In light of the targets set forth by the European Commission regarding the recycling of municipal
waste (see Section 3.2.1), the Danish Government (2018b) state that they aim for a more harmo-
nized management of municipal waste. Currently, every local authority designs its collection schemes
for municipal waste and sets up its own sorting criteria. This means that private waste collectors and
waste treatment operators must adapt to different local solutions, resulting in an unharmonized na-
tional waste management system and a fragmented market (Danish Government, 2018b). In addition,
the strategy states that this results in some materials being classiﬁed as recyclable in one municipality,
but as suitable for incineration in another. Thus, the Danish Government (2018b) has cooperated with
local authorities, industry associations, and the business community on developing common guide-
lines on sorting criteria and collection schemes for municipal waste, which were launched in late 2020.
The guidelines are an interpretation of the requirements set forth by the Executive Order on Waste
and the objectives of the political agreement Climate Plan for a Green Waste Sector and Circular
Economy. The key objectives will be discussed in Section 3.3.3.
Taking into consideration the EU’s focus on minimizing plastic waste, the strategy also mentions the
launch of the Danish National Plastic Action Plan, to be published later the same year. Key points
from the plan will be discussed in the following section.
3.3.2
NATIONAL PLASTIC ACTION PLAN
The National Plastic Action Plan proposes 27 visions for circular plastic consumption. It states that
Denmark approximately produces 340,000 tonnes of plastics waste annually (Danish Government,
2018a). Of this amount, it states that a mere 18% is recycled, and that the management of plastic
waste differs greatly among the municipalities. In addition, it describes that it currently is difﬁcult to
collect plastic waste of a sufﬁciently good quality and recycle it into new plastics of high quality, that
the existing recycled plastics are of varying quality, and that the demand for recycled plastics as a
feedstock amounts to a mere 6% of the total demand for plastics in Europe.
The plan states that it supports the implementation of the EU’s legislative proposal on SUPs and the
recycling targets for plastic packaging. Key measures affecting the health care industry include:
• establishing a National plastic center, to coordinate the Danish plastics efforts (e.g., in
terms of assessing the barriers for reuse and recycling of plastics in Denmark);
• initiating an EPR scheme for packaging from 2025;
• creating standards for sorting and collection, of plastic waste collection schemes and sort-
ing criteria;
2The Responsible Procurer is a webpage where procurers can ﬁnd green criteria ready to copy paste into tender documents
for a number of product areas and Total Cost of Ownership tools for selected product areas (Danish Environmental Protection
Agency, nd).
==PAGE:29==
3.3
DANISH NATIONAL INCENTIVES
16
• mapping of PVC products on the Danish market and the options for substitution; and
• developing common European requirements for design for recycling.
Although the plan does make mention of measures that will indirectly affect the healthcare industry, it
does not at any point directly address the production of plastic waste at Danish hospitals, or the need
for investigating it.
3.3.3
CLIMATE PLAN FOR A GREEN WASTE SECTOR AND CIRCULAR ECONOMY
In 2020, the Danish Government and several parties in the Danish Folketing reached an agreement
on the Climate Plan for a Green Waste Sector and Circular Economy, which sets forth a number
of concrete initiatives to mitigate the goals described in their strategy. Among the key measures
described in the plan, is the requirement that all Danish municipalities collect the following ten waste
fractions; food, paper, cardboard, metal, glass, plastic, textile, beverage and food cartons, residual
waste and hazardous waste from both households, and the public and private sector. For households,
this must take effect mid 2021 (with minor exceptions), and by no later than the end of 2022 for the
public and private sector. This requirement has been made with the intention of harmonizing the
waste management of municipal waste. Moreover, another key initiative mentioned in the plan to
support harmonization, is the use of common waste sorting symbols, see Figure 6, which will be
described in Section 3.3.4. This requirement follows the individual waste schemes in terms of latest
time of implementation.
The plan also mitigates the visions set forth in the National Plastic Action Plan in terms of minimizing
plastic waste. Here, a requirement is laid down in the Executive Order on Waste, that all Danish
municipalities must, by no later than the beginning of 2022, require a 60% actual recycling rate of its
collected plastic waste (current practices are estimated at under 50% (Dakofa, 2018)).
3.3.4
COMMON WASTE SORTING SYMBOLS
In 2017, a set of common waste sorting symbols were launched, titled the ’The Common Pictogram
System’ (in Danish, ’Det fælles piktogramsystem’), with the purpose of making waste sorting easier
for citizens and corporations in Denmark. The system was developed by The Danish Waste Asso-
ciation, Local Government Denmark (KL) and the Danish Environmental Protection Agency (Dansk
Affaldsforening, nd). The pictogram system consists of 91 pictograms - designed for both waste col-
lected by households, and for waste collected at recycling stations (Dansk Affaldsforening, nd), see
Figure 6. They are composed of a color on a white background, with a label and an ikon.
According to the Danish Environmental Protection Agency (2016), the main purpose of the system
has been to provide people with identical pictograms whenever and wherever they are disposing of
their waste in Denmark - whether they are at home, at the workplace, at recycling stations, or in
public. In addition, the Danish Waste Association advises companies to use the pictograms on their
packaging or products to help indicate its appropriate disposal (Dansk Affaldsforening, nd).
Until now, the pictogram system has been voluntary and free to use, as long as it is used in relation
to waste sorting. As can be seen in Figure 6, so far there are 91 municipalities who are using the
pictogram system. However, as mentioned in Section 3.3.3, their use will be a mandatory requirement
for the collection of household waste from 2021, and from corporate waste from both the public and
private sector, from 2022. The extent of which they have been implemented in Danish hospitals will
be discussed in Section 4.
==PAGE:30==
3.4
REFLECTIONS
17
Figure 6: The Common Pictrogram System and the extent of their national application.
Source: Adapted from Dansk Affaldsforening (nd)
3.4
REFLECTIONS
The EU have communicated their dedication to closing the loop in the European linear economy, and
have set several different targets and measures that have an indirect impact on the Danish healthcare
system. However, no measures have been found that directly target the healthcare sector. In addi-
tion, the majority of the actual targets primarily focus on improving product value chains downstream
(i.e., with respect to increased sorting efﬁciency and recycling) rather than upstream (i.e., optimizing
production and promoting a culture of repairing). This accusation is based on the targets set forth in
==PAGE:31==
3.4
REFLECTIONS
18
their revised legislative proposal on waste and the Packaging and Packaging Waste Directive which
sets targets requiring increased sorting and recycling rates. Nevertheless, they have required that all
Member States ensure the implementation of EPR schemes no later than 2025. In addition, the pro-
duction of several different types of SUPs are facing a complete ban in connection with their Directive
on SUPs, which includes both their production and import within and to the EU. These initiatives are
likely to have a major impact on packaging materials.
The Danish Government have also communicated their desire for a more circular economy and have
communicated their desire to accommodate the EU targets. The government have also communi-
cated their desire for more sustainable procurement practices. However, the national targets seem to
share the same primary focus as the EU, focusing mainly on making improvements downstream in
the value chain. For example, they have stated that they wish to harmonize their waste management
system and, thus, require the mandatory collection of ten different waste fractions from all munic-
ipalities from both households, and the public and private sector. In addition, they have required
the implementation of the Common Pictogram System in all municipalities, in order to simplify the
act of sorting, and minimize contamination of sorted streams. However, no targets have been found
speciﬁcally related to procurement.
With the implementation of the EU and Danish national targets, Danish hospitals will be required to
increase their sorting efﬁciency, and to implement a collection of the ten different recyclable fractions
mentioned in Section 3.3.3. The SUPs that are facing a complete ban have, however, not been found
to be directly linked to healthcare practices. Instead, these are mainly used by their canteens and
kitchens. Thus, the degree of which they will impact the amount of SUPs being disposed of in the
Danish hospitals is not expected to be signiﬁcant. With the implementation of EPR for packaging
materials by 2025, it is possible that an increasing amount of packaging waste will gradually become
more and more recyclable.
However, with no actual targets being put forth speciﬁcally targeting
product value chains upstream, the hospitals may face difﬁculties in increasing their recycling rates
for plastic products.
3.5
KEY FINDINGS
Section 3 has provided an overview of the overall concept of the circular economy, and of
some of the initiatives that have been made on EU- and national Danish level, in terms of
adopting more circular practices in the health care industry.
Concept:
• The circular economy promotes a system where products and materials are kept in use
for as long and efﬁciently as possible, whilst reducing waste to a minimum.
• Products must be designed and manufactured for easy disassembly, such that their parts
can be either repaired, reused, remanufactured or recycled.
Initiatives:
• The actual targets set forth by both the EU and the Danish Government mainly focus on
Continued on following page.
==PAGE:32==
3.4
REFLECTIONS
19
downstream practices in product value chains. For example, the Danish Government
have communicated their desire for a more harmonized waste management system,
where they require the fulﬁllment of the EU’s recycling targets, an increased amount
of sorted fractions in all municipalities by both the public and private sector, and the
implementation of the Common Piktogram System.
• There are no requirements aimed directly at the healthcare system, other than the im-
plementation of a sorting scheme for the following 10 fractions; food, paper, cardboard,
metal, glass, plastic, textile, beverage and food cartons, residual waste and hazardous
waste. However, indirect measures include the required fulﬁllment of the recycling tar-
gets, implementation of EPR and bans on different SUPs. For example, the EU Commis-
sion have communicated that by 2030 60% of municipal waste and 70% of all packaging
waste must be recycled in Denmark. Speciﬁc targets have also been presented for dif-
ferent types of packaging, such as 55% for plastic, whereby only a mere ≈14% is being
recycled in Denmark today.
• With the implementation of EPR in Denmark and the EU, it is likely that a noticeable ef-
fect will be observed in the recyclability of packaging materials. However, it is uncertain
whether hospitals will face difﬁculties in increasing their recycling rates to a sufﬁcient de-
gree, with no targets speciﬁcally focusing on upstream practices in product value chains.

• Den cirkulære økonomi fremmer et system, hvor produkter og materialer holdes i brug

• Produkter skal designes og fremstilles i en kvalitet, der øger deres levetid, idet produk-

4
THE DANISH REGIONS
Since 2007, ﬁve regions have been responsible for ensuring the quality of the Danish Healthcare
System, and for operating the public hospitals (Danish Regions, 2011). A map of the ﬁve regions has
been presented in Figure 7, including their municipalities and placement of somatic hospitals. In Ta-
ble 3 the area, population, population density, and number of employees and hospitals in each region
has been compared. The regions are administrative entities at a level below the central government
but above the 98 municipalities. Their main task is to manage the hospitals. However, they also have
tasks in the social sector, and work with regional development. The regions do not have municipal
powers and are primarily ﬁnanced by block grants from progressive income taxes from the central
government, and by taxes collected by their constituent municipalities (Commonwealth Fund, 2020).
The amounts that are allocated to the regions by the central government are adjusted for demographic
and social differences to ensure an adequate balance between rich and poor municipalities (Ministry
of Social Affairs and Senior Citizens, nd; Commonwealth Fund, 2020). Regions cannot decide their
budgets independently as the overall framework for the regional economy is set out in the annual
ﬁnancial agreement between the government and the Danish Regions3 (Ministry for Economic Affairs
and the Interior, 2014).

==PAGE:37==
4.1
METHOD
24
Currently there is no uniform national waste management strategy in place for hospitals in Denmark.
In addition, hospitals in Denmark are regarded as small and medium-sized enterprises, for which
according to the national Statuary Order on Waste, the Danish municipalities are not responsible for
either transporting or treating recyclable waste fractions. Therefore, each region is responsible for the
management of their own recyclable hospital waste. However, depending on the level of centralization
within each region, the management of hospital waste can vary greatly from hospital to hospital.
As previously mentioned, the implementation of the targets set forth by the EU demand that Denmark
increases its national recycling rates regarding residual waste and packaging waste. This section
will assess the degree of which Danish somatic hospitals need to increase their recycling rates, and
what barriers may prevent them in doing so. In order to determine which barriers each region faces
in terms of increasing their recycling rate, this section aims at providing an overview of the current
management practices implemented for their residual waste, the research that is being conducted
and the initiatives that have been implemented in each region.
4.1
METHOD
Due to the lack of readily available information relating to regional waste management practices, inter-
views were conducted with at least one representative from each region, in order to gain a thorough
understanding of the topic. The interviewees were selected based on their level of insight regarding
regional waste management practices, waste production and recycling rates, and implementation of
initiatives relating to circular economy. An overview of the interviewees from each region is provided
in Table 4. Interviews were carried out based on the seven stages of an interview, proposed by Kvale
and Brinkmann (2009):
1. Thematizing: A purpose for the interviews was deﬁned and pre-knowledge of the subject matter
to be investigated was obtained. Here, a general understanding of the ﬁve Danish regions was
gained from the Danish Regions (2011), the Commonwealth Fund (2020) and the Ministry of
Social Affairs and Senior Citizens (nd) and a more speciﬁc understanding of each region was
gained through each of the region’s respective websites and climate strategies.
2. Designing: Semi-structured interviews were prepared for each region, by listing a set of key
questions that would help deﬁne the areas being investigated, whilst leaving room for conversa-
tion and elaboration. The list of questions were sent to the interviewees a few days in advance,
and they were notiﬁed of the interview structure.
3. Interviewing: The interviewing process was carried out virtually, through Teams during March
and April 2021. One main virtual interview was carried out with at least one representative from
each region, with a duration of 1 - 2 hours. Each interview was recorded with the purpose of
avoiding the need to take notes in real-time. This provided the opportunity to be more responsive
and observant of the interviewees, and contributed to a more smooth dialogue. In addition, this
allowed for key points to be summarized after the interview. Prior to recording the interviews,
permission was obtained from each interviewee.
4. Transcribing: It was not regarded as necessary to transcribe the interviews. However, the key
points from each interview were summarized, and can be found in Appendix A.
5. Analyzing: The key points from the summarized interviews were compared across regions,
in order to gain an understanding of their similarities and differences. This understanding was
used to better identify the barriers faced by the hospitals, in terms of increasing their recycling
==PAGE:38==
4.1
METHOD
25
rates. A ﬁnal overall review of the barriers will be presented later in the report, in Section 7.
6. Verifying: The summaries of each interview were sent to all of the interviewees from the re-
spective interview via e-mail for veriﬁcation. Additionally, all supplementary questions to support
the interviews were asked via e-mail.
7. Reporting: The ﬁndings of the interviews were reported in this section.

Prior to the production of this thesis, visits were made to Hospital Sønderjylland, where the waste
management practices from eight different departments were observed. This provided a general
understanding of waste management practices in Danish hospitals. The ﬁndings related to these
visits have been reported in Sund (2020). Unfortunately, due to the given circumstances regarding
the Covid-19 pandemic, it was not possible to visit more hospitals. Lastly, the following digital debate

4.2.1
ORGANIZATIONAL STRUCTURE AND WASTE MANAGEMENT
(a)
(b)
Figure 8: A (a) centralized and (b) decentralized
organizational structure.
Based on the interviews, it was clear that all of the
regions differed in their approach to waste manage-
ment. In this regard, some of the regions had imple-
mented a more centralized organizational structure,
where the majority of decisions and agreements re-
lated to waste management were made by a cen-
tral organizational body in the given region, see Fig-
ure 8a. Others had a more decentralized structure,
where every hospital was largely responsible for its
own management, see Figure 8b.
In Figure 9 an overview of the degree of centralization of the different regions has been presented.
As can be seen, the regions have been assessed as having either a centralized approach, semi-
centralized approach or decentralized approach to waste management. Regions were considered as
having a semi-centralized approach if they had some degree of centralization, such as one primary
purchasing agreement initiated by the regional level of management, or if they were working towards
increasing their level of centralization.
Figure 9: The regional degree of centralization in terms of their
approach to waste management.
4.2.1.1
CENTRALIZED APPROACH
As can be seen, the Capital Region was the only region with a fully centralized approach to managing
waste. The region had a joint facilities management system called, Center for Real Estate (which in
danish translates to ’Center for Ejendomme’ (CEJ)), which was responsible for operating the waste
management system at the regional level. Figure 10 provides a visualization of the region’s overall
==PAGE:40==
4.2
RESIDUAL WASTE MANAGEMENT IN THE FIVE REGIONS
27
organizational structure in terms of waste management, of which the CEJ has the main authority.
The CEJ was formed in 2015 and reports to the Regional Council. It consists of an executive board
and seven units, which together cover work areas within building operation and maintenance. The
CEJ are responsible for the overall ”external” waste management system for all of the hospitals in
the region, i.e., the collection and transport, and purchasing agreements. The individual ”internal”
waste management systems for each hospital are managed on site, i.e., the sorting schemes in each
department and the collection and transport of waste from the departments to the hospital’s waste
terminal. These are managed by the service and logistics teams at the hospitals, and by the working
environment representatives in each department. However, a standard set of guidelines have been
provided by the CEJ to all hospitals and departments, to secure consistency.
Figure 10: The organizational structure of
the Capital Region’s waste management
system.
Source: Adapted from Nielsen (nd)
In Figure 11a, b and c, on the following page, the typical
waste management setup in any given department in the
Capital Region has been presented. As can be seen, the
region uses different types of waste bins. Smaller bins
are hung on walls for waste types that are produced in
small quantities (e.g., porcelain, electronics, glass and
metal), and waste trolleys are placed on the ground for
waste types that are produced in larger quantities (e.g.,
plastic, residual waste and clinical hazardous waste).
The waste trolleys are found as either single or double
models (containing either one or two bins per trolley re-
spectively). Once bins are full, or an operation or pro-
cedure has been completed, the bins are transported to
the department’s wash room, which is translated from the
Danish, ’skyllerum’, see Figure 11d, e and f. Here the
waste is transferred into larger bins that are collected by
waste porters. Waste porters transport the waste to a
larger waste room, usually placed in the basement level
of the hospital, where the waste is transferred into ap-
propriate waste containers. Waste is to be transported
primarily at the basement level, to the extent of which it
is possible. The waste is then collected from the waste
room, and transported to the hospital’s waste terminal
where it is prepared for collection.
The CEJ has created one primary purchasing agreement for the entire region, to which they send
most of their recyclables from all of their hospitals. The reason why the CEJ has made agreements
with one primary purchaser of their recyclable waste, is to secure a consistent set of requirements for
every given waste fraction, and methods of sorting it across all of their hospitals. In addition it makes
it easier for them to collect their waste related data and monitor their progress. Their mixed waste on
the other hand, is picked up by the municipality, and is sent to incineration with energy recovery.
The Capital Region is for the most part responsible for their own transport of waste, for which they
use a hooklift truck and distribution truck, depending on the fraction. The hospitals in the region use
the same types of waste bins and containers, as shown in Figure 11, which have been marked with
the Region’s own labelling system. However, these have been replaced with the Common Pictogram
System, as a result of the new national requirements.
