4.2.1.2
SEMI-CENTRALIZED APPROACH
Both the Central Denmark Region and Region Zealand were found to have semi-centralized systems.
The Central Denmark Region was in the process of working towards implementing a more centralized
structure, with the recent establishment of its Center for Sustainable Hospitals (which in Danish trans-
lates to Center for Bæredygtige Hospitaler (CfBH)), and with their focus on initiating an agreement
with one primary purchaser for the region’s recyclable waste.
The aim behind establishing the CfBH, has been to intensify their transition to more circular economic
practices, in the region’s hospitals. In this regard, the region has worked towards creating a more cen-
tralized structure in terms of working with sustainability. Maria Gaden, who is the Chief Manager of
the center, stated that the hospitals in the region have previously worked on individual ad hoc sustain-
ability projects. However, she stated that they were interested in gaining a more centralized authority
in terms of decision-making, when dealing with sustainability issues especially (e.g., to implement a
common sorting strategy at all of the hospitals in the region). According to Maria, it is absolutely nec-
essary that some form of authority has the power to make decisions on behalf of the region’s roughly
30,000 employees when dealing with circular economy, as it makes it much easier to implement an
efﬁcient system. The CfBH consists of one sustainability representative from each hospital, that meet
at least once every two weeks to discuss matters related to sustainability and circular economy, and
secure that the hospitals are working efﬁciently towards meeting their goals (i.e., that they are not
conducting identical pilot projects at multiple hospitals, and so on).
The Central Denmark Region has also implemented a sustainability steering group at the regional
level. This group consists mainly of directors from across the region, such as from the Center for
Regional Development, from the individual hospitals and from the highest regional level. Under the
steering group, a technical working group has been established, which consists of members that
have a more hands on or technical approach to sustainability, such as logistics or service managers,
procurement managers and Maria herself. Local sustainability steering groups have also been imple-
mented at the hospital level, to collaborate with the sustainability representatives from the CfBH, on
implementing the initiatives which have been agreed upon by the regional steering group.
The CfBH is working towards transitioning the region from its previously decentralized approach to
waste management and circular economy, to a centralized one. Currently, they are working on inves-
tigating the extent of which they can merge their purchasing agreements, and ideally arrive at one
primary agreement on the regional level. Up until now, each hospital has been responsible for their
own agreements, which has resulted in many different purchasing agreements across the region.
Region Zealand was found to have a semi-centralized system as it had one primary purchasing agree-
ment for the entire region’s waste, which had been initiated by a central organizational body, which
is responsible for procurement, production and logistics. However, with the exception of the purchas-
ing agreement, the hospitals were largely responsible for their own waste management. In addition,
the region did not seem as though it was in the process of working towards increasing their level
of centralization. When asked about whether or not it could be beneﬁcial for the region to adopt a
more centralized approach to waste management, both Lærke Toftlund, an external consultant work-
ing closely with Region Zealand, and Jesper Nielsen, one of the region’s Development Consultants,
meant that it was important to take the local conditions of each hospital into account when planning
the waste management system (e.g., local businesses who might be interested in buying some of
the waste fractions for use in their own production). In addition, Lærke mentioned that it was im-
portant to take into consideration the maturity of the system, with respect to the views and values
==PAGE:43==
4.2
RESIDUAL WASTE MANAGEMENT IN THE FIVE REGIONS
30
among employees regarding their perceptions of waste sorting, which may differ greatly from hospital
to hospital.
4.2.1.3
DECENTRALIZED APPROACH
The Regions of North Denmark and Southern Denmark both had more decentralized approaches to
waste management. In both of the regions, all hospitals were responsible for their own management
and purchasing agreements, and there were no common guidelines for sorting. According to the
interviewees, many of the hospitals did not have any employees responsible for waste management
at the department level. Instead, the majority of tasks related to waste management would be the
responsibility of a service or logistics department, at the hospital level. However, according to Sund
(2020), some employees presented a high level of interest for waste sorting and circular economy,
and would go out of their way to increase the department’s recycling rate.
The North Denmark Region had recently established a central working group to discuss matters
related to waste management and circular economy, which included external consultants from the
Capital Region. With this new group, they hoped to work towards an increased level of centralization
along With the aid of the Capital Region. The Region of Southern Denmark had recently appointed
a new Project Leader, Marianne Mortensen, to harmonize the waste management system of one of
the hospital units in the region, which is comprised of three hospitals. According to her, the waste
management systems were very different at each of these hospitals. In addition, Birgitte Bagge,
the region’s energy and Environment Coordinator, mentioned that they too were looking to make a
regional primary purchasing agreement.
4.2.2
RESIDUAL WASTE PRODUCTION AND RECYCLING TARGETS
Figure 12: The average residual waste production and recycling
rates for each region.
All of the regions produced a signif-
icant amount of residual waste com-
pared to their total waste production,
and had relatively similar recycling
rates. Figure 12 depicts the average
residual waste production and recy-
cling rates for each region, including
their 2030 recycling targets. Accord-
ing to several interviewees, many of
the same suppliers are responsible
for the products procured by the Dan-
ish hospitals. Thus, only a small vari-
ation in the types of materials found
in their waste is expected. The typi-
cal composition of the residual waste
fraction from Danish hospitals will be
discussed in Section 5.
Initially, this section aimed to observe the recycling rates from each region, in order to assess the
degree of which they needed to increase them to meet the EU targets, and as a means for comparison
of each region’s approach to waste management. However, it was found that some of the regions did
not monitor their recycling rate. In addition, the recycling rates have been calculated based on the
==PAGE:44==
4.2
RESIDUAL WASTE MANAGEMENT IN THE FIVE REGIONS
31
amount of material sorted for recycling, as opposed to the actual amount that has been recycled. It
should be noted that not all of the waste, which has been collected for recycling, is actually recycled.
This is due to the potential removal of impurities at the waste facilities. However, it was not possible
to achieve this data. Thus, it is important to take into consideration that the recycling rates do not
represent the actual amounts of recycled materials, but rather a ”best case scenario”, based on the
sorted materials for each region.
It was found that the Capital Region produced the largest amount of residual waste among all of
the regions, producing around 10,000 tonnes per year, which corresponds to about 0.28 tonnes per
employee annually. In addition, they had one of the lower recycling rates, found to be around 20%
in 2019. In their Regional Growth and Development Strategy, the Capital Region have set forth their
own waste targets, stating that they aim for a recycling rate of 40% by 2025, 50% by 2030 and at least
80% by 2035 (Capital Region, 2020). All municipalities within the region have agreed to the targets.
The Central Denmark Region had the next highest production of residual waste, producing around
5,000 tonnes annually, which corresponds to about 0.18 tonnes waste per employee annually. The
region had a recycling rate of about 21% in 2019, which was an increase of about 2% from the year
before (Central Denmark Region, 2019). However, this increase was found mainly to be caused by
the production of an increased amount of construction waste from a building project in the region
(Central Denmark Region, 2019). In their Strategy for Sustainability 2030, the region sets forth three
ambitious targets relating to circular economy. Here, they state that they aim for a:
• recycling rate of 70% by 2030,
• 30% decrease in waste production, and
• 30% reduction in resource consumption (measured in CO2 emissions).
The Region of Southern Denmark produced around 4,900 tonnes residual waste in 2020, which
corresponds to about 0.19 tonnes per employee annually. In addition, the region was found to have
the largest recycling rate of about 26% (Region of Southern Denmark, 2019). According to (Region
of Southern Denmark, 2018), their recycling rate was about 20% in 2018. However, according to
Anders Krabbe Nielsen, a Development Consultant in the region, 2018 was the ﬁrst year where they
accounted for their waste production. Thus, he stated that the amounts measured are associated with
some uncertainty, and that they expected the recycling rate from 2018 to be closer to about 23.2%.
According to Anders, the increase in the region’s recycling rate was caused by a decrease in the
incinerated fraction. In their Climate Strategy 2020 - 2023, the Region of Southern Denmark (2020)
have communicated their desire to obtain more resource efﬁciency, and that they aim to reduce their
total amount of annual waste production by 10%, and increase their recycling rate to 45% by 2030.
Region Zealand was found to have the smallest residual waste production, producing about 2,500
tonnes annually in 2019, which corresponds to about 0.17 tonnes waste per employee annually. How-
ever, the region also produces a range of other fractions that are sent to incineration, which have not
been included in the MWS production. This may, thus, have had an inﬂuence on the smaller residual
waste production compared to the other regions. Additionally, the region currently does not monitor
its recycling rate. It has, therefore, not been possible to provide an estimate. However, based on
the recycling rates from the other regions, it is very likely that it is around 20%. In the region’s Re-
gional Development Strategy 2020-2030, they state their visions in terms of becoming more resource
efﬁcient. The region states that they will work towards increasing their recycling rate, aiming for a
recycling rate of 55% for all of their plastic waste (which is the same as the EU target for plastic
packaging waste). However, no concrete targets have been set forth regarding residual waste, or an
==PAGE:45==
4.2
RESIDUAL WASTE MANAGEMENT IN THE FIVE REGIONS
32
overall recycling rate. It has therefore not been included in Figure 12.
The North Denmark Region was found to produce the second largest amount of residual waste per
employee, producing about 2,900 tonnes of residual waste in 2019, which corresponds to around 0.24
tonnes per employee annually. In addition, the region had the lowest recycling rate, of around 18%.
According to Torben Kronborg Pihl, a Development Consultant from the region, they have become
increasingly aware of challenges preventing them from obtaining valid data for their annual climate
accounts, and how this also affects their abilities in ﬁnding appropriate solutions for increasing their
resource efﬁciency. In this regard, they have conducted a large assessment in collaboration with
NIRAS, on the existing waste streams in the region. They hope that it will allow them to prepare an
ofﬁcial waste strategy by the summer of 2021. In 2010, the region entered into an agreement with the
Danish Nature Conservation Association on being a Climate Region4. However, no concrete targets
have been established for recycling yet.
4.2.3
COMMUNICATION ACROSS REGIONS
With the Danish Regions representing all of the regions in Denmark, some degree of national cen-
tralization does exist. However, it was found that most hospitals in Denmark were largely responsible
for their own waste management, and thus, a national centralization of waste management was not
found. The interviewees were asked whether or not a national waste management group existed,
that facilitated knowledge sharing across regions. To this, the interviewees stated that multiple ERFA-
groups (a group for informal exchange of experience) did exist, with the purpose of discussing different
work areas. In this regard, an energy and environment ERFA-group was described, that would meet
to discuss matters related mainly to energy, where waste management matters were also occasionally
discussed. It was described that this group would meet for half a day, around four times annually, and
that the matters would, thus, be discussed at a very general level. Another interviewee mentioned
a circular economy network, that would meet to discuss matters related to all aspects of circular
economy. However, he mentioned that the topics discussed here were very broad.
Recently, the Danish Regions has focused on facilitating a joint collaboration across regions and other
Nordic countries in terms of discussing procurement, and establishing requirements for the suppliers.
In this regard, they are assessing which products exist on the market as sustainable alternatives, and
which alternative products are missing. However, in many of the regions, the extent of which the
procurement team work together with the waste team is limited. Thus, it is not expected that waste
representatives are included in the discussion of procurement requirements.
Although no national-level of centralization was evident in terms of waste management, many of the
interviewees seemed positive towards the idea. As previously mentioned, Maria Gaden, from the
Central Denmark Region, explained that it was important with some degree of centralization. She
described that in order to facilitate an efﬁcient and sustainable system, it was necessary to make top
down decisions on behalf of the regions and hospitals. She explained that it was beneﬁcial to adopt
a centralized approach so as to allow for a more efﬁcient decision-making process, and to make the
fulﬁllment of visions much simpler, as the authority would be placed in the hands of a smaller group of
people. In addition, she mentioned that it would allow for less confusion in terms of who to report to.
In connection with this, she added that the Central Denmark Region were very interested in facilitating
a national network for sustainable hospitals that could communicate with their own CfBH.
4Climate Regions commit to reducing their annual CO2 emissions by 2% every year until 2025.
==PAGE:46==
4.2
RESIDUAL WASTE MANAGEMENT IN THE FIVE REGIONS
33
4.2.4
IMPLEMENTATION OF THE COMMON PICTOGRAM SYSTEM
All of the interviewees were asked whether or not the Common Pictogram System had been imple-
mented in the hospitals of their region. Depending on the level of centralization, this was not always
possible for the interviewees to answer. However, most of the interviewees were fairly sure that the
pictograms had yet to have been implemented. Typically, it is the company with the purchasing agree-
ment that is responsible for providing the labelling on their waste bins. Sorting guidelines are then
produced based on the labels used by the purchaser.
Most of the interviewees did not have too much of an opinion in terms of transitioning to the common
system. However, a few of the interviewees mentioned their concerns. Here, especially Thyge Nielsen
and Thomas Madsen from the Capital Region, communicated their frustration. They felt that the
Capital Region already had a well functioning system, and that transitioning would imply that they
’degrade’ it. They mentioned that they were not entirely satisﬁed with the new pictograms, as they felt
that some of the fractions, which are currently included in their sorting scheme, are missing from the
common system. After having inquired about the missing pictograms, they were told that they would
have to pay for their production, if they were interested in including them in their own system.
4.2.5
INITIATIVES AND PILOT PROJECTS
According to the interviewees, all of the regions were to some extent in the process of mitigating
EU- and national targets in terms of increasing their level of resource efﬁciency. However, before
implementing new initiatives, the interviewees explained that pilot projects are typically carried out
with the purpose of testing the effects of each initiative. The interviewees described several pilot
projects that either had been completed, were in the process of being carried out, or were in the
preparatory stage. Some examples of these will be described in this section.
4.2.5.1
PLASTIC WASTE
Several regions had carried out a number of projects regarding their plastic waste fraction, and were
in the process of testing the extent of which their plastic waste could be recycled by a local plastic
recycling company called Dansk Affaldsminiminering ApS, which is located in the Central Denmark
Region. Previously, the Capital Region had experienced issues regarding their plastic waste, as their
primary purchaser could not recycle the fraction. In fact, several interviewees mentioned a lack of
efﬁcient local recycling facilities, especially regarding plastic. One interviewee explained, that the low
price of incineration in Denmark, makes it difﬁcult for international more efﬁcient plastic recyclers, to
set up facilities in Denmark. However, according to Dansk Affaldsminimering ApS, they are willing to
take many of the fractions. However, the amount of plastic, which is removed from their operations
prior to recycling, is unknown.
The Central Denmark Region has collaborated with Aarhus University Hospital (AUH) and COWI on
a material ﬂow analysis of their residual waste, over the course of three years between 2016 - 2019
(Gentænk Plast, 2020). Here they focused especially on mapping their plastic waste fraction, with
the aim of increasing the recycling of their resources, and the quality of the recycling of their plastic
packaging waste from hospital equipment. According to Gentænk Plast (2020), it is crucial to act
across both the internal and the external value chain, if one wants to facilitate a successful circular
economy. The external value chain is also referred to as the supply chain, and includes the entire
process from the extraction of raw materials to the disposal of the ﬁnished product. On their website,
they describe how they have worked with both the internal and external part of the value chain.
==PAGE:47==
4.2
RESIDUAL WASTE MANAGEMENT IN THE FIVE REGIONS
34
In recent years, Region Zealand has collaborated with Gate21 on several different projects. Gate21
is an organization that works together with municipalities, regions, companies and researchers, to
test and develop concrete solutions for creating a green transition (Gate21, nd). Currently, they are
collaborating on a project focusing on plastic products and plastic waste. The project aims at reducing
the amount of plastic products that are purchased by the region, along with increasing the recycling
rates. In this regard, Lærke Toftlund explained that pilot projects are currently being conducted at
several different hospitals in the region, where they are assessing the plastic waste fraction in great
detail in collaboration with Roskilde University (RUC). The different types of plastics that are found are
assessed in a laboratory, to properly determine their composition and treatment options. In addition
PhD student Tiffany Marilou Ramos from RUC was observing the extent of which pyrolysis can be
used to treat certain types of plastic wastes (especially of the non-recyclable fraction). The purpose
of using pyrolysis is to produce oil, which can be used to produce new plastic products.
4.2.5.2
WASTE TROLLEYS AND BINS
Along with assessing their plastic waste fraction, Region Zealand is also testing the use of waste
trolleys for sorting different plastic fractions in some of its department wash rooms. The waste trolleys
that are being tested, are the same ones as those used by the Capital Region. However, Lærke
explains that the majority of the plastic waste that is produced in the departments, is disposed of
before it reaches the wash room, and thus, ends up in the residual waste fraction. Therefore, she
states that the ideal solution would be to place the bins at the point of use (e.g., in the patient rooms,
or operating rooms etc.). However, she mentions that they too face spatial challenges which makes it
difﬁcult to ﬁnd room for the bins.
As previously mentioned, the Region of Southern Denmark conducted an assessment of their residual
waste composition at Hospital Sønderjylland in 2020. Here, waste trolleys were proposed to match
the needs of the hospital staff for their operating rooms and other departments (Sund, 2020). In
Figure 13, the trolley that was proposed for operating rooms has been presented in three different
positions. The trolley was initially designed based on the requirements of the operating doctors and
nurses, as no convenient options were available on the market. However, according to Sund (2020),
it could also match the needs of other departments. For example, in ofﬁces, preparation rooms and
patient wards, where there typically is a limited amount of space. In Figure 14, the trolley proposed
for other departments has been presented.
The requirements set forth by the operating staff included that the trolley should not take up more
ground space than the waste trolleys already in use in their departments, and that it should be easily
moved around. Taking these requirements into consideration, Sund (2020) developed a ﬁrst draft
design idea. According to Birgitte Bagge, from the Region of Southern Denmark, they have devel-
oped an initial prototype based on the design, together with a trolley manufacturer. She explained
that they will be testing its impact on their recycling rates from a number of different hospitals and
departments over the course of 2021. As can be seen in Figure 13, the idea behind the trolley was
that it should be equipped with an adjustable lattice, such that it could accommodate hospital staff
in their given working conditions, whether it be sitting down or standing up. In addition, it should be
possible to mount smaller waste bins on the lattice, for different waste fractions, based on the types of
waste produced under the given working conditions. Thus, it would be possible for a staff member to
completely personalize their waste trolley based on their needs. The Central Denmark Region were
currently in the process of implementing weights in their bins, so as to monitor the amounts of waste
that are produced by each department, so as to better design their waste management practices.

36
4.2.5.3
WASTE TRANSPORT
The Central Denmark Region are in the in the process of preparing their hospitals for a more uniform
sorting system, and in connection with this, they have agreed to gather their sorted plastic polymers
at two of the hospitals in the region, where they could prepare the fractions for their further collection.
The region is responsible for its own transport vehicles, which it uses to distribute goods. Maria
explained that they had planned to streamline their use of vehicles to collect plastic waste from the
hospitals in connection with their deliveries. By doing so, the region could minimize the extent of
which the vehicles would have to drive with an empty load.
However, the Central Denmark Region were prevented in carrying out their new initiative, due to a
tightening of a national law regarding transport of empty containers that have previously been used
to store ﬂammable liquids. According to Maria, the law now states that these items can no longer
be transported in an ordinary waste bag or transport cage. They must instead be transported in a
liquid-tight approved container containing an inner bag plus absorbent material. Unfortunately, this is
not possible for the region, if they are to use their own transport vehicles, as it would require that they
purchase hundreds of liquid-tight containers. In addition, she explained that it is not possible for them
to compress the fraction due to a ﬁre hazard. Alternatively, they are now assessing whether or not
they can install a shredder at one of the region’s hospitals, so that they can shred the fraction instead,
and transport it as ﬂakes.
4.2.5.4
ONLINE PLATFORMS
Currently, most of the regions use nudging and education as a way to push their employees towards
increased sorting. Here, the regions typically use online platforms to share information and teaching
material. However, these platforms are typically not accessible for members outside of the region.
According to Maria, from the Central Denmark Region, many of the observations made from e.g., pilot
projects regarding hospital waste are very relevant on the national level. In this regard, she states
that the region is currently in the process of creating an online open platform, with the intention of
sharing their sustainability projects and results. The aim behind this platform is to create transparency
throughout the region, and give all of the hospitals in the region, including all of the other regions, the
opportunity to learn from their insights.
The Central Denmark Region has also shared its ﬁndings and insights regarding its material ﬂow
analysis, which was conducted in collaboration with COWI A/S and is described in Section 4.2.5.1,
on the online platform, GENTÆNK PLAST (which in Danish means, ’rethink plastic’). It can be found
here, otherwise see Figure 15a and b. The results from their analysis have been presented in a
database, which can be downloaded directly from the online platform, see see Figure 15c and d. The
purpose of the platform is to disseminate and share the knowledge that has been acquired through
the project, so that others can ﬁnd inspiration and use the tools that have been developed. The region
states that their analysis has had its primary focus on plastic packaging in hospitals, however, that a
large part of the content, especially regarding their ‘process guide’, can be applied in other contexts
(Gentænk Plast, 2020).
Several other interviewees mentioned the use of an existing online marketplace for redistributing old
furniture and other devices or equipment. However, the extent of which this platform was actually used
was unknown. Interviewees mentioned that some of the hospitals had a room that was dedicated to
the storage of the old furniture or equipment that were to be put up for redistribution through the
platform. However, they were unsure of whether or not staff members actually registered the items.

When asked whether it could be helpful with an online platform for knowledge sharing across regions,
some of the interviewees expressed that using such a platform seemed like a lot of work on top of
their already busy work day. They explained that they preferred to have information sent to them by
e-mail, if the content required their direct attention. However, most of the interviewees agreed that an
online platform could be beneﬁcial.
4.3
REFLECTIONS
As it has been described, all of the regions differ in their approach to waste management, with some
taking on a centralized approach, and others a more decentralized approach. However, the major-
ity of the regions take a decentralized approach to waste management, with the exception of the
Capital Region. Thus, many hospitals are responsible for organizing their own waste management
systems, resulting in many different approaches to waste management, and many different purchas-
ing agreements. Yet, in most hospitals it seems that a service or logistics department is responsible
for managing waste from the hospital-level, and that there rarely is any formal department-level man-
agement. Nevertheless, many of the interviewees communicated their desire for a more centralized
system, due to a number of associated beneﬁts. According to the Corporate Finance Institute (2021),
==PAGE:51==
4.3
REFLECTIONS
38
beneﬁts include:
• cheaper costs associated to having one primary regional purchasing agreement, reduced
duplication of responsibilities, securing that identical assessments are not carried out si-
multaneously, and the reduced need to hire specialists (as the amount of systems in use
are reduced);
• efﬁcient decision-making processes as decisions are made by a limited group of people,
and thus, many topics can be discussed in a single meeting;
• a smoother implementation of visions, initiatives and strategies as decisions are made top
down, and thus, allow little room for conﬂict; and
• a clear chain of command and lines of communication, and thus, increased transparency
across the regions in terms of knowing who to report to or consult with;
However, some of the interviewees also mentioned that an increased level of centralization could
oversee local opportunities or issues. Thus, they expressed their concern with increasing the level of
centralization. According to the Corporate Finance Institute (2021), a more centralized approach can
also have some disadvantages. These include a lack of:
• understanding of issues related to implementation of initiatives among decision makers, as
they do not take part in the actual implementation processes; and
• employee loyalty, as employees are required to perform tasks conceptualized by top exec-
utives, and are thus deprived of the ability to use their own creativity, and suggest ways of
performing certain tasks.
The Capital Region had not only applied a centralized approach to waste management in terms of
their decision-making. They were also the only region to have taken on the responsibility for their own
waste transportation. Interviewees from the other regions argued that this was not possible outside
of the Capital Region, due to the large geographical differences. The Capital Region is the smallest
of the regions, and thus, the distance between each hospital is much smaller compared to the other
regions. It could be argued that this difference in geography among the regions, could also cause for
difﬁculties in terms of national-level, centralized decision-making.
The Capital Region was found to have the highest average residual waste production, and one of
the lowest recycling rates among the ﬁve regions. Although this does not seem to favor a centralized
waste management system, several factors must be taken into consideration before such a conclusion
can be drawn. For example, as previously mentioned, the recycling rates should not be interpreted as
the actual amounts of waste recycled from each region. The recycling rates were calculated based
on the amount of waste sorted by each region for recycling. However, in many cases, depending
on the quality of the sorted fraction, some of the sorted fraction is removed prior to recycling by the
purchaser, and thus, should not be included in the actual recycling rate. As the recycling rate does
not provide any information in terms of the quality of the segregated fractions, a better performance
among employees in terms of sorting, can have negative implications on the recycling rates obtained.
In such a case, employees may produce a smaller amount of segregated waste, however, with a
higher quality. Thus, less waste will be removed for alternative treatment, such as incineration, further
down the value chain. This would result in a higher actual recycling rate, as opposed to a fraction of
low quality, where a larger fraction of the sorted waste is regarded as non-recyclable. In order to draw
any conclusions on the effects of centralization on the recycling rate, the actual recycling rates must
be obtained for each region.
==PAGE:52==
4.3
REFLECTIONS
39
In addition, some of the regions had disregarded certain fractions, which are associated to high annual
ﬂuctuations (e.g., construction waste), when calculating their recycling rate. This was done to better
monitor the progress made by hospital staff in terms of sorting abilities from year to year. Moreover
some regions included other fractions that were sent to incineration in their residual waste fraction,
whereas others did not. Both of these factors could also contribute to some regions having a higher
residual waste production than others or a lower recycling rate. In order to provide a better basis
for comparison, the accounting methods regarding the recycling rate and residual waste production
should be investigated further, in order to secure that they have been accounted for identically.
The regions had very different 2030 recycling targets. Here, some regions had very ambitious targets,
aiming for a recycling rate of 70%, and others no targets at all. However, the regions that currently
were found to have the greatest insight into their waste compositions, seemed to be among the regions
with the highest targets. Nevertheless, all of the regions must at least double their average calculated
recycling rates if they are to meet the EU- and national targets.
It seems that most of the regions face issures regarding spatial limitations. In order to combat this
issue, several of them were looking into developing different types of waste trolleys. However, the
Capital Region currently had the same types of waste trolleys installed at all of their hospitals, Region
Zealand were in the process of testing these bins at some of the hospitals in their own region, and
both the Region of Southern Denmark and the Central Denmark Region were in the process of testing
their own waste trolleys.
4.4
KEY FINDINGS
Section 4 has provided an overview of the residual waste management systems that are in
use by Danish somatic hospitals, including their implementation of initiatives and conducted
research relating to increasing their resource efﬁciency. The section is written largely based
on semi-structured interviews, that were conducted with a total of ten representatives - at least
one from each of the Danish regions.
Waste management:
• The regions differ in their system maturity and approach to waste management, with most
regions having a very decentralized approach, where all of their hospitals are responsible
for their own management. Only the Capital Region has a fully centralized approach,
where one organization, at the regional level, deﬁnes a set of common guidelines on
behalf of all hospitals in the region.
• There is no formal communication across regions regarding waste management. Rep-
resentatives from the regions meet a few times per year to discuss matters related to
energy and environment. However, the time spent on discussing matters that relate to
waste management is limited.
• Most of the regions face issues regarding spatial limitations. This made it difﬁcult for them
to implement enhanced sorting schemes in their medical departments.
Continued on following page.
==PAGE:53==
4.3
REFLECTIONS
40
Waste production and targets:
• The recycling rates among hospitals in Denmark range from 18% to 26%. However, not
all regions calculate their recycling rate in the same manner, and some regions do not
calculate one at all. This makes it difﬁcult to monitor the progress made by all of the
regions in terms of meeting the EU recycling targets. In addition, the recycling rates are
measured based on materials sorted for recycling, and not what is actually recycled.
• According to several interviewees, hospital equipment and products, which are used by
Danish hospitals, are procured from many of the same suppliers. Therefore, the compo-
sition of residual waste is expected to be very similar for all of the hospitals in Denmark.
Initiatives for increased resource efﬁciency:
• All regions are assessing methods by which to increase their resource efﬁciency.
In
addition, several of the same assessments are being made simultaneously by different
regions (e.g., assessments of the residual waste composition, although they are not ex-
pected to vary greatly from hospital to hospital).
• Examples of initiatives that are being assessed or carried out include the testing of waste
trolleys to mitigate spatial limitations, creating online platforms for knowledge sharing and
educational purposes and assessing the plastic waste (both in terms of composition and
the possibility of treatment through pyrolysis).

==PAGE:56==
43
5
RESIDUAL WASTE
In the 1960’s hospitals began embracing the use of plastic equipment in their practices, substituting
materials such as metal, glass and ceramics (Johnsen, 2015). Since then, hospitals around the world
have begun using an increasing amount of single-use devices or products, and even more so with the
spread of the current global COVID-19 Pandemic (Ngo, 2020; Fishman, 2020). Today, plastics thus
reign supreme in the world of medical devices (Johnsen, 2015).
Single-use healthcare devices, are deﬁned as devices that are intended to be used on one individual
during a single procedure (EUR-Lex, 2017). They have been found to be beneﬁcial for a number of
reasons including cheaper associated upfront costs, convenience, securing sterility and patient and
worker safety (Ngo, 2020; Fishman, 2020; Johnsen, 2015). However, the introduction of single-use
equipment in the health care industry has inevitably increased the amount of hospital waste being
generated, with global waste production increasing tenfold between 1900 and 2000, and is expected
to double again by 2025 (Hoornweg et al., 2013). In addition, sterile packaging is used to secure
sterility of the single-use equipment. These are often composed of complex composites, which can
be difﬁcult to recycle, contributing to low recycling rates among hospitals worldwide (Sookne, 2019).
This section will provide an overview of the average composition of residual waste from hospitals in
Denmark, in order to gain an insight of the potential that exists to increase its recycling rate.
5.1
METHOD
In order to determine the average composition of residual waste from Danish hospitals, this report
takes into consideration ﬁndings from three different sets of ﬁeld data, in the form of waste audits.
These were conducted at four different hospitals and in three different regions. All of the waste audits
were conducted using judgemental sampling (i.e., waste samples have been selected based on the
knowledge and professional judgment of the given researcher or project leader of the study). An
overview of the three studies that were used is provided in Table 5.
Table 5: List of studies and their focus areas.

5.1.1
DATA COLLECTION
As the thesis by Absalon (2021) had not yet been ﬁnalized during the preparation of this thesis, a
semi-structured interview was carried out with the author, Lucas Absalon. However, the interview
was conducted in a slightly less formal manner when compared to those conducted in connection
with Section 4, as questions were not sent to the interviewee in advance, and the interviews were
not transcribed / summarized. The reason for this, was that the purpose of the interview was to gain
an overview of the methodologies used, and not to obtain actual results. The results were obtained
through an e-mail correspondence, where a list of questions were sent to the author. The e-mail
correspondence can be found in Appendix B.1.
In addition, as the actual internal project report regarding the waste audit carried out by Backer (2016)
had not yet been published, it was also regarded as relevant to conduct a semi-structured interview
with the former project leader, Susanne Backer. The interview was conducted in the same manner as
described in Section 4. A summary of the interview can be found in Appendix B.2.

5.2
COMPARISON OF THE THREE STUDIES
5.2.1
COMPARISON OF METHODOLOGIES
The three studies that were selected for comparison were all based on waste audits conducted at
Danish hospitals. However, they were all conducted in separate regions, assessing a different number
of departments and using varying sample sizes. This can be seen in Table 7, where these parameters
are compared. In addition, the time frame of which the waste audits spanned were also different. For
example, the waste audit conducted by Sund (2020), examined waste that was collected over the
==PAGE:58==

course of about 12 hours per department, Backer (2016) examined waste that was collected over the
course of 48 hours per department, and the waste audit conducted by Absalon (2021), on the other
hand, examined waste that was collected over three days per week, over the course of three weeks.

During the audits, bags of residual waste were opened, and different recyclable fractions were sorted
and weighed. The types of fractions that were sorted differed between the studies. The audits con-
ducted by Sund (2020) and Absalon (2021) examined most of the recyclable fractions in the residual
waste, whereas Backer (2016) only examined the clean plastic fraction. The purpose of the waste
audit conducted by Sund (2020) was to map the segregation potential of the residual waste fraction
at Hospital Sønderjylland. Thus, all materials that were regarded as either recyclable or potentially
recyclable were sorted. An overview of the material fractions that were sorted by Sund (2020) is
provided in Table 8.

On the other hand, the fractions that were sorted by Absalon (2021) were based on the common
sorting instructions provided by Region Zealand, which are placed in all wash rooms in the Region’s
hospitals, to aid the staff in sorting correctly. Thus, the items that were sorted by Absalon (2021),
were items that had been sorted incorrectly as residual waste. The fractions that were sorted were
largely similar to the ones sorted by Sund (2020), with the exception of the following dissimilarities:
• electronic equipment was sorted;
• EPS was not sorted;
• glass was disregarded due to the minimal size of the fraction;
• food waste was collected separately from biowaste, in an ’other’ fraction, also containing
products that were covered in food left overs to an inseparable degree;
• only clean plastic was sorted in the plastic fraction, i.e., products that did not contain
medicine residues, metal, organic residues, etc., thus to form a fraction that consisted
of pure plastic with little to no contamination; and
• cardboard had been collected separately from paper waste.
Backer (2016) only assessed the clean plastic waste fraction of the residual waste (i.e., plastics that
had not been exposed to patient contact), and thus, used medical plastics and all other materials
were disregarded. A total of ≈90 kg of clean plastic waste was sorted and assessed. The purpose of
the audit was to identify the types of polymers that were present in their clean plastic waste fraction,
and the amounts of each type.

The total weight measured regarding the recyclable materials, will be referred to as the hospital’s
’segregation potential’. Out of the total amount of waste assessed, Sund (2020) and Absalon (2021)
measured an average segregation potential of ≈27% and ≈37%. As can be seen, the two fractions
with the largest potential were plastic (≈12.1% of the total) and biowaste (≈10.2%). According to
Sund (2020), the biowaste fraction mainly consisted of food scraps. However, Absalon (2021) also
identiﬁed a large portion of paper towels, which he deemed to be biodegradable (≈61% of the total
fraction). These were sorted as residual waste by Sund (2020).
Electronics were not sorted by Sund (2020), although a small portion of electronics were identiﬁed.
In addition, beverage cartons were not sorted by Absalon (2021), although they too were identiﬁed in
the residual waste. It is unknown whether Absalon (2021) also identiﬁed EPS, as it was not among
his sorted fractions. However, it is likely that it could have been included in his plastic fraction.
The waste that was considered non-recyclable was regarded as the actual residual waste fraction.
According to Sund (2020) and Absalon (2021), it was mainly composed of surgical linens and gowns,
face masks, singe-use gloves, hospital diapers and intravenous (IV) bags and tubing. IV bags and
tubing often contain polyvinyl chloride (PVC), which should not be incinerated (Plastindustrien, nd).
Backer (2016) observed many different types of plastic which she separated into 17 different fractions.
The waste mainly consisted of plastic packaging waste, as equipment is typically used before it is
disposed of, and thus exposed to patient contact. Although all of the plastics that were collected in the
waste audit were clean, they were not necessarily recyclable. As mentioned in Section 3.1, different
factors have an impact on whether or not a material is recyclable. These include the environmental
impacts associated to recycling a given material (e.g., whether the impacts of recycling the material
are larger than the impacts associated to alternative treatment options), and whether it is ﬁnancially
feasible (e.g., whether the costs associated to recycling are higher than alternative treatment options,
and whether there is a demand for the recycled material) (Sørensen et al., 2019). In addition, some
plastics are more difﬁcult to recycle than others, based on their content of additives (i.e., substances
that are added to the plastic to give it the desired properties and appearance) (Dakofa, 2018).
Backer (2016) found that ≈55% of the plastics were recyclable. Of this amount, the vast majority
were ﬂexible plastics (≈88%), whereas foils only accounted for ≈3% and rigid plastic for ≈9%. The
two largest fractions observed of the total 90 kg included ’unknown plastics’ (≈39.6%) and LDPE
(≈26.6%). The unknown plastics fraction was composed of many different items. However, according
to Susanne Backer, their composition was not possible to identify visually, even by experts. A labora-
tory assessment of a random sample of the fraction, proved that it consisted mainly of laminates, that
were made of composite materials (e.g., different types of plastics or metalized plastics). Laminates
are difﬁcult to recycle as they consists of multiple layers of different materials which are demanding to
separate. According to Absalon (2021), who also conducted a polymer analysis of his sorted plastic
fraction, he too identiﬁed many different types of plastics, of which many were difﬁcult to recycle.
The LDPE fraction, on the other hand, mainly consisted of transport ﬁlm around pallets and as the
plastic layer in much of the sterile packaging - or peel pouch packaging (otherwise known as steril-
ization pouches). According to Backer (2016), peel pouches are bags which can be separated into
two pieces, and consist of either two plastic parts (alike or different), or a plastic part and a paper part
(they are typically glued together, but can also be welded). In fact, the majority of the plastic found
consisted of peel pouch packaging, which was also conﬁrmed by Sund (2020) and Absalon (2021).
The peel pouch packaging is not always easy to collect. This is because, peel pouch packaging can
be observed with both two plastic parts of the same type of plastic, of multiple plastics, and of one
==PAGE:61==
5.2
COMPARISON OF THE THREE STUDIES
48
side paper and one plastic. In addition, the packaging materials are often not labelled with a resin
identiﬁcation code. This means that the staff must ﬁrst identify the type of material that the given
peel pouch is composed of, then they must separate the individual parts from each other entirely, and
ﬁnally sort them in the appropriate container. Some of the plastic combinations identiﬁed by Backer
(2016) were not regarded as recyclable, resulting in a contamination of the given waste stream, if the
staff mistakenly misidentiﬁes a given packaging material and sorts it incorrectly. In addition, there
is often glue holding the packaging parts together. The type of glue used can differ from item to
item, which also contaminates the waste stream. The packaging is made so that it is easy for staff
to quickly rip open, and remove the content. However, it is typically only necessary to partially open
them, before the content can be removed. Thus, the vast majority of the packaging material that is
disposed of, has not been fully separated. This results in a large contamination of paper labels.
In Table 10, a list of all of the plastics assessed is presented, together with the the main items they
were found in, and their current recyclability according to Gitte from Aage Vestergaard Larsen A/S.

==PAGE:64==
5.3
THE AVERAGE COMPOSITION OF GENERAL HOSPITAL WASTE
51
surgical linen and gowns, and beverage bottles. In addition, beverage cartons can be found, along
with food packaging, food scraps, napkins, disposable kidney dishes and empty glove boxes.
5.2.3
LIMITATIONS
All of the studies were performed over a short time frame, and from a few selected departments.
This limits each waste sample size and proﬁle to the clinical activities occurring during that period.
However, waste proﬁles differ according to clinical activity, and thus, the waste audit data represents a
small sample of the total recyclable waste streams for each department. Thus, the calculated average
amount of recyclables will not necessarily present the actual rate of recyclables in all departments. For
example, some departments, such as the operating departments, typically produce a larger amount
of plastic packaging waste compared to other departments, due to the large amount of SUPs used. In
addition, the sampling time from each department can also affect both the amount of waste collected
and its proﬁle. For example, the kitchen department may not produce the same amount of food waste
from sunday to monday, as it would in the middle of the week.
Another limitation of all three studies, involves their analysis of waste volume by weight. The clean,
dry, recyclable packaging materials are lightweight in comparison to the non-recyclable fraction, which
was typically composed of disposable linen, used paper towels and diapers. In contrast to the pack-
aging materials, these all absorb moisture. Thus, their weight will multiply in comparison to the dry
packaging fractions, thus, skewing the diversion rate.
Miscommunication among the researchers, clinical staff and waste porters also contributed to some
fractions not being delivered for assessment in some of the studies. This resulted in some depart-
ments contributing with smaller waste fractions, than what would actually have been produced in the
given time frame. This could have contributed to a different composition of the given fraction.
Lastly, not all of the fractions sorted indicate the amounts that are actually recyclable. For example,
in the waste audit at Hospital Sønderjylland, materials were sorted despite having been used. Thus,
the plastic fraction from that audit contains both clean and used plastic items. Here, the waste audit
conducted at Holbæk Hospital seems to provide a slightly more clear indication of the recyclable
fractions, as the sorting process was based on the sorting guidelines provided by Region Zealand. In
addition, the waste audit conducted at AUH provides a much more clear indication of the recyclable
fraction of the plastic waste. The fact that the three sampling campaigns were done independently
presents a limitation, as they are not harmonized, and thus, sorting was done a bit differently from
audit to audit. This could affect the comparability.
5.3
THE AVERAGE COMPOSITION OF GENERAL HOSPITAL WASTE
In order to present an average composition of hospital residual waste, results from three different
waste audits have been used. The average was presented in Table 9. The waste audits were also
conducted in three different regions, so as to compare the regional difference in composition. Here,
the results from the waste audits conducted by Sund (2020) at Hospital Sønderjylland, and Absalon
(2021) at Hospital Holbæk, have been compared to ﬁnd a general composition of biowaste, ﬁbers,
metal, glass, and residual waste. The amount of beverage cartons and EPS in the general compo-
sition is based on the results provided by Sund (2020), and the amount of electronics was obtained
by Absalon (2021). Their plastic fractions have been compared to the amount of plastic measured in
the waste audit conducted by Backer (2016) at Aarhus University Hospital. In addition, the individ-
ual types of plastics found in the plastic fraction are based solely on the results provided by Backer
==PAGE:65==
5.3
THE AVERAGE COMPOSITION OF GENERAL HOSPITAL WASTE
52
(2016). Most of the material fractions have been found based on the average of the results from the
different studies. However, due to their dissimilarities, some assumptions and corrections were made.
The assumptions made for each waste fraction will be described in this section.
As can be seen, the average segregation potential in residual waste from Danish hospitals has been
estimated to ≈32%. The largest potentials were found in the biowaste and plastic waste fractions,
which were estimated to ≈15% and ≈12% of the total residual waste, respectively.
5.3.1
ASSUMPTIONS
Biowaste
In order to obtain the biowaste average potential, the average was obtained between the results
of Sund (2020) and Absalon (2021). The biowaste fraction identiﬁed by Absalon (2021) was roughly
double the size of the one identiﬁed by Sund (2020). However, this was found to be due to a difference
in sorting, where Absalon (2021) regarded paper towels as biodegradable and Sund (2020) did not.
Thus, it is likely that the potentials would have been more similar, had the materials been sorted in the
same manner. However, the precise amount of paper towels that were regarded as residual waste by
Sund (2020) is not known, and thus, the average between them has been assumed as representative
of the actual average segregation potential.
Fibers
The potentials found for paper and cardboard were quite similar between the two studies. Here, the
average between them was regarded as representative of the actual average segregation potential.
Plastics
The plastic segregation potential was found to be very similar in the results obtain by Sund (2020)
and Absalon (2021). The potential obtained by Backer (2016) was a few percent lower. However, her
potential portrays the actual recyclable potential of her plastic fraction, whereas this may not be the
case for the other two studies. Sund (2020), for example, did not perform a polymer analysis, and thus,
the actual recyclable potential is not known. Originally, Backer (2016) identiﬁed 18% clean plastics of
the residual fraction, which is slightly higher than in the two other studies. Thus, the average between
the three studies has been assumed as representative of the actual average segregation potential.
Within the recyclable plastic fraction, the results obtained by Backer (2016) were used to estimate
that ≈88% of the fraction is ﬂexible, ≈3% is foils and ≈9% is rigid plastic.
Metals
The potential found for metal by Sund (2020) was larger than the one found by Absalon (2021).
However, in both studies the fraction only contributed with a minor potential.
Thus, the average
between them was regarded as representative of the actual average segregation potential.
Glass
No glass was identiﬁed by Absalon (2021), and only a small amount was identiﬁed by Sund (2020).
Here the average of the two potentials was regarded as representative of the actual average segre-
gation potential.
Beverage cartons
No beverage cartons were sorted by Absalon (2021), although they were identiﬁed. As the speciﬁc
potential of his beverage cartons was not identiﬁed, the potential measured by Sund (2020) has been
assumed as representative of the actual average segregation potential.
==PAGE:66==
5.4
REFLECTIONS
53
EPS
Once again, EPS was not sorted by Absalon (2021), although they were identiﬁed. Thus, the potential
measured by Sund (2020) has been assumed as representative of the actual average segregation
potential.
Electronics
Because electronics were not sorted by Sund (2020), although a small amount of electronics was
identiﬁed, the potential that was identiﬁed by Absalon (2021) has been regarded as representative of
the actual average segregation potential of electronic waste.
Non recyclable waste
Finally, the average amount of residual waste found in both studies was regarded as representative of
the actual amount of non-recyclable materials found in the residual waste fraction of Danish hospitals.
5.3.2
UNCERTAINTY OF THE AVERAGE COMPOSITION
Due to the limitations mentioned in Section 5.2.3, the estimated average composition of residual
hospital waste is coupled to some degree of uncertainty. Several dissimilarities were found in the
methodologies used between the different studies, however, they largely identiﬁed similar material
contributions.
If a decreased level of uncertainty is to be obtained, of the estimated average, more studies should
be conducted, also assessing waste from other regions, where the waste audits are conducted in
the same manner. In this estimate only results from three different regions have been taken into
account. Thus, it is possible that waste audits conducted in other regions may have obtained different
results. However, based on the interviews conducted in both this section and Section 4, it seems
that the materials found in the residual waste fractions of the hospitals are largely similar, and that the
biowaste and plastic waste fractions will provide the largest segregation potential on a national scale.
5.4
REFLECTIONS
An average residual waste composition for Danish hospitals has been estimated based on three
different waste audits. The results from the audits showed very similar compositions of residual waste
from the different hospitals. Here it was gathered that the main types of recyclable fractions found
in the residual waste are biowaste and plastics. However, of the plastic waste that was considered
’recyclable’, a large share was found to be represented by peel pouch packaging which is typically
made of complex composites, that can be difﬁcult to recycle.
Although several recyclables were found, an even larger non-recyclable fraction was also recorded.
Nevertheless, none of the studies recorded the precise compositions of this fraction. Thus, it is not
possible to conclude on the exact amount of the different materials found, however, both Sund (2020)
and Absalon (2021) have described the composition of the fraction as being largely represented by
surgical linens and gowns, IV bags and tubes, latex gloves and masks. As the non-recyclable fraction
represents such a large share of the total residual waste, effort should be made to document the exact
types of products found. This is important in terms of gaining a better understanding of the possible
design-related alterations that could be made, in order to increase the amount of products that can
actually be recycled, so as to reduce the non-recyclable fraction.
As mentioned, the main types of recyclables found were biowaste and plastics, however, also many
other types of recyclable materials were observed. It is important to investigate the potentially avoided
==PAGE:67==
5.4
REFLECTIONS
54
emissions that are associated to diverting these materials from incineration to recycling, in order to
gain a better understanding of which materials should be prioritized in a sorting scheme.
5.5
KEY FINDINGS
Section 5 compares the ﬁndings from different waste audits, that assess the material composi-
tion of residual waste from four different Danish hospitals. Using the results found within these
three studies, an average composition of residual waste is proposed for Danish hospitals.
The estimated average composition:
• Non-recyclable (residual) waste (≈68%)
• Biowaste (≈15%)
• Plastic waste (≈12%) (of which ≈88% is ﬂexible, ≈3% is foils and ≈9% is rigid)
• Fibers (≈3%)
• Beverage cartons (≈1.2%)
• Electronics (≈0.3%)
• Glass (≈0.2%)
• Metal (≈0.1%)
• EPS (≈0.06%)
The average composition is coupled to some uncertainty due to the small amount of waste
audits used to compose the average. However, based on information obtained through inter-
views, it has been found likely that the materials found in the residual waste fractions of the
hospitals are largely similar. Plastics have been found to dominate both the recyclable and
non-recyclable fraction. However, even among the recyclable fraction, the plastics are con-
sidered as difﬁcult to recycle, due to the large composition of peel pouch packaging, which is
difﬁcult to both sort and recycle due to a number of reasons including:
• they are made of many different types of plastics;
• their material composition is difﬁcult to identify and they are often are not labelled with a
resin identiﬁcation code or any other guidelines for sorting;
• they often have a paper label or are glued together using different types of glue; and
• it takes time to separate them and sort them correctly.

CASE STUDY
(LCA)

==PAGE:70==

6
CASE STUDY
LCA OF THE RESIDUAL WASTE MANAGE-
MENT AT HOSPITAL SØNDERJYLLAND
INITIAL REMARKS
This section presents a comparative Life Cycle Analysis (LCA) screening model of eight
different treatment- and disposal scenarios for residual hospital waste at Hospital Sønder-
jylland in the Region of Southern Denmark.
The analysis is based on a screening model, that is built using very generic processes. Thus,
the results merely provide a general idea of the overall ’ranking’ of the scenarios, within each
of the assessed impact categories (i.e., a ranking of the level of impact from each scenario in
comparison to one another within each impact category). The impact scores should not be
regarded as the actual impacts expected from each scenario, and should not be disclosed to
the public. In order to provide more precise impact scores, the model should be built based
on the speciﬁc technologies used by the actual treatment facilities, that would treat each of the
fractions assessed. However, this was beyond the scope of this analysis.
6.1
INTRODUCTION
Hospital Sønderjylland have stated that they
annually produce approximately 1,100 tons of
waste, whereof 69% is sent to incineration with
energy recovery. The Region of Southern Den-
mark has found that 82% of this waste originates
from their hospital’s residual waste fractions. In
light of this, Hospital Sønderjylland has set itself
the goal of reducing its share of residual waste
by 10% within the next ten years.
In this re-
gard, Sund (2020) analyzed the hospitals’ resid-
ual waste composition, and investigated whether
it was possible to increase the recycling rate, see
Section 5 and Appendix B.4.1 for a description
of the study and the residual waste composition.
According to Sund (2020) a total of 226.9 kg of
residual waste was analyzed, whereby an over-
all average recyclable potential of ≈27% was
found. As described in Section 5, the potential
consisted of eight recyclable fractions, see Ta-
ble 11. The average composition, that was pro-
posed in Section 5.3 has also been displayed.
Table 11: Residual waste composition .
