In this analysis, the EPS and electronic fractions
are disregarded. The EPS is disregarded due
to its small size, and the electronics are disre-
garded as it was not assessed by Sund (2020).
==PAGE:71==
6.2
METHOD
58
6.1.1
PURPOSE OF THE CASE STUDY
The case study examines the environmental im-
pacts associated with the current management
of the average residual waste fraction from an
average Danish hospital. In addition, it exam-
ines the degree of which they can minimize the
impacts through increased sorting and recycling.
The purpose of the study is to gain a deeper
understanding of which barriers are most im-
portant to eliminate, from an environmental per-
spective. These barriers will be identiﬁed as the
ones that prevent the recycling of the materials
with the greatest minimization in impacts. Hos-
pital Sønderjylland, has been selected as a hos-
pital that is representative of an average Dan-
ish hospital, and has therefore been used for the
modelling in this case study.
Thus, a comparative LCA screening model will
be conducted, examining eight different path-
ways.
These have been labelled Scenario A,
B and Ci=1..6, with Scenario A being the refer-
ence scenario (i.e. the scenario that is currently
in place today):
• Scenario A: The total amount of residual
waste is incinerated with energy recovery
(Reference scenario).
• Scenario B: The full recyclable potential is
realized, such that all recyclable fractions,
Xi=1..6, (where i indicates each individual
recyclable fraction) are recycled, and the
non-recyclable fraction is incinerated, with
energy recovery.
• Scenario Ci: One recyclable fraction, Xi,
is recycled, and the remaining waste is in-
cinerated as residual waste with energy re-
covery, where:
– Scenario C1: i = bio waste
– Scenario C2: i = ﬁbers
– Scenario C3: i = plastics
– Scenario C4: i = metals
– Scenario C5: i = glass
– Scenario C6: i = beverage cartons
6.2
METHOD
The comparison of the different scenarios in
this case study, is based on the ISO standard-
ized LCA tool (ISO 14040-14044), which is com-
posed of four steps. These include (1) goal and
scope deﬁnition; (2) inventory analysis (LCI); (3)
impact assessment (LCIA); and (4) interpreta-
tion. The steps have been described thoroughly
in the book, ’Life Cycle Assessment: Theory and
Practice,’ by Hauschild et al. (2017).
6.2.1
GOAL AND SCOPE
The goal of this LCA is to compare eight differ-
ent waste management scenarios from Hospital
Sønderjylland, in terms of their associated envi-
ronmental impacts. The LCA will be conducted
using the LCA software, EASETECH (Clavreul
et al., 2014), which was developed at DTU En-
vironment, for assessing environmental impacts
of waste management systems.
6.2.1.1
LIMITATIONS
This case study serves as a comparison of the
different scenarios which will be described in
Section 6.2.2. Thus, it does not assess abso-
lute sustainability. This means, that the results
for any of the given scenarios will merely provide
an indication of how much smaller or larger the
impacts are in comparison to the other scenar-
ios. In addition, this case study does not assess
either social or economic sustainability.
Thus,
the results can merely be used to gain an under-
standing of the environmental impacts.
Furthermore, this study does not present a
hotspot analysis.
This means that it does not
identify the individual process contributions to
the overall impact score for every scenario.
Thus, the results from the case study will merely
present an overview of which scenario has the
biggest aggregated impact within each impact
category (i.e., the total impact of all processes
that are carried out in a given scenario in terms
of, for example, climate change).
==PAGE:72==
6.2
METHOD
59
Moreover, the data used to set up the model,
was obtained through ﬁeld work of eight differ-
ent departments at Hospital Sønderjylland. The
ﬁeld work was conducted over the course of one
week by Sund (2020). This data, thus, serves
as a representation of the average composition
of the waste generated at Hospital Sønderjylland
at any given time.
However, it is important to
take into consideration, that many different fac-
tors can affect the amount and type of waste
generated from day to day. These include fac-
tors such as the types of clinical activities being
carried out during the assessment, and the time
of day, or day of the week. For a more accurate
representation of the waste, the time frame of
the waste audit could have been extended. For
a more detailed description of the limitations re-
garding the data collected during the waste au-
dit, see Section 5.2.3.
Another limitation of this study, is that the mod-
elling has been carried out using mainly generic
processes, which were readily available in the
EASETECH software.
Several of theses pro-
cesses have not been updated with respect to
state-of-the-art technologies.
The processes
that are used are largely based on the technolo-
gies that would be used to treat the waste, how-
ever, no actual data has been obtained from the
actual recycling facilities that currently treat Hos-
pital Sønderjylland’s waste.
In fact, the oldest
data that has been used to build some of these
processes dates back to 2001, which was used
to build the glass recycling process. More accu-
rate data should be obtained regarding each of
the processes, so as to obtain more reliable re-
sults, and favorably from the actual recyclers of
the materials. The only process which was not
modelled using a generic process, was the re-
cycling of beverage cartons. This process was
modelled using data obtained from Fiskeby in
Sweden, regarding their current operations. This
data was obtained in connection with a previous
study, which was conducted by the author of this
thesis, which assessed different treatment path-
ways for beverage cartons. Elements of the re-
port, including the limitations associated with the
process are presented in Appendix C.4.
As mentioned in the initial remarks, because the
study is based on generic processes, the results
should merely be used to gain a general im-
pression of the ’ranking’ of the scenarios within
each impact category. Thus, the actual impacts
that are obtained in connection with the analy-
sis, should be regarded as very rough estimates.
Moreover, this analysis does not allow for rank-
ing of the scenarios across categories, as the
results have merely been characterized.
Lastly, due to the scale of the case study, it is
difﬁcult to identify long-term and short-term con-
sequences on the market from introducing small
changes (studied under decision context A).
6.2.1.2
FUNCTIONAL UNIT
The functional unit chosen for this study is:
“The treatment of 1 tonne of average residual
waste at Sygehus Sønderjylland.”
6.2.1.3
SYSTEM BOUNDARIES
This study assesses the life cycle environmen-
tal impacts (LCIA) associated with the cur-
rent management of residual waste at Sygehus
Sønderjylland, and compares them to the im-
pacts from seven different hypothetical manage-
ment scenarios. The time horizon of the impacts
is 100 years. The analysis is based on data from
2020, provided by Sund (2020).
The system boundaries for each scenario are
presented in Figure 17, and will be explained
in Section 6.2.2. As can be seen, the study as-
sesses the environmental impacts from “gate-to-
grave”, i.e. from the point of which the materi-
als enter the waste management phase. As this
case study presents a rough analysis, it is as-
sumed that the sorting and collection processes,
and transport of the collected waste fractions to
the recycling facilities, are the same in all sce-
narios, although this would not actually be the
case. Thus, these stages have been excluded
from the system boundaries.
==PAGE:73==
6.2
METHOD
60
Figure 17: The system boundaries for each scenario.
==PAGE:74==
6.2
METHOD
61
6.2.1.4
MODELLING APPROACH
This LCA will serve as micro-level decision sup-
port, for which the International Life Cycle Data
System (ILCD) recommends the use of attri-
butional modelling (i.e., it is assumed that the
systems assessed are isolated from the rest
of the technosphere or economy, and average
representative data is applied in all processes
(Hauschild et al., 2017)).
In addition, system
expansion is applied in the handling of multi-
functional processes. This means that all sec-
ondary co-products that arise from the treatment
of the residual waste, such as recovered en-
ergy and/or secondary raw materials, will be ac-
counted for, and treated as alternatives to pri-
mary products that have the same functionality
on the global market. The co-products are listed
in the blue boxes in Figure 17, and the substi-
tuted products are depicted in the boxes the stip-
pled outline.
6.2.2
LCI
The inventories and excel calculations can be
found in Appendix C and Appendix E, respec-
tively. The data utilized in this report has mainly
been provided by Sund (2020) and was de-
scribed in Section 5, where a new average esti-
mate of hospital residual waste composition was
also presented. Most of the processes that were
used to model the different scenarios were found
in EASETECH. However, the processes related
to the recycling of beverage cartons were pro-
duced in a previous report, as previously men-
tioned. Thus, for a more in-depth description of
their set-up, see the report in Appendix C.4. In
addition, the marginal electricity used in all pro-
cesses was adapted so as to better represent
Danish current average data. In this regard, a
2014, medium voltage electricity process was
obtained from the ecoinvent database 3.5.
In the following sections, the model set-up of
the different scenarios will be described, includ-
ing the different assumptions that were made.
For all scenarios, it is assumed that the initial
waste composition is composed of the fractions
presented in Table 11, under Sund (2020). In
order to model this composition, the following
EASETECH material fractions were used:
• ’vegetable food waste’ as bio waste;
• ’dirty cardboard’ as ﬁbers;
• ’soft plastic’ (91%) and ’hard plastic’ (9%)
as plastic;
• ’clear glass’ as glass;
• ’beverage cans (aluminium)’ as metal
• ’juice cartons (carton/plastic/aluminium)’
as beverage cartons; and
• ’diapers, sanitary towels, tampons’ (70%),
’disposable
sanitary
products
(cloths,
gloves)’ (10%), and ’non-recyclable plas-
tic’ (20%) were used to model the non-
recyclable residual waste.
No material fractions were adjusted with the ex-
ception of soft plastic, where the water content
was reduced from 14.11% to 5%.
6.2.2.1
SCENARIO A
Scenario A represents the current management
of residual waste at Hospital Sønderjylland.
Here, the total amount of residual waste pro-
duced is sent to a waste to energy plant that
produces heat and electricity. The EASETECH
process is titled, ’Waste to energy plant, generic,
DK, 2012 [with substitution]’ and was last up-
dated in 2016.
In the process, the ﬂy ash
(≈0.6%) is sent to Norway for neutralization, us-
ing the process titled, ’Utilization for neutraliza-
tion of waste acid, Norway, 2006’. The remain-
ing bottom ash fraction is utilized as aggregate
in road construction, using the process titled,
’MSWI BA aggregate in Road 100% leaching
emissions to soil’.
6.2.2.2
SCENARIO B
In Scenario B, the total recycling potential is re-
alized and all recyclable fractions are sorted at
the source and sent to their respective recycling
facilities. The non-recyclable materials are sent
==PAGE:75==
6.2
METHOD
62
to a waste to energy plant that produces heat
and electricity, and modelled as in Scenario A.
It is assumed that bio waste is used to make
biogas through anaerobic digestion.
This as-
sumption has been made based on the infor-
mation provided on Marius Pedersen A/S’s web-
site regarding treatment of food waste. Marius
Pedersen A/S is a Danish company who spe-
cializes in collection, transport, sorting, trading
and disposal of waste as well as recyclable ma-
terials (Marius Pedersen A/S, 2014).
In addi-
tion, Marius Pedersen A/S is the primary pur-
chaser of waste from hospitals in the Region of
Southern Denmark.
The EASETECH process
used to model this treatment is titled ’Anaer-
obic digestion (municipal organic solid waste),
Thermophilic, 2007 [with substitution]’ and was
last updated in 2016.
The process produces
biogas for energy production and digestate for
fertilizer substitution at farms, at a hypothetical
one stage ”wet” thermophilic anaerobic diges-
tion plant. The process assumes a 2% leakage
of methane into the atmosphere.
The ﬁber fraction is assumed to be treated at
Fiskeby’s paper mill in Norrk¨oping, Sweden. The
EASETECH process used to model this treat-
ment is titled ’Paper (Cardboard and mixed pa-
per) to cardboard, Sweden, 2006 [with substitu-
tion],’ and was last updated in 2016. The paper
mill treats paper and cardboard and produces
multiboard, with a total substitution ratio of 81%
(i.e., the ratio with which the waste ﬁbers replace
new cardboard, also taking into consideration
the technological efﬁciency). All of the residues
are burnt in an incineration plant, and the energy
recovery is included in the combustion process.
The plastic fraction is assumed to be recycled
at a HDPE facility based upon data from an
American facility.
However, according to the
EASETECH process documentation, the elec-
tricity has been adapted and modelled as Danish
marginal coal electricity, and the ancillary ma-
terials are based on European datasets.
The
EASETECH process is titled ’HDPE recycling,
USA 2010 [with substitution]’ and was last up-
dated in 2016. The facility receives ridig HDPE
and produces recycled HDPE pellets. Accord-
ing to Backer (2016), ﬂexible plastic in the form
of LDPE was the most abundant type of plas-
tic that was recovered, among the clean plastic
waste that was assessed during their waste au-
dit at AUH. According to her ﬁndings, rigid HDPE
only contributed with a minor fraction, see Ap-
pendix B.4.3. Thus, this process is coupled to a
great deal of uncertainty. The process uses data
from six different HDPE reclaimers and assumes
the following process sequence; debaling, grind-
ing, washing, drying, extruding and pelletizing.
A substitution ratio of 72% is assumed of virgin
HDPE. However, this depends on many differ-
ent factors and can vary greatly as described in
Section 7. Finally, all residues in the process
are sent to a generic European landﬁll.
Metal is assumed to be recycled at an aluminium
recycling plant in ¨Almhult, Sweden. The metal
fraction is assumed to be composed of only
aluminium cans, as this was the primary type
of metal found in the waste assessed during
the audit. The EASETECH process used is ti-
tled, ’Aluminium scrap to new aluminium sheets
(remelting), Sweden, 2015 [with substitution]’,
and was last updated in 2016. It assumes that
the waste is collected, sorted and shredded in
the recycling plant, and that the recyclable metal
is melted, alloyed and reprocessed to new alu-
minum sheets. The substitution ratio is assumed
to be 87%. Residues are assumed to be sent to
a generic European landﬁll.
Glass is assumed to be sorted prior to recy-
cling. The glass is sorted using the EASETECH
process, ’Glass Sorting and treatment Europe,
based on Rigamonti’, with a sorting efﬁciency of
94%. The process is based on data that was
obtained prior to 2007. It is assumed that the
sorting process produces 6% residues which are
sent to a generic European landﬁll. After sorting,
the glass is recycled using the EASETECH pro-
cess, ’Glass recycling, Europe based on Riga-
monti, updated’, at a glass manufacturing plant,
where it is used in the production of new glass
containers together with virgin raw materials.
==PAGE:76==
6.3
RESULTS AND DISCUSSION
63
Beverage cartons are assumed to be treated at
Fiskeby’s paper mill, where the carton is sep-
arated from the aluminium and plastic.
Here
it is assumed that 87% of the incoming bever-
age carton fraction consists of carton, and that
13% consists of aluminium and plastic. The car-
ton is then recycled in the paper mill, and used
to make multiboard, which according to Fiskeby
(nd), is a type of packaging board manufactured
from 100% recovered ﬁbres. This is done using
the process titled, ’Paper to cardboard, Sweden,
2006,’ which has a substitution rate of 82.8%. All
of the residues from the paper mill are burnt in an
incineration plant with energy recovery. The alu-
minium and plastic fraction, on the other hand, is
sent to a cement production facility. This process
is found through the ecoinvent database, and is
titled, ’Cement, portland ﬂy ash cement 6-20%,
cement production, ﬂy ash 6-20%, RoW’, which
assumes a 100% substitution of new cement.
6.2.2.3
SCENARIO Ci=1..6
In Scenario Ci=1..6, one recyclable fraction, Xi,
is interchangeably sent for treatment at its re-
spective recycling facility, as described in Sce-
nario B. The rest of the recyclable fractions
are incinerated together with the non-recyclable
waste, as described in Scenario A. No new pro-
cesses were modelled for this scenario.
• Scenario Ci=1: bio waste is recycled and
all other waste is incinerated.
• Scenario Ci=2: ﬁbers are recycled and all
other waste is incinerated.
• Scenario Ci=3: plastics are recycled and
all other waste is incinerated.
• Scenario Ci=4: metals are recycled and
all other waste is incinerated.
• Scenario Ci=5: glass is recycled and all
other waste is incinerated.
• Scenario Ci=6: beverage cartons are re-
cycled and all other waste is incinerated.
6.2.3
IMPACT ASSESSMENT
The impact categories selected for the impact
assessment are based on the ILCD recom-
mended impact factors by the European Com-
mission (2010), see Table 12. A midpoint anal-
ysis is performed to assess these impacts using
the ILCD recommended LCIA methods, which
have also been described in the table.
This
study does not make use of either normalization
or weighting to support the results interpretation.
6.2.4
INTERPRETATION
The results of the analysis will be presented and
discussed in Section 6.3.
A validation of the rankings will also be carried
out in Section 6.3.2, where the model will be
run using the average residual waste composi-
tion data from Table 11, instead of the data ob-
tained by Sund (2020).
This is done with the
purpose of investigating the degree of which the
ranking of the scenarios change, when using
the data that was actually obtained at Hospital
Sønderjylland and the estimates that were made
regarding the average composition from Danish
hospitals in general.
If the overall rankings of the scenarios, within
each impact category, do not change when us-
ing the different compositions, it will indicate that
the model is not sensitive to small differences in
the composition of hospital residual waste.
6.3
RESULTS AND DISCUSSION
6.3.1
CHARACTERIZED MIDPOINT ASSESS-
MENT
The characterized results for the midpoint impact
assessment are presented in Table 13. As previ-
ously mentioned, because the results are char-
acterized, the impacts must only be compared
between the scenarios within each impact cate-
gory, and not across categories. This is because
the results have neither been normalized, nor
weighted, and are presented in different units,
as seen in Table 12.
==PAGE:77==
6.3
RESULTS AND DISCUSSION
64
Table 12: The characterization (midpoint) references utilized in this report. Both of the impact categories under
“Depletion of abiotic resources” follows the ILCD recommended characterization factors.
As can be seen in Table 13, on the follow-
ing page, the results have been presented us-
ing a heatmap, which displays the largest im-
pacts within each impact category in red, and
the smallest impacts in green. As can be seen,
Scenario B is associated with the smallest im-
pacts in the majority of the impact categories.
This is with the exception of the Eutrophica-
tion (aquatic) indicator, where it has the high-
est impact among all of the Scenarios, and in
Ozone depletion, Human toxicity (non-cancer ef-
fects) and Ionising radiation, where it is associ-
ated with the second lowest impacts. The main
process contributing to the large impact of the
Eutrophication (aquatic) indicator is the HDPE
recycling process. However, on the contrary, it
seems that it is especially the recycling of plas-
tic that is responsible for the lowest impacts in
many of the categories, along with the recycling
of metals.
Another scenario that is associated with lower
impacts is Scenario C3.
This was to be ex-
pected, as the main process contributing to the
lower impacts in Scenario B, was the diversion of
the plastic fraction from incineration to recycling.
It has the second lowest impact in six impact cat-
egories, namely Climate change, Human toxicity
(cancer effects), Particulate matter, Photochem-
ical ozone formation and Terrestrial acidiﬁcation.
In addition, it has the lowest impact in its Deple-
tion of abiotic resources (fossil) indicator. This
is to be expected as it prevents the production
of virgin plastics, thereby reducing the amount of
fossil fuels being extracted. On the other hand, it
also scores the highest impacts in two of the sce-
narios, namely Ozone depletion and Human tox-
icity (non-cancer effects). The scenario scores
high in Ozone depletion due to emissions from
landﬁlling the residues.
As with Scenario C3, Scenario C4 is assiociated
with quite a few of the lowest impacts.
Once
again, this was to be expected due to the ob-
served impacts of metal recycling in Scenario B.
However, in contrast to Scenario C3, Scenario

C4 scored the lowest impacts in Ozone depletion
and Human toxicity (non-cancer effects). In ad-
dition, it also scored the lowest impacts in Ionis-
ing radiation, and Depletion of abiotic resources
(elements). Its low impact in Depletion of abi-
otic resources was also to be expected as the
process of recycling aluminium prevents the ex-
traction of primary aluminium, which is an abiotic
resource. This scenario does not score the high-
est impact in any of the impact categories.
Scenario C2 scored the lowest impact in Eu-
trophication (aquatic), in contrast to Scenario B.
It seems that the processes that cause Scenario
B to perform poorly on this indicator are the pro-
cesses relating to plastic recycling and the re-
cycling of beverage cartons. This is mainly due
to phosphorus emissions to soil and water from
the recycling of plastic and beverage cartons, re-
spectively.
It is surprising that the processes,
which relate to the recycling of ﬁbers and of the
ﬁbers in the beverage cartons, perform so differ-
ently on this indicator (which is seen for scenar-
ios C2 and C6, respectively), as the main pro-
cess contributing to the phosphorus emissions
in C6 is the manufacturing of pulp. Both scenar-
ios provide a manufacturing of pulp. However,
Scenario C2 is based on a generic EASETECH
process whereas Scenario C6 is based on a pro-
cess built using recent information provided from
Fiskeby.
Thus, it seems that the process ex-
changes from these two processes should be
investigated further. However, as they both are
associated with minor impacts compared to the
other fractions, this study will not make further
attempts to investigate these processes.

Figure 18: The main process contributions for scenarios A, B and Ci=1..6 for the Climate change impact category.
Scenarios: A, all waste is incinerated; B, all material fractions are recycled and non recyclables are incinerated;
C1, bio wastes are recycled and the rest is incinerated; C2, ﬁbers are recycled and the rest is incinerated; C3,
plastics are recycled and the rest is incinerated; C4, metals are recycled and the rest is incinerated; C5, glass is
recycled and the rest is incinerated; and C6, beverage cartons are recycled and the rest is incinerated.
Note: The ’other processes’ include utilization for neutralization of waste acid, MSW incineration bottom ash
(MSWI-BA) aggregate in road construction, anaerobic digestion, ﬁbers to cardboard, glass recycling, cement
production, manufacture of pulp and manufacture of cartonboard/multiboard.
The reference scenario, Scenario A, scores the
highest impact in the largest number of impact
categories. It scores the highest impact in four
categories, namely in Photochemical ozone for-
mation, Terrestrial acidiﬁcation and Eutrophica-
tion (both terrestrial and marine). It also scores
one of the highest impacts in several other cate-
gories, and is not responsible for the lowest im-
pacts in many of the categories. Scenarios C1,
C5 and C6 are also categories that do not score
the lowest impacts in any of the categories.
Because many of the climate strategies that
have been adopted, both at the national and re-
gional level, focus especially on lowering emis-
sions of carbon dioxide (CO2), the individual pro-
cess contributions to the Climate change indica-
tor will be investigated further.
In Figure 18,
the main process contributions to the Climate
change impact category have been illustrated for
all of the eight scenarios.
As can be seen, the main processes contributing
to this impact category are the waste to energy
process, the remelting of aluminium and HDPE
recycling. Smaller contributions have been ag-
gregated to form the ’other processes’, which is
composed of a total of eight different processes,
see Appendix C.2. However, as can be gath-
ered from Figure 18, the contributions from the
other processes are minor in comparison.
The main process that contributes to climate
change in the majority of the scenarios is the
incineration of the waste, which emits >500kg
CO2-equivalents (eq) per tonne of waste incin-
erated for scenarios A, C1, C2, C4, C5 and C6,
mainly due to the plastic content.
This is be-
cause plastic is the material fraction that emits
the largest amount of CO2 when incinerated
among all of the fractions, as it is made of fossil
fuels. In addition, the recycling of plastic con-
tributes to the largest decrease in CO2-eq emis-
sions, out of all of the recycling processes. This
is due to the fact that it is the recyclable fraction
that is most common among all of the recyclable
materials. The emissions that are coupled to the
recycling process, are lower than emissions cou-
pled to the extraction and manufacturing of virgin
plastic, and thus, the substitution of virgin plastic
produces a ’negative impact’.

depicting the highest.
6.3.2
RESULT VALIDATION
The heat map from Table 13 indicates that Sce-
nario B is the most favorable process in terms
of the Climate change impact category, followed
by Scenario C3. However, with the plastic frac-
tion contributing to such great variations in im-
pacts regarding climate change, it is interesting
to observe the degree of which a different resid-
ual waste composition will alter the impact re-
sults. For this reason, the model has been run
using the average composition of residual hos-
pital waste, that was presented in Section 5.3,
see Table 11. Thus to observe whether a slight
change in waste composition, which is what can
be expected from hospital to hospital, will have
an impact on the ranking of the scenarios.
In Appendix C.3, the percentage change in the
results, which was observed when substituting
the data from Sund (2020) with the average, has
been illustrated. As this case study mainly fo-
cuses on the Climate change impact category,
it was not regarded as relevant to include the
results from all of the impact categories in the
actual report. Thus, in Table 14 a comparison
of the results for the Climate change impact cat-
egory, between the two different data sources,
has been displayed.
As can be seen, a decrease in the amount of
plastic inputted into the model (the average data
contains ≈1% less plastic in its total waste com-
position compared to the data obtained from
Sund (2020)), contributes to a decrease in the
amount of CO2-eq that is emitted for Scenario A,
where the plastic is incinerated together with all
of the other waste. However, it increases in sce-
narios B and C3, where the plastic is recycled.
Nevertheless, it is important to take into consid-
eration that the environmental impacts that are
associated with the production (or avoided pro-
duction) of these materials have not been in-
cluded in the model set-up. For example, if the
impacts associated to the avoided production of
plastic were included, it is likely that Scenarios B
and C3 also would face lower emissions.
Although some of the impact results increase,
and others decrease when running the model on
the different waste compositions, no difference
was observed in the ranking of the most and
least favorable scenarios for the Climate change
impact category. This means, that although the
emissions of CO2-eq increase by 16% and 19%
for scenarios B and C3, respectively, recycling
all of the recyclable fractions (i.e., Scenario B) is
still the most favorable scenario in terms of the
Climate change impact category, followed by the
recycling of plastic where the remaining waste is
incinerated (i.e., Scenario C3). However, a slight
difference is seen in the ranking of Scenarios A,
C1, C2 and C4. However, once again it is likely
that that this would differ had the avoided pro-
duction emissions been included.
==PAGE:81==
6.3
RESULTS AND DISCUSSION
68
6.3.3
CONCLUSION
Finally, the major conclusions of the case study
are presented. In addition, the main limitations
and recommendations are also outlined to facili-
tate future iterations.
6.3.3.1
CONCLUSIONS
• Scenario B (where all material fractions
are recycled) has the lowest impact in the
greatest amount of impact categories (in-
cluding the Climate change impact cat-
egory, which is measured in kg CO2-eq
emissions), followed by Scenarios C3 and
C4 (where plastic and metal are recycled
respectively, and the remaining fractions
are incinerated). The main processes that
contribute to the avoided emissions are the
recycling of plastic and metals.
• Scenario A (where all fractions are incin-
erated), C5 and C6 (where glass and bev-
erage cartons are recycled respectively,
and the remaining fractions are inciner-
ated) have the largest impact in the great-
est amount of impact categories (includ-
ing the Climate change impact category).
Scenario C5 emits the greatest amount of
CO2 followed by Scenario C6.
• When the amount of incoming waste con-
tains a slightly lower plastic and metal frac-
tion, this contributes to an increase of CO2
emissions in the scenarios where these
fractions are recycled. However, the model
does not take into account the avoided
emissions coupled to the avoided produc-
tion of these materials. Had the produc-
tion emissions been taken into account, it
is likely that these Scenarios also would
face a reduction in emissions.
• Only a slight difference in the ranking of
the Scenarios was observed for the Cli-
mate change category, when running the
model using two different waste composi-
tions.
Thus, it is likely that the average
composition can be used as a good proxy
for residual waste from Danish hospitals.
6.3.3.2
LIMITATIONS
• The model is built using mainly very gen-
eric processes, and does not include the
emissions associated with the production
of the different material fractions found in
the waste stream. Thus, the results should
not be disclosed to the public, and the
impacts obtained should not be regarded
as the actual impacts from the actual re-
cycling processes of the recyclables. In-
stead, they should merely be regarded as
very rough estimates.
• The generic process used to model the re-
cycling of plastic is based on data from
a facility that recycles rigid HDPE. How-
ever, the plastic fraction of hospital resid-
ual waste is assumed to be composed of
merely 9% rigid plastic of which only a mi-
nor fraction is HDPE. The greater majority
of plastic is assumed to be ﬂexible plastic
composed of LDPE.
6.3.3.3
RECOMMENDATIONS
• Each of the scenarios should be adapted,
such that the processes better represent
the likely treatment methods used by Hos-
pital Sønderjylland.
• As plastic is the largest fraction, effort
should especially be made to obtain data
regarding the processes relating to the re-
cycling of plastic materials.
• Production emissions should also be in-
cluded in the model, so as to observe the
level of avoided emissions.
• Scenarios C2 and C6 should be closely
assessed in terms of their recycling pro-
cesses and degree of substitution, as they
differ greatly in their impacts relating to
pulping in cardboard manufacturing.
• Once more data has been obtained re-
garding the non-recyclable plastics, and
the emissions coupled to treating them
with pyrolysis, it could be interesting to in-
clude this treatment process in the model.
==PAGE:82==
6.3
RESULTS AND DISCUSSION
69
6.4
KEY FINDINGS
Section 6 presents a case study that investigates the environmental impacts assoctiated with
eight different treatment pathways for residual waste from Hospital Sønderjylland. These sce-
narios include
1. a reference scenario, where all residual waste is incinerated with energy recovery;
2. a scenario where all recyclables are recycled, with co-product substitution, and only the
non-recyclable waste is incinerated, with energy recovery; and
3. a group of six scenarios where one of the recyclable fractions is recycled in each sce-
nario, and the total remaining amount of waste is incinerated, with energy recovery.
The scenario where the entire segregation potential was realized was found to produce the
least amount of environmental impacts in the greatest amount of impact categories. This was
also the case for the climate change impact category, where this scenario emitted the least
amount of CO2-eq emissions. This was mainly due to the recycling processes of plastic and
metal. Which thus contributed to the Scenarios where these fractions were recycled to produce
the second and third smallest amount of CO2-eq emissions.
When testing whether slight alterations to the composition of the residual waste would impact
the ranking of the scenarios in terms of their CO2-eq emissions, almost no impacts were ob-
served. This means that although the composition of residual waste may differ slightly from
hospital to hospital, it should not greatly impact the overall ranking of the scenarios in terms
of the Climate change impact category. However, this model does not take into consideration
the emissions associated with the production (or avoided production) of the material fractions
in the incoming waste stream, which may have an impact on the results.
6.5
OPSUMMERING
Afsnit 6 præsenterer et case study, der undersøger miljøp˚avirkningerne associeret med otte
forskellige behandlingsscenarier for restaffald fra Sygehus Sønderjylland.
Disse scenarier
inkluderer
1. et referencescenarie, hvor alt restaffald forbrændes med energigenvinding;
2. et scenarie, hvor alle genanvendelige fraktioner genanvendes med produktsubstitution,
og kun det ikke-genanvendelige affald forbrændes med energiudnyttelse; og
3. en gruppe p˚a seks scenarier, hvor ´en af de genanvendelige fraktioner sendes til genan-
vendelse i hvert scenarie, og den samlede resterende mængde affald forbrændes med
energigenvinding.
Scenariet, hvor hele det genanvendelige potentiale blev realiseret, viste sig at producere den
mindste mængde miljøp˚avirkninger i den største mængde p˚avirkningskategorier. Dette var og-
Fortsættes p˚a næste side.
==PAGE:83==
6.3
RESULTS AND DISCUSSION
70
s˚a tilfældet i kategorien Klimaændringer, hvor scenariet med genanvendelsen af det totale
genanvendelige potentiale udsendte den mindste mængde CO2-ækvivalente emissioner. Dette
skyldtes hovedsageligt genanvendelsesprocessen af hhv. plast og metal. Dette bidrog s˚aledes

Ved testning af, om mindre ændringer i sammensætningen af restaffaldet ville p˚avirke rangord-

==PAGE:84==
BARRIERS
7
==PAGE:85==
72
7
BARRIERS
In a circular economy, resources are managed efﬁciently, and kept in the economy loop for as long
as possible. Therefore, the amount of materials that end up as waste should be kept to a minimum.
One method of preventing waste, is to increase the amount of resources that are recycled. However,
what seems to be a feasible approach in theory, may not always prove to be easily achievable in
practice. This study has found that this can be due to a number of reasons, such as impracticality,
lack of regulatory or organizational support or insufﬁcient technology.
Although a large potential
of recyclables were identiﬁed in residual waste from Danish hospitals, several barriers have been
identiﬁed that prevent them from realizing it. This section will present the barriers that have been
found to currently prevent Danish hospitals from increasing the recycling rate of their residual waste,
and assess their perceived degree of prevalence.
7.1
METHOD
As explained in Section 2, the barriers were identiﬁed using an iterative approach, where three pri-
mary research areas were investigated. Once the barriers had been deﬁned, they were assessed
and some barriers were removed to avoid double counting. Thereafter, relevant interviewees were
identiﬁed, with the purpose of further reviewing the list of barriers, see Table 15.
Table 15: List of contact persons and their profession.
Interviewee(s)
Profession
Line Geest Jakobsen
Consultant in the department for Society, Economics and Environ-
ment, COWI A/S
Trine Lund Neidel
Consultant in the department for Society, Economics and Environ-
ment, COWI A/S
Thomas Dejbjerg Pedersen
Environmental Coordinator at Aarhus University Hospital (AUH),
Central Denmark Region
Anne Riis
Project leader at the Center for Sustainable Hospitals, Centra Den-
mark Region
The interviews were conducted with different speciﬁc intentions, and thus, using different approaches.
Initially, two consultants from COWI A/S, Trine Lund Neidel and Line Geest Jakobsen, were identiﬁed
as relevant interviewees, due to their experience with projects relating to hospital waste, including the
one by Backer (2016). This interview was conducted in an unstructured manner, as the main purpose
of the interview was to learn from their experience from previous projects and check for any barriers
that may have been overlooked. Thus, no speciﬁc questions were prepared in advance, to allow for
the interviewees to guide the dialogue, and the interview was not transcribed or summarized.
Thereafter, the Environmental Coordinator from AUH, Thomas Dejbjerg Pedersen, was identiﬁed as
a relevant interviewee due to both his current position, and his previous experience working in a
waste to energy company. The purpose of this interview was to obtain his opinion of each of the
deﬁned barriers. Thus, this interview was conducted in a semi-structured manner, following the same
seven step model as previously described in Section 4. A summary of the interview can be found
==PAGE:86==
7.2
DEFINED BARRIERS
73
in Appendix D.1. During the interview, each of the barriers were discussed in depth, and Thomas
provided descriptions of his experiences (or lack of) with each barrier.
Once the list of barriers had been ﬁnalized, a survey was prepared, with the purpose of assessing the
degree of prevalence of which each barrier was regarded by representatives from each of the regions,
see Appendix D.2. In connection with this, a total of nine participants (at least one participant from
each region) were identiﬁed to participate in the survey, whom will remain anonymous. In the survey,
each of the barriers were listed, and a short description was provided of each. The participants were
asked to rank each barrier from 1 to 3, where
1:
indicates that the participant does not perceive the
given barrier as prevalent in the region;
2:
indicates that the participant perceives the barrier
as somewhat prevalent in the region; and
3:
indicates that the participant perceives the given
barrier as very prevalent in the region.
In order to assess whether or not a perceptional variation could be observed among participants from
the same region, four participants were selected to participate in the survey from one of the regions.
Prior to the distribution of the surveys, the Project Leader at the Center for Sustainable Hospitals
in the Central Region, Anne Riis was contacted. Anne is currently working on the Regional Waste
Strategy, and thus, has great insight into both the current waste management practices in place, and
the barriers faced by the region. Thus, a semi-structured interview was carried out with Anne, also
following the same seven step model as described in Section 4, where she was asked about each
of the barriers. The main purpose of this interview was to test the survey questions, and to observe
whether or not any changes were necessary. Thus, it was not regarded as necessary to provide a
summary of the interview.
7.2
DEFINED BARRIERS
This study has identiﬁed 13 different barriers that prevent Danish hospitals from increasing the recy-
cling rate of their residual waste streams, see Table 16. These barriers have been divided among
four different categories:
Social:
Refers to factors relating to community participation, motivation,
knowledge, cooperation, attitudes, habits, culture, etc.
Organizational:
Refers to factors relating to organizational structures, coordina-
tion, communication channels, policies, strategies, funding, dis-
tribution of resources, etc.
Regulatory:
Refers to factors relating to politics, laws and regulations, targets
and agreements, etc.
Technical:
Refers to factors relating to waste equipment and structures,
available technologies, product design, etc.
However, some of the barriers have been assigned to more than one category. In the following section,
each of the barriers will be described.
==PAGE:87==
7.2
DEFINED BARRIERS
74

As can be seen, this study has identiﬁed six technical and organizational barriers, ﬁve regulatory bar-

Figure 19: The distribution of results of the barrier survey, assessing the perceived prevalence of each barrier.
Barriers: The barriers can be seen in Table 16 above.
==PAGE:88==
7.2
DEFINED BARRIERS
75
7.2.1
BARRIER DESCRIPTIONS
1. Lack of EU and national targets regarding production and procurement
Median level of perceived prevalence: 2 (somewhat prevalent)
The ﬁrst barrier that has been identiﬁed, is the lack of EU and national targets regarding production
and procurement, and has been categorized as a regulatory barrier. As it was discussed in Section
3, several different initiatives and targets have been put forth, both at the EU and national level, to
both monitor and increase sorting. An example of this can be seen in the targets set forth regard-
ing increased recycling of both municipal waste, and different packaging materials. Another example
includes the launch of the Common Pictogram System and the required collection of ten different
recyclable fractions by both the public and private sector. However, not many targets have been put
forth in terms of upstream activities, such as, production or procurement. This places a large respon-
sibility on the producer of waste, to increase their sorting efﬁciencies and recycling rates, which may
not always be possible if their procured products are not easily recyclable. However, with Denmark
adopting EPR for packaging in 2024, this is likely to have an effect on the composition and labelling
of packaging materials.
Nevertheless, as it was found in Section 5, hospitals make use of a vast amount of SUPs, and much
of their equipment is made of plastics and polymers such as PVC, the majority of which are discarded
as residual waste. With the lack of targets and regulatory requirements set forth regarding production
of hospital products and equipment, it is difﬁcult for hospitals to procure more sustainably. As previ-
ously mentioned, this has caused for the Danish Regions to collaborate with other Nordic countries,
on putting together a common list of Nordic requirements for sustainably produced products. How-
ever, this once again places the majority of the responsibility on the purchaser of the products/waste
producer, who often does not have the power to inﬂuence large multinational corporations. It is likely
that this has impacted the level of perceived prevalence of the barrier. As can be seen in Figure 19,
this barrier was assessed mainly as somewhat prevalent.
2. Lack of restrictions on amount and type of polymers contained in SUPs
Median level of perceived prevalence: 3 (very prevalent)
The next barrier which was identiﬁed was the lack of restrictions on the amount and type of polymers
contained in medical SUPs, which also was categorized as a regulatory barrier. Plastic waste has
been found to be the largest recyclable material fraction evident within the residual waste stream
of Danish hospitals, see Section 5. In addition, it has also been documented that a vast amount of
plastics are found in the non-recyclable fraction of the stream. The LCA from Section 6 concluded that
diverting the plastic waste from incineration to recycling, provided the largest avoided environmental
impacts, compared to any of the other recyclable material fractions. However, the model was built
upon the assumption that all of the plastic waste, which was recycled, was of a homogenous rigid
HDPE fraction. As was found in Section 5, this is by far the case. The waste audit conducted by
Backer (2016) documented over 17 different fractions just within the clean plastic waste fraction. This
makes it difﬁcult for hospitals to set up sorting systems, due to the many different types of plastics. It
also makes it difﬁcult for the hospital staff to distinguish between the different types of plastics.
However, currently no EU or national initiative is being taken, to limit the amount of polymers which
are used in medical appliances. In fact, the EU’s directive on SUPs explicitly states that several of the
SUPs which will be subject to a Europe-wide ban do not include those used for medical purposes.
This barrier was regarded as very prevalent by the vast majority of the participants.
==PAGE:89==
7.2
DEFINED BARRIERS
76
3. Lack of local recycling facilities
Median level of perceived prevalence: 2 (somewhat prevalent)
The ﬁrst technical barrier that was identiﬁed was the lack of local recycling facilities. Several intervie-
wees mentioned their desire to initiate purchasing agreements with local recycling facilities. The main
reason for this was based on the lack of traceability in terms of tracking their exported waste fractions.
Once waste has been delivered to a treatment facility, it is difﬁcult to secure that it is not exported
once again, or incinerated.
There are a large number of international recycling companies which recycle different types of mate-
rials very efﬁciently, and thereby at a good price. However, as it was mentioned in Section 4, several
interviewees have explained that there currently are no equally efﬁcient opportunities for all types of
waste materials within Denmark’s borders. They explained that this can make it difﬁcult for the regions
to enter into sustainable purchasing agreements. In addition, one interviewee explained that the low
costs of incinerating waste in Denmark, prevent these international companies from setting up their
services in Denmark. As can be seen in Figure 19, the majority of the participants perceived this as
a somewhat prevalent barrier.
4. Insufﬁcient quantities of recyclable fractions due to a fragmented market
Median level of perceived prevalence: 2 (somewhat prevalent)
As it was pointed out in Section 4, most of the hospitals in Denmark are largely responsible for their
own waste management system, and making their own purchasing agreements. For this reason,
there are several recyclable fractions that occur in small quantities, as opposed to if the regions were
to merge their fractions, and engage in a more regional purchasing agreement. This was identiﬁed as
the ﬁrst organizational barrier, as it seems that a higher level of communication between hospitals and
regions, could eliminate this barrier. According to some of the interviewees, they had witnessed large
ﬂuctuations in prices among hospitals within the same region, even though they had agreements with
the same waste company. Some of the survey participants explained that the regions sort in many
different fractions, however, much of the waste produced within the actual hospital departments are
disposed of as residual waste, which also contributes to the smaller amounts of sorted recyclable
fractions. This potential was also conﬁrmed in Section 5, where it was estimated that the residual
waste fraction on average is composed of ≈32% recyclable materials.
Small fractions of recyclables can also pose a threat in relation to preventing purchasing agreements
in the ﬁrst place. This is because, the waste recipients may not regard treating the material fractions
as a proﬁtable business, if the amounts of the fractions being produced are not stable (or if they are
not produced in sufﬁcient amounts). This will prevent them from offering the service. This was not
regarded as a barrier by just under half of the participants. However, the remainder of the participants
regarded it as both somewhat and very prevalent.
5. Lack of motivation among employees
Median level of perceived prevalence: 1 (not prevalent)
The ﬁrst social barrier that was identiﬁed, was the lack of motivation among some of the employees.
People are different and some people are more passionate about circular economy and waste sorting
than others. As described by Christensen (2011), there can be various reasons why employees do not
feel motivated to sort waste. For example, Sund (2020) reported that there was a large variation in the
amount of initiative that had been taken among the inspected departments at Hospital Sønderjylland.
==PAGE:90==
7.2
DEFINED BARRIERS
77
However, according to the survey results, most of the participants did not regard this as a barrier.
Many communicated a large motivation among their employees or colleagues, and a large frustration
over not being able to sort all of the waste that they would at their own home for example. Some
reported that it was perhaps more a lack of knowledge among some employees, rather than a lack
of motivation. One participant explained that some employees feel frustrated over the large amount
of plastic, that must be disposed of as residual waste, when only smaller fractions can be sorted for
recycling. They explained that this can make employees question whether their large effort is worth
the seemingly small impact that they feel they are making. Nevertheless, some participants perceived
this barrier to be very prevalent. Thus, it seems that this barrier is not completely irrelevant.
6. Difﬁcult to discern how to sort different products
Median level of perceived prevalence: 3 (very prevalent)
The next barrier that was observed was the difﬁculty faced when sorting some products. This was re-
garded as both an organizational, regulatory and technical barrier as it should be mitigated in several
different ways. Technically, as it was mentioned in Section 5, far from all packaging materials, as well
as SUPs or other appliances are labelled with sorting guidance or information regarding material com-
position. This can make it very difﬁcult for the user of the appliance, or the person who is disposing of
the item, to discern how to sort it. In addition, in terms of organization, as it was mentioned in Section
3, Denmark currently takes a very unharmonized approach to waste management, which results in
very different local solutions among the municipalities. Moreover, it was also found that every hospital
was largely responsible for their own management of waste. This results in many different sorting
schemes, both at the household level, and on the job. It is very likely, that the many different sorting
strategies that are in place on a national level, add to an even higher degree of confusion among the
public. Not only is it not always possible to discern the material composition of the item which is being
disposed of, but in some cases, depending on the local sorting scheme, it may need to be sorted in a
commingled fraction, other times in a single fraction, and in some cases it might not be appropriate for
recycling at all. The reason as to why this barrier was also regarded as a regulatory barrier, was due
to the lack of regulations targeting the labelling of products in terms of sorting instructions, which was
discussed in Section 5. This barrier was regarded as very prevalent by almost all of the participants.
7. Not always possible to separate materials for sorting
Median level of perceived prevalence: 3 (very prevalent)
Another technical barrier that was identiﬁed, was that it is far from possible to separate all products
or packaging items which are composed of different parts or materials. This was argued in Section
5, where many sterile wraps were documented. Sterile wraps were found to be composed of two
sides of either the same type of plastic, two different types of plastic, or one side plastic and one
in a completely different material (e.g., paper or laminate foil). According to the interviewees, it will
take more time and resources, for staff to separate all sterile wraps, in order to sort them properly.
In addition, a few of the interviewees mentioned that some of the wraps can produce dust when
separated, which should be avoided.
However, it was not only some of the packaging that was difﬁcult to separate. This was also the case
for several single use appliances and other disposable equipment. For example, some appliances
contain several different parts, of which only a few have actual contact with patients. However, most
of these appliances are disposed of as one unit, in the residual waste fraction. However, many of
these items are composed of plastics, which could have been recycled if it had been possible for the
==PAGE:91==
7.2
DEFINED BARRIERS
78
staff to separate the parts, and sort them correctly (in a simple, quick and safe manner). This also
relates to the frustration among staff members, which was described for Barrier 5, where employees
must dispose of multiple whole units of IV-systems containing numerous tubes and IV-bags, whereas
they must sort smaller items like plastic containers for recycling. This barrier was regarded as very
prevalent by the vast majority of the participants.
8. Hospital waste is regarded as ’infectious’ and not appropriate for recycling
Median level of perceived prevalence: 2 (somewhat prevalent)
Some appliances or other items that are used and discarded in hospitals, have been in contact with
a patient. Some of these patients may have an infectious disease or the like. Thus, it has been
found that hospital waste in some cases is generally regarded as ’infectious’. This was identiﬁed as
both a social and regulatory barrier. According to some of the interviewees, some waste companies
are unwilling to accept waste that has been in contact with patients.
In addition, they explained
that it in some cases would be rejected at the border, if attempts were made to export the waste to
international treatment facilities. According to some of the interviewees, this has happened when they
have attempted to export waste to countries such as Germany, due to different regulations. However,
it is unsure whether or not, these regulations are still in place today. Several of the interviewees
and survey participants explained that the perception of hospital waste differs among the recycling
or sorting facilities, and that an increasing number of facilities are open to taking more and more
fractions. In addition, they explained that it also depends on the type of waste treatment facility,
and the technology and working conditions that they can offer. Nevertheless, as it was described in
Section 3, no targets have been put forth at either the EU or national level, that speciﬁcally target
residual hospital waste, although the sector is known for producing a large amount of residual waste.
According to the interviewees, this is likely due to a general perception of all hospital waste being very
contaminated and infectious, despite the estimate made by World Health Organization (2018b), who
claim that 85% of the waste is non-hazardous.
However, another issue that was mentioned was the perception of the waste from the hospital staff.
One interviewee explained that the kitchen staff, for example, would not accept biowaste returns
from patient rooms, due to fear of infected waste streams. This barrier was largely perceived as a
somewhat prevalent barrier.
9. Contaminated waste streams
Median level of perceived prevalence: 1.5 (somewhat prevalent)
Waste streams can be contaminated for many different reasons, and was regarded as a social, or-
ganizational and technical barrier. Waste from hospitals will inevitably sometimes be contaminated
with blood or other body ﬂuids. However, as it was found in Section 5, several other factors can also
contribute to a contaminated waste stream, some of which are more difﬁcult to avoid than others.
These factors can relate to the issue which was discussed in Barrier 7, regarding the difﬁculty (and
sometimes inability) of separating certain items prior to their disposal, due to their design. However,
other more invisible design factors such as glue can also contribute to a contamination. For example,
many of the packaging materials are put together using different types of glue. If the recycling facilities
are not aware of the types of glue that are used, it can be difﬁcult for them to recycle the fraction whilst
maintaining a high quality material. The lack of regulatory initiatives preventing these contaminations,
has been the main factor contributing to the perception of this barrier as falling under the regulations
category. Nevertheless, the hospital staff can also make sorting errors due to lack of knowledge or
==PAGE:92==
7.2
DEFINED BARRIERS
79
human error. For this reason, the barrier has also been considered as a social barrier.
Although these contaminations would become an issue if more of the materials that were found in
the residual waste stream were sorted for recycling (e.g., the plastic fraction), the barrier was merely
regarded as somewhat prevalent. However, there was a large variation in the perceptions among the
different regions, and even within the same region. When assessing the comments of the participants
who did not perceive it to be a large barrier, it seems to be because the hospital staff are not required
to take factors such as glue or labels into consideration within their current sorting schemes. Instead,
these types of materials are disposed of as residual waste, as was discussed in Section 5. Thus,
employees may not regard contamination to be a barrier if they were to increase their recycling rate
of the recyclable fractions that they are currently included in their sorting schemes. However, if more
materials were to be included (e.g., peel pouches), it is likely that the perceived prevalence of this
barrier would increase.
10. Sometimes health care tasks demand a shift in priorities
Median level of perceived prevalence: 2 (somewhat prevalent)
Hospital staff save lives, and sometimes they must deal with emergencies. In such situations, human
life is of the upmost highest priority, and staff members may not have time to think about disposing
of waste in the correct manner. This will especially be the case if materials require separation prior
to sorting, or if is not completely clear how the item should be sorted. Some of the participants ex-
plained that it would require an increased number of employees, if the full recycling potential was to
be realized during such procedures. This is why the barrier was perceived to fall under the organiza-
tional category. As can be seen in Figure 19, the majority of the participants regarded this barrier as
very prevalent. However, some did not regard it as prevalent at all. Thus, it seems that few regions
are better at mitigating this barrier than others, and provides another example of an area where an
increased communication among regions could be beneﬁcial.
11. Lack of time in a busy working environment
Median level of perceived prevalence: 2 (somewhat prevalent)
As it was mentioned in Barrier 7, it will take an increased amount of time, and thus more resources, if
the total recycling potential of the residual waste fraction is to be realized. As it was found in Section
5, this is especially the case for the plastic fraction, where many items are composed of several
different materials, which require separation prior to sorting. In addition, as mentioned in Barrier 6,
it can sometimes be difﬁcult for staff members to discern how to sort the material which, thus, adds
to the feeling of not having enough time to sort correctly. Thus, a lack of time in a busy working
environment was identiﬁed as another organizational barrier.
One of the participants communicated that they, through the use of focus group interviews, had ob-
served that employees were very motivated in terms of ’going the extra mile’ to sort their waste.
Other participants claimed that it would inevitably increase the hospital’s expenses, if the full recycling
potential was to be realized, especially the materials that require separation. This statement was in-
dependently backed up by the majority of participants and regarded as somewhat prevalent by almost
all of the participants.
12. Lack of space for sorting
Median level of perceived prevalence: 2.5 (somewhat to very prevalent)
==PAGE:93==
7.3
LIMITATIONS
80
There is not always a sufﬁcient amount of room to distribute an increased number of sorting bins
around in the wards or patient rooms. In addition, there is a limited amount of space in many of the
wash rooms and preparation rooms, which is where much of the sorting takes place at many of the
hospitals, (at the department level). This was also mentioned in Section 4, based on the ﬁndings of
Sund (2020). Here it was found, that especially the operating departments were frustrated with the
lack of space, as they felt that they discarded a great deal of recyclable materials as residual waste.
Thus, it was found that the region of Southern Denmark were attempting different methods of which
they could accommodate this barrier. One of these methods included the production of a new type
of waste trolley, as seen in Figure 13 and 14. Based on the interviews, it seemed that other regions
were also testing different types of waste trolleys.
According to the vast majority of both interviewees and survey participants, this barrier had a some-
what perceived prevalence. The few participants who did not perceive it as a barrier, explained that
it was a matter of re-organizing and proposing local practical solutions. This barrier was, thus, re-
garded as both an organizational and technical barrier. This is because, it can be mitigated either by
re-organizing the already existing space and facilities that are available. However, it also demands
the designing of new solutions, that are applicable in a limited amount of space.
13. Lack of ﬁnancial incentives for sorting some materials
Median level of perceived prevalence: 2 (somewhat prevalent)
As it was explained in Section 3, the circular economy is constrained by three pillars, namely tech-
nology, sustainability and economy. Thus, although most materials can technically be sorted and
recycled, it might demand very expensive processes, and the end results might not provide a sufﬁ-
cient quality. With respect to such products, a market typically does not exist, as was discussed in
Section 5. In some cases, the virgin material is cheaper than the recycled material, which can lead
companies to buying the virgin material rather than the recycled material, even if they would have pre-
ferred the recycled material. In many cases, it has also been found to be cheaper to incinerate waste,
rather than recycle it. Thus, this was regarded as a regulatory barrier, as it can be mitigated through
taxes or other legal measures. Many participants reported that the hospital in which they worked,
did not earn any money from sorting their waste for recycling. Therefore, most of the participants
perceived this as a somewhat to very prevalent barrier.
7.3
LIMITATIONS
Only a limited number of candidates were selected to participate in the barrier survey. However, the
results were able to provide an overall idea of the perceived prevalence. Nevertheless, within one of
the regions, four candidates were selected to participate in the survey, in order to observe the degree
of variation among their answers. A slight variation was observed. This could have been due to
differences in the region, or perhaps some questions may have been misunderstood, and answered
on the basis of misconception. Thus, the survey should be conducted on a wider range of participants,
in order to obtain a deeper insight into the general trend of perceived prevalence within each region.
In addition, the majority of the individuals, which were selected to participate, did not have experience
working in the medical departments. Thus, their knowledge of some of the barriers may be slightly
superﬁcial, or based on a general perception. A more diverse group of candidates should be selected
for the survey, if a more accurate result is to be obtained.
==PAGE:94==
7.3
LIMITATIONS
81
7.4
KEY FINDINGS
Section 7 has provided an overview of the barriers that are faced by Danish hospitals in terms
of increasing the recycling rate of their residual waste, and thereby, in moving towards a circular
economy. This study has identiﬁed 13 different barriers, through an iterative approach, which
has been displayed in Figure 2. Each of the barriers were then categorized as being either
a social, organizational, regulatory or technical barrier. In addition, a survey was conducted,
assessing the perceived prevalence of each barrier, among a total of nine participants (at least
one from every region). The survey provided the participants with the opportunity to rank every
barrier as either not prevalent, somewhat prevalent or very prevalent.
The barriers that are identiﬁed by this study, including their assigned categories and median
level of perceived prevalence include:
1. Lack of EU and national targets regarding production and procurement
(Regulatory) (Somewhat to very prevalent)
2. Lack of restrictions on amount and type of polymers contained in SUPs
(Regulatory) (Very prevalent)
3. Lack of local recycling facilities
(Technical) (Somewhat prevalent)
4. Insufﬁcient quantities of recyclable fractions due to a fragmented market
(Organizational) (Somewhat prevalent)
5. Lack of motivation among employees
(Social) (Not prevalent)
6. Difﬁcult to discern how to sort different products
(Organizational, regulatory, technical) (Very prevalent)
7. Not always possible to separate materials for sorting
(Technical) (Very prevalent)
8. Hospital waste is regarded as ’infectious’ and not appropriate for recycling
(Social, regulatory) (Somewhat prevalent)
9. Contaminated waste streams
(Social, organizational, technical) (Not to somewhat prevalent)
10. Sometimes health care tasks demand a shift in priorities
(Organizational) (Somewhat prevalent)
11. Lack of time in a busy working environment
(Organizational) (Somewhat prevalent)
12. Lack of space for sorting
(Organizational, technical) (Very prevalent)
13. Lack of ﬁnancial incentives for sorting some materials
(Regulatory, technical) (Somewhat prevalent)
