==PAGE:96==
DISCUSSION
8
==PAGE:97==
84
8
DISCUSSION
8.1
BARRIER PRIORITIZATION
This study has found that the hospital staff is generally very motivated when it comes to increasing
their recycling rates. Nevertheless, several barriers have been identiﬁed that prevent them from doing
so. The majority of these barriers were found to be technical, or organizational barriers. However,
also many regulatory barriers were identiﬁed. Among the barriers that were perceived to be the most
prevalent were, (1) the current lack of restrictions on the amount and type of polymers contained in
SUPs, (2) the difﬁculties faced in discerning how to sort many different products, and (3) the design of
the product sometimes preventing its separation prior to disposal. In addition, according to the results
from the LCA, it was found that the largest avoided environmental impacts among all of the material
fractions, were obtained from diverting the plastic fraction from incineration to recycling. In addition, it
is likely that this also would be the case for the plastic found in the non-recyclable fraction.
When comparing the ﬁndings from the LCA with the most prevalent barriers, it seems that all four
barriers prevent the recycling of the plastic fraction in the residual waste. In addition, the barriers
regarding the lack of restrictions on polymers, and the design preventing separation, also affect the
amount of plastic that ends up as non-recyclable.
However, when taking all of the barriers into consideration, some barriers seem to be more respon-
sible for amplifying a greater extent of the other barriers, than others. For example, the regulatory
barrier regarding the lack of restrictions on polymers in SUPs, is likely to amplify several other barri-
ers if attempts are not made to eliminate or mitigate it. For example, the lack of restrictions result in
an increase of many different types of plastic products found throughout the hospitals, which makes
it difﬁcult for the hospitals to set up appropriate sorting schemes, and results in insufﬁcient amounts
of individual recyclable fractions. The many fractions make it difﬁcult for hospitals to ﬁnd the space
for the increased number of bins needed to sort these fractions, which may results in many different
local solutions (as there is little to no coordination of waste management schemes between hospi-
tals), resulting in the potential increase in confusion among hospital staff when having to sort an item,
especially when moving between hospitals, or even departments, as the solutions in place may differ.
This increased confusion will likely lead to an increase in human sorting error, which will inevitably
lead to more contaminated waste streams. The same type of ampliﬁcation is to be expected for the
regulatory barrier regarding the lack of EU and national targets that were found for production and
procurement.
Some barriers also act as bottle necks to a greater extent than other barriers. This is seen in the
regulatory and technical barrier regarding the lack of ﬁnancial incentives for sorting some materials.
Even if the hospitals are able to produce a higher degree of sorted recyclables, waste companies
will not be interested in purchasing them, if there is no market (e.g., if virgin materials are cheaper
than the recycled materials). This will likely also lead to an increased frustration among hospital
staff, as their efforts with sorting may go to waste. According to Pieters (1991), this can cause for
the motivation among many staff members to drop both rapidly and dramatically, which can require
a lot of effort to reverse. Pieters (1991) distinguishes between costs (e.g., expected or experienced
sacriﬁces) and beneﬁts (e.g., expected or experienced positive consequences) of behavior, in terms
of understanding the reasons behind certain attitudes or responses. Here, it is important that the
perceived costs do not outweigh the perceived beneﬁts. A similar type of bottle neck effect could also
be expected from the social and regulatory barrier regarding the perception of hospital waste as being
==PAGE:98==
8.2
BARRIER ELIMINATION
85
infectious or not appropriate for recycling. Naturally, some hospital waste is infectious. However, this
should not prevent the large amount of recyclables, which are non hazardous, from being recycled.
As mentioned, although this study identiﬁed a large segregation potential, an even larger fraction was
regarded as non recyclable, of which was estimated to consist of a rather large portion of different
types of plastics. Usually, plastics are regarded as non recyclable if they cannot be separated from
a non recyclable component, or if they have had contact with a patient. However, in some cases,
it is not the entire item that has had contact with a patient. Thus, the technical barrier, which was
mentioned above, regarding the fact that not all materials are separable for sorting, is also regarded
as having a bottle neck-like effect. This is because it completely prevents the possibility of sorting the
item for recycling, even when other barriers such as lack of space, have been eliminated.
Because some of the barriers have more wide ranging effects than others, it is important to identify
them, such that they can be prioritized in terms of elimination. This study regards regulatory barriers
as especially important, as they have the power to effect upstream tasks such as product design,
which can make it easier for hospital staff to sort the waste, and they can affect market response
by use of ﬁnancial incentives. By targeting these barriers, it is very likely that several other barriers
will become less prevalent or diminish entirely. For example, by targeting the barriers regarding EU
and national targets for upstream activities and regarding restrictions on polymers, it is likely that
barriers regarding difﬁculties in discerning material types, in separating products and contaminated
waste streams will decrease. In addition, it is also likely that it will take less time to sort the materials,
which will also have an effect on the barrier regarding lack of time in a busy working environment.
Moreover, it is especially important to focus on barriers that prevent motivation, such as the lack of
ﬁnancial incentives, as described above, which could be eliminated by use of taxation.
8.2
BARRIER ELIMINATION
There are many different methods by which barriers can be eliminated. This especially depends on
the type of barrier, and its category. However, it also depends on the local circumstances of the given
hospital that is facing the barrier. As it was found with the survey in Section 7, barriers are perceived
differently from region to region, and even by members within the same region. This can be due to a
number of reasons. For example, as the participants were asked to ﬁll out the survey online, some
questions may have been misunderstood, and answered on the basis of misconception. However, it
could also be due to different local circumstances, such as the size of the hospital and the amount of
space available in the departments, a different organizational structure, the size of the region, or the
amount of experience or knowledge that has been obtained in relation to waste sorting.
As it was described in Section 4, all of the regions are already taking many steps to eliminate numer-
ous of the different barriers, which have been identiﬁed by this study, at their hospitals. Some regions
have conducted waste audits to map their segregation potential; some have created publicly available
databases with information regarding results, which they have obtained through their own research;
some are experimenting with different types of waste trolleys or other creative methods of space max-
imization, with the purpose of increasing the segregation efﬁciencies throughout their departments;
and others are conducting detailed laboratory assessments of their plastic waste fractions. However,
as this study has shown, the regions are all facing many of the same barriers. In addition, it has
also been found that much of their residual waste is very similar in both composition and segregation
potentials. Nevertheless, it has been found that numerous regions are conducting similar research
simultaneously, without necessarily being aware of it. Thus, this study regards it as a vital step in the
barrier elimination process, to increase the amount of communication that is facilitated across both
==PAGE:99==
8.2
BARRIER ELIMINATION
86
departments, hospitals and regions.
As it was argued in Section 4, this study proposes an increased level of centralization among the
regions in terms of their decision-making regarding waste management, to mitigate this issue. In
Section 4.3, the different advantages and disadvantages that are associated with centralization
were discussed. Here, the advantages mentioned included cheaper costs, efﬁcient decision-making,
smoother implementation of initiatives and an increased transparency. On the other hand, the disad-
vantages that were mentioned included a decrease in employee loyalty, which could arise if employ-
ees were deprived of their ability to take responsibility, or from not being included in decision-making
processes.
When taking into consideration both the advantages and disadvantages associated with centraliza-
tion, and based on some of the organizational structures that are already in place, it is likely that a
more effective waste management system could be constructed for Danish somatic hospitals. The
purpose of such a centralization, would be to aid the regions in eliminating barriers more efﬁciently,
and thereby increasing their recycling rates. Based on the knowledge that has been obtained through-
out this study, it seems that some level of centralization could prove a favorable option (e.g., that
different areas of responsibility are divided among different levels of management). In other words,
some decisions may prove suitable for national-level decision-making, whilst others may prove more
suitable for the regional-, hospital- or even department-level. This was seen in the Capital Region,
where external waste management tasks were managed by the CEJ, and internal tasks by the Service
Departments in the hospitals. Thus, this study proposes an alternative model for a more centralized
approach to waste management, see Figure 20. As can be seen, examples of different areas of
responsibility have been given with respect to a national-, regional-, hospital- and department level of
management, that attempt to mitigate the disadvantages of centralization, and take into consideration
the different local circumstances of each hospital.
8.2.1
MODEL DESCRIPTION
National-level management
As can be seen, the model proposes that some working areas are fully centralized, and made by
a national level of management.
These could include deﬁning common sorting guidelines for all
somatic hospitals in Denmark, developing an online platform for educational purposes, establishing
ﬁxed methods of accounting, putting forth requirements to suppliers and facilitating meetings and
events for knowledge sharing. Here, the national level of management could for example, require for
identical waste trolleys and bins to be used by all hospitals and deﬁne a ’general’ system set-up in
terms of their placement and handling, whilst allowing room for ﬂexibility. This could, for example, be
done by providing a range of different system set-ups depending on the amount of space available,
the type of department, and so on. In addition, they could prepare sorting instructions based on
their common guidelines, and provide them on an online platform. In this way, the department level
of management at each hospital, can acquire their instructions from one place. This would ensure
uniformity of sorting instructions on a national level and minimize confusion. In addition, the role of
the national level of management would be to obtain the general overview of the general tendencies
that are apparent among hospitals in Denmark. This would also make it easier for them to organize
pilot projects, in terms of identifying which hospitals were appropriate to conduct them in.
Regional-level management
As the interviewees mentioned, several differences such as geography or system maturity, can make
it difﬁcult to make decisions on behalf of all hospitals in Denmark. To accommodate these points, the
==PAGE:100==
8.2
BARRIER ELIMINATION
87
Figure 20: An initial proposal for an alternative model for a more centralized approach to waste management,
for the Danish hospitals.
Note: The model has been constructed in connection with the preparation of this thesis, and it is based on the
information obtained through interviews and the already existing organizational structures. However, the model
and its different areas of responsibility have not been discussed with any of the interviewees. In addition, no
ﬁnancial aspects have been taken into consideration. It is therefore to be perceived as an initial proposal which
should be further developed.
==PAGE:101==
8.2
BARRIER ELIMINATION
88
model proposes that a regional level of management has the responsibility of initiating the purchasing
agreements for the given region. In addition, the regional level of management should coordinate
the appropriate logistics options for the waste in the region, taking into consideration the amount of
hospitals in the region, their distance to one another, national regulations etc. For example, Maria
from the Central Denmark Region, mentioned the installation of shredders at one of the region’s
hospitals for the purpose of preparing plastic waste for transportation. This could be an example of a
regional-level task.
In addition, the regional level of management should be responsible for conducting the region’s waste
accounts, and securing that they are performed in accordance with the national guidelines.
The
purpose of this would be to provide a better basis for comparison of regional progress, and to better
identify which areas need improvement throughout the system. As it was found in Section 4, many
of the regions make use of different accounting methods when calculating their recycling rate. This
makes it difﬁcult to compare and monitor the progress of the regions on a national level. According to
Christensen (2011), rewards are an effective method to affect efﬁciencies of the segregation system.
If common accounting criteria were established, it could be possible to reward hospitals that perform
well, by e.g., nominating them as the ’best waste segregator of the year’ or providing them with
a ’circular economy champion’-stamp and so on. This could also provide employees with a more
direct beneﬁt of their efforts, rather than merely affecting society at large, which could contribute to
even higher levels of engagement. A more uniform method of accounting will also make it easier for
regions to monitor where increased levels of assistance are needed, or where to focus their efforts
and resources. For example, the regional-level could assign members of a hospital-level management
team, from a hospital which is performing well, to assist a team from a hospital that is struggling.
Generally, the regional level of management should have an overview over the tendencies among the
hospitals in the region, so as to assist the national level of management in their decision making.
Hospital-level management
Like in the Capital Region, the model presented in Section 20 proposes a hospital level of man-
agement, that is responsible for managing the internal waste management tasks. Here, they should
assess the degree of which it is possible for the hospital to meet the common guidelines set forth by
the national level, and coordinate speciﬁc methods of nudging based on the given maturity of their
system and level of motivation among their employees.
In addition, the hospital level of management should provide the employees of the hospital with ex-
pertise in terms of rules regarding sorting of certain material types. Thus, if employees are unsure of
how to sort a speciﬁc type of material or of the material composition of a given product, they should be
able to consult with the hospital level of management. In addition, the members of the hospital level
of management should act as the voice of the given hospital’s employees. Therefore, they should
maintain a high level of communication with the members of the department levels of management,
and secure that their frustrations are heard. Moreover, they should monitor the amount of waste that
is segregated at the hospital, possibly by use of weights in bins, as is being tested in the Central
Denmark Region, and identify which departments are performing well. Thereby, they too can provide
rewards to departments, or speciﬁc employees, that are performing well.
Department-level management
Finally, the model proposes a department level of management which is responsible for securing that
all of the decisions, which are made on the national level, actually work when applied in practice. In
addition, it is responsible for securing that the department’s employees meet the common guidelines
to the degree that it is possible. If spatial limitations are evident, the department level of management
==PAGE:102==
8.2
BARRIER ELIMINATION
89
should make sure that it takes appropriate steps to best accommodate these. In addition, it must
assess what types of waste fractions are mainly produced by the department, to better meet the
needs of the employees in terms of e.g., providing the appropriate sized bins for the department’s
waste trolleys.
This level of management should also be responsible for the registration of all products and equipment
that is meant for redistribution, on the online marketplace, after which the responsibility is shifted to
the hospital level, to make sure the items are properly redistributed to the purchaser. All sorting
instructions should be managed by the department level of management, and should be acquired
from the online platform provided by the national level.
8.2.1.1
COMMUNICATION ACROSS LEVELS OF MANAGEMENT
The purpose of having each level of management responsible for a set of clearly deﬁned tasks, is to
increase the level of transparency on a national level, so that employees always know who to report
to or consult with, when in doubt or in need of expertise or insights. Each level of management should
meet on a regular basis, both internally and with other levels of management, to discuss decisions,
progress, obstacles, barriers and so on.
This is to help build employee loyalty, as each level of
management is provided with some degree of inﬂuence.
Although some decisions are made by the national level of management, increased transparency will
provide them with the ability to easily consult with all levels of management, when in need of insights.
For example, if they are to discuss a decision that will have an inﬂuence on the department level,
they can easily consult with department-level representatives during the decision-making process.
This increased level of transparency, could likely contribute to a more robust system. A personal
observation was made during the selection of interviewees for the different interviews, which were
conducted in connection with each research area. Here, a noticeable difference was experienced
from region to region in terms of how difﬁcult it was to identify the right candidate for the interview.
This was largely due to the different systems in place (or lack thereof). A greater level of transparency
will make it easier to identify, the appropriate candidate for the given topic of interest. This will be
beneﬁcial both internally (e.g., among employees and members from all levels of management), and
externally (e.g., among consultants, waste companies, press, students, and so on).
8.2.1.2
GENERAL REMARKS AND MODEL DRAWBACKS
It should be mentioned that the model merely provides an outline to an alternative approach to waste
management, focusing mainly on optimizing the system, such that it becomes better equipped to take
on the barriers faced in transitioning to a circular economy. In this regard, there has been a focus
on yielding the beneﬁts communicated by the Corporate Finance Institute (2021) to a more central-
ized system, whilst the model has been adjusted with the purpose of minimizing their communicated
disadvantages. However, ﬁnancial aspects have not been taken into consideration during the prepa-
ration of the model. Thus, it is not possible to conclude whether such a system can sustain itself
economically. Further research is necessary to be able to draw upon such conclusions.
In addition, it is likely that it would require much effort to transform from the current systems in place,
to the one proposed in Figure 20. As it has been discussed, currently many different systems are
in place among Danish hospitals. It is likely, that some feel that they already have a system that
works well for them, and thus, would not be interested in transitioning to a new model. In addition,
the smoother implementation of initiatives, which was described as an advantage to the centralized
==PAGE:103==
8.3
DANISH HOSPITALS IN A CIRCULAR ECONOMY
90
decision-making system by Corporate Finance Institute (2021), assumes a more top down approach
than the one presented in Figure 20. According to the Corporate Finance Institute (2021), it is the
involvement of only a few people that makes the decision-making process more efﬁcient. This is
because they are able to discuss the details of each decision in one meeting, and communicate them
to the lower levels of the organization for implementation. They go on to explain that the process
will take longer and conﬂicts will arise, if lower-level managers are involved in the decision-making
process. According to the Corporate Finance Institute (2021), some members of the lower level of
management may object to the decisions if their input is ignored, which instead can result in a lengthy
and complicated implementation process. However, the model proposed by this study, does take this
into consideration to some degree, as it allows for some levels of management to be fully responsible
for speciﬁc areas of responsibility. In order to assess the possible effects, more research would need
to be conducted, speciﬁcally assessing the effects of an implementation of the model. Perhaps an
assessment could be made of the Capital Region’s waste management system, as they are currently
the only region with a centralized level of management. In addition, the response to such a system
should be assessed among hospital staff on a national scale.
8.3
DANISH HOSPITALS IN A CIRCULAR ECONOMY
Currently the average recycling rate among Danish hospitals was found to be around 20%. It is
unclear whether or not the target set forth by the EU in terms of achieving a recycling rate of 60% of
municipal solid waste by 2030, also encompasses the residual waste produced by small to medium
enterprises, such as Danish hospitals. However, if this is the case, the Danish hospitals will need
to more than double their current recycling rates. In addition, currently no monitoring of recycling
rates regarding packaging waste was identiﬁed, and thus, it is difﬁcult to make any conclusions on
the distance to targets, which are faced by the regions. However, with the estimated segregation
potential found in the residual waste in Section 5, it seems that the hospitals currently do not have the
possibility of meeting a target of 60%, even in an ideal situation where the total amount of recyclable
materials were recycled (whilst also anticipating that these fractions contained no contaminants).
Taking into consideration the vast amounts of SUPs used by the Danish hospitals, it seems that they
are currently ignoring the inner, most highly prioritized loop of the circular economy (i.e., that products
are kept in use for as long as possible). In addition, allegedly the regions are still in the process of
switching from reusable products to disposable products (Godt Sygehus Byggeri, nd). According to,
Rasmus Nielsen, who is a consultant working with sustainable transition in the Captial Region, the
new sterile centers, which are being built in the hospitals, have been designed to handle a smaller
amount of products than before (Godt Sygehus Byggeri, nd). Thus, this poses a challenge in itself, if
requirements are made for more reusable products in Danish healthcare.
It seems that the many different waste management systems in place, and the lack of communication
between regions, will make it difﬁcult for them become fully resource efﬁcient, and complying with
the requirements set forth by a circular economy. Inevitably, hospitals must prioritize patient and em-
ployee safety in their practice, and this may not always harmonize with the requirements of a circular
economy. However, as this section has outlined, it seems that the hospitals do have the potential
to move towards increased resource efﬁciency in several ways. The different recommendations pro-
posed by this study, which can aid the hospitals in moving towards a more circular economic practice,
will be outlined in Section 8.5
==PAGE:104==
8.4
LIMITATIONS
91
8.4
LIMITATIONS
This section will outline the general limitations that have been identiﬁed in connection with carrying out
this study. The limitations that were perceived more speciﬁcally for each of the research areas, have
been discussed in each of the given sections. For example, the limitations that speciﬁcally regarding
the studies used to estimate the average composition of residual waste from Danish hospitals have
been described in Section 5.2.3. Moreover, the limitations regarding the LCA have been described
in Section 6.3.3.2. Thus, this section provides a more general overview of the limitations perceived
of the project as a whole.
8.4.1
INTERVIEWS
As this study was carried out during the Covid-19 pandemic, all of the interviews were carried out
virtually, via Teams. In addition, supplementary information was typically acquired through e-mail.
Because of this, no visits were made to any of the regions, with the exception of the Region of
Southern Denmark, which was visited in connection with a previous study. However, this means that
the majority of the information, which has been provided in this study, is based on information that
has been obtained through means of virtual communication. Based on the ﬁndings acquired in the
Region of Southern Denmark, many observations were made, which could not have been obtained,
had physical inspections not been made of numerous departments. Here, it was possible to talk to
multiple employees, with many different proﬁles, and witness their surroundings in person. This was
very helpful when conducting the interviews in connection with this study, as it provided some level of
prior understanding and background knowledge regarding some of the daily routines that are carried
out at a hospital. However, it may also have acted as a bias, as associations were naturally made to
the departments, which were inspected in person, when discussing the conditions of other hospitals
with interviewees from the other regions.
However, some steps were taken to mitigate this limitation. For example, all of the descriptions of each
of the regions, which can be found in Appendix A, were sent to the interviewees from the given region
for approval. In addition, interviews were conducted with several different individuals from many of
the regions, with the purpose of gaining a broader insight of their practices. Nevertheless, all of the
interviewees worked at either the regional- or hospital level. No individuals working at the department
level, such as doctors, nurses, waste porters or kitchen staff, were interviewed in connection with
this study. However, some of the interviewees did have previous experience with working at the
department level.
8.4.2
WASTE COMPOSITIONS
This study estimates the average waste composition of residual waste from Danish hospitals. This
estimate was made based on the ﬁndings from three different waste audits. These three waste audits
differed in the types of fractions that were segregated, and in their sampling time. This resulted in the
need to correct some of the fractions, when estimating the average composition. This was because,
some material fractions were only segregated in one of the studies, and some were merged in the
other, and so on. In addition, some of the fractions that were segregated in all of the studies, were
separated in different ways. This was the case for the plastic fraction, for example. Here, two of the
studies separated only clean plastics, whereas another study also included some dirty plastics.
The two studies that assessed the entire residual waste composition, and not only a single fraction,
were both conducted during the Covid-19 pandemic. This could also cause some disturbance in the
==PAGE:105==
8.5
RECOMMENDATIONS
92
data, compared to if it had been assessed prior to the pandemic. More waste audits would need to
be conducted, preferably in the same manner, to assess this potential disturbance.
Another limitation coupled to the generation of the average residual waste composition, was that only
one of the audits had been conducted ﬁrst hand. The two others were based on information obtained
through interviews, and from literature. However, one of the waste audits was in the process of being
carried out, and thus, no literature was readily available. The ﬁndings from this audit were, thus,
solely based on information obtained through interviews. Therefore, it was not possible to gain a
deeper insight into the actual materials that were observed in the waste. However, descriptions were
provided of the general composition of each fraction by the author.
Finally, although this study has focused mainly on the materials that are more readily recyclable, a
large portion of plastics were considered to be non-recyclable. This was typically because they had
been in contact with a patient, if they contained PVC, or if it was not possible to separate them for
sorting. However, it is possible that a large segregation potential exists within this fraction, if product
design was reevaluated. For example, as previously described, this could be by designing products
such that the components, which are to come into contact with patients, could be easily detached
prior to disposal.
8.4.3
FINANCIAL ASPECTS
All of the proposals that have been made in connection with this study, have not included any ﬁnancial
evaluations. This means, that it is not possible to make any conclusions regarding whether or not the
proposals can sustain themselves from a ﬁnancial perspective. Therefore, the proposals should be
regarded as an initial proposal for a more ideal system set-up given that unlimited ﬁnancial resources
were available. However, more work would need to be carried out, to investigate the actual costs
of the proposals. Thereafter, the proposals should be compared, in terms of their costs and their
expected outcomes or impacts, before an actual strategy can be put forth.
8.5
RECOMMENDATIONS
This section will propose recommendations for further work. However, recommendations speciﬁcally
regarding the LCA, have been described in Section 6.3.3.3.
8.5.1
ALTERNATIVE MODEL IMPLEMENTATION
This study proposes the implementation of an alternative model to decision-making regarding hospital
waste management. This model is based on the observations that were made in connection with this
study. In addition, it is based on the disadvantages and advantages, which are coupled to increasing
the level of centralization in the decision-making process, that were found from literature. However,
it has made no attempts to research the actual responses to the model, among the individuals that
work in the regions or Danish hospitals. In this regard, it could be interesting to conduct a wide-range
survey, assessing the perception among employees from all throughout the sector. In addition, the
costs associated to its implementation should also be investigated.
8.5.2
A GENERAL WASTE MANAGEMENT SET-UP
As mentioned in the model description, the model proposes that the national level of management
design a general waste management set-up. The purpose of the general set-up is to minimize the
==PAGE:106==
8.5
RECOMMENDATIONS
93
degree of variation in waste management approaches from hospital to hospital, thereby to minimize
confusion among employees. Research must be made into the local conditions of each hospital in
Denmark, in order to gain an overview of the different types of local conditions that are apparent,
and thereby, prepare a general set-up that meets their requirements. Inevitably, one general set-up
will not be applicable to all hospitals. However, a general set-up could be proposed with alternative
supplementary options depending on the types of issues that were identiﬁed locally. Research should
also be made, regarding the cases where such a general set-up would not be applicable, so as to
better understand these conditions, and the extent of which such cases are evident.
8.5.3
ONLINE PLATFORMS
The alternative model proposed by this study, also mentions the construction of an online platform for
educational material, sorting guidelines and knowledge sharing. Research should be made in terms
of assessing the available online platforms that are currently in use by the regions. Here, a comparison
could be made regarding the different types of material which they offer, their user experience, and
the general impact that they have on the employees in the region. Once sufﬁcient knowledge has
been obtained, a new platform can be constructed.
8.5.4
SEGREGATION POTENTIAL OF NON RECYCLABLES
As previously mentioned, this study has not assessed the segregation potential within the non re-
cyclable fraction of residual waste to a great extent. Materials were merely considered to be either
recyclable or non-recyclable and, thereby, sorted accordingly. However, taking the large size of the
non-recyclable fraction into account and the current distance to the EU target regarding the recycling
of plastic products, research should be conducted to assess its composition, so as to propose alter-
native options to product design. It is important to take into consideration the guiding principles of the
circular economy, which state that we must keep products and materials in use and design out waste
and pollution. Thus, the ﬁndings from such research could be included in the common Nordic re-
quirements, which are currently in the process of being constructed, for the multinational suppliers of
medical appliances. By providing concrete speciﬁcations, it is likely that the suppliers will react more
openly to the requirements, which may have an effect on the recyclability of the products produced.
In addition, with Danish hospitals relying on a large amount of SUPs in their practices, efforts should
be made to observe the potential to assess which products could perhaps be substituted with more
permanent solutions.
However, as was mentioned in Section 4, research is currently also being conducted, by PhD Tiffany
Marilou Ramos at RUC, on the extent of which it is possible to use pyrolysis as a treatment method
for non-recyclable plastics. Using her ﬁndings, the LCA screening model should be updated, so as to
take into consideration the potentially avoided impacts of using pyrolysis to treat the non-recyclable
plastic fraction. And in addition, the quality of the oil output, and thereby the types of products in which
it can be used to make, should also be assessed.
8.5.5
AMOUNT AND TYPE OF POLYMERS IN SUPS
One of the barriers, which was identiﬁed by this study, mentions the lack of restrictions on the amounts
and types of polymers that are found in medical SUPs. This was based on the large variation in
polymers that was observed in the residual waste fraction. However, it is very likely that this variation
is even larger in the non-recyclable fraction, than the one observed among the clean plastic fraction.
==PAGE:107==
8.5
RECOMMENDATIONS
94
Thus, research should be conducted in terms of mapping the characteristics that are necessary in
medical appliances and packaging. Once this has been done, it should be easier to propose a list
of restrictions for the types of polymers that should be allowed in medical SUPs. In addition, it could
also make it easier for the producers of packaging to make the necessary changes to the materials
that they use, once the EPR scheme has been adopted, such that they are of a more homogenous
composition on a global scale.
==PAGE:108==
CONCLUSION
9
==PAGE:109==
96
9
CONCLUSION
This study’s main objective was to assess the barriers that prevent Danish somatic hospitals from
increasing the recycling rate of their residual waste. In order to obtain such an assessment, eight
speciﬁc objectives were deﬁned. Thus, this conclusion seeks to answer each one of these objectives.
1. Provide an overview of the current circular economy-related targets, initiatives and poli-
cies that may have a direct or indirect impact on the residual waste production from
Danish hospitals, on both the EU and national Danish level.
Both the EU and Danish Government have communicated their desire to move towards a circular
economy. In connection with this, they have each put forth several different targets and measures that
aim to close the loop in our linear economy. However, almost no requirements were found to be aimed
directly at the healthcare system. In addition, the actual targets set forth seem to mainly focus on
downstream practices in product value chains. An example of this was seen with the recycling targets
set forth by the EU, and adopted by the Danish Government. These focus only on increasing recycling
rates, and disregard potential reductions in waste. This places the majority of the responsibility of
increased recycling rates, on the producers of waste. Nevertheless, the Danish Government have
also announced their implementation of EPR for packaging, which must come into force by 2025,
which will likely have an indirect impact on the amount of packaging waste that is produced by Danish
hospitals. In addition, a more direct requirement is seen in that the Danish Government have required
that all households, and the public and private sector implement a sorting scheme for a deﬁned list of
10 speciﬁc fractions. This will likely promote a more homogenous sorting scheme on a more national
scale. Therefore, the healthcare sector will be impacted both direcly and indirectly by the different
initiatives, which have been put forth, both in terms of the packaging waste that they produce, and the
recycling targets.
2. Provide an overview of the waste management systems currently in place among the ﬁve
Danish regions.
Interviews with 10 different individuals were conducted to examine the ﬁve Danish regions. Here it
was found that all of the regions differ in their approach to waste management. Most of the regions
had a very decentralized approach, where each hospital is responsible for its own management.
Only one region was identiﬁed as having a fully centralized approach, where one organization is
responsible for deﬁning the guidelines of the waste management system of all hospitals in the region.
Thus, many hospitals were responsible for initiating their own purchasing agreements with waste
companies. In addition, no formal communication was observed between the regions in connection
with waste management.
3. Compare the current recycling rates of the ﬁve regions and their distance to EU targets.
All of the regions were found to be motivated in terms of meeting the EU recycling targets. Several
regions had also implemented their own regional recycling targets, of which some were more ambi-
tious than others. However, it was observed that not all of the regions currently monitor their recycling
rate, and that some make use of different calculation methods. Nevertheless, the average recycling
==PAGE:110==
97
rates that are currently monitored were found to range from 18% to 26%. It has thus been assumed
that all regions have a recycling rate of ≈20%. In addition, no recycling rates were obtained for pack-
aging materials speciﬁcally. It was therefore not possible to compare their distance to the EU targets.
However, if hospitals are required to meet the EU target set forth in terms of obtaining a recycling
rate of 60% for municipal solid waste by 2030, all regions will need to more than double their current
recycling rates.
4. Assess the composition of residual waste from a number of Danish hospitals and identify
the most prevalent items.
Interviews with two different individuals, and three different waste audits, from separate studies, which
were conducted in different regions, were used to assess the material composition of residual waste
from four different Danish hospitals. Two of the studies assessed the entire composition of the resid-
ual waste, and the third assessed only the clean plastic fraction. Here it was found that plastic and
biowaste represented the largest fractions of residual waste, of which were mainly composed of ﬂex-
ible plastics in the form of peel pouch packaging, and left-over food scraps, respectively. In both of
the audits, which assessed the entire composition, a very large non-recyclable fraction was observed.
This fraction was mainly composed of IV-bags and tubes, different types of single use appliances,
and surgical linens and gowns. However, the non-recyclable fraction was not assessed in great detail,
and thus, the exact amounts and types of materials found in this fraction are based on visual esti-
mates. More effort should be made to assess this fraction, so as to identify methods of eliminating
non-recyclable products in the design stage.
5. Propose an average composition of residual waste from Danish hospitals.
Using the results found within the three studies, an average composition of residual waste was pro-
posed for Danish hospitals. This composition was composed of ≈15% biowaste, ≈12% plastic (of
which ≈88% was ﬂexible, ≈3% was foil and ≈9% was rigid), ≈3% ﬁbers, ≈1.2% beverage cartons,
≈0.3% electronics, ≈0.2% glass, ≈0.1% metal and ≈0.06% EPS. The remaining ≈68% were as-
sumed to be composed of non-recyclables. The average composition is coupled to some uncertainty
due to the limited number of waste audits that it is based on, and due to their slightly different method-
ologies. However, according to several interviewees, the composition of residual waste from Danish
hospitals is expected to be quite similar on a national scale.
6. Compare the avoided environmental impacts associated to diverting each of the recy-
clable material fractions from incineration to recycling.
An LCA screening model was carried out to compare the environmental impacts associated with
recycling all of the different recyclable material fractions. The model was built using mainly very
generic processes, and disregards both transport and production of the products which have been
disposed of. It is thus coupled to a great deal of uncertainty, and therefore, the analysis has been used
merely to rank the materials in terms of which fraction is associated with the greatest avoided impact,
when diverted from incineration to recycling. Here, the recycling of plastic was found to be the process
that by far was associated to the greatest avoided impacts in the majority of impact categories. It was
also found to result in the largest avoided impact of CO2-eq emissions. Although it was not tested,
it is likely that the plastics in the non-recyclable fraction also are associated with a large impact on
==PAGE:111==
98
the CO2-eq emissions. Two different residual waste compositions were used to assess the avoided
CO2-eq emissions, in order to observe whether it would result in a difference ranking of the materials,
in terms of which was coupled to the highest and lowest impacts. Here it was found that a slight
difference in composition, did not alter the ranking to a great extent. Thus, the analysis concludes
that it is likely that the average composition, which was proposed by this study, can be used as a
proxy for residual waste of Danish hospitals. However, more research should be conducted to clarify
this statement.
7. Investigate which social, organizational, technical and regulatory barriers that currently
prevent the Danish regions from realising the segregation potential of their residual
waste fraction.
This report has made use of an iterative method to identify 13 different social, organizational, reg-
ulatory and technical barriers that currently prevent Danish somatic hospitals from increasing the
recycling rate of their residual waste. The list of barriers includes; (1) lack of EU and national targets
regarding production and procurement; (2) lack of restrictions on amount and type of polymers con-
tained in SUPs; (3) lack of local recycling facilities; (4) insufﬁcient quantities of recyclable fractions
due to a fragmented market; (5) lack of motivation among employees; (6) difﬁcult to discern how to
sort different products; (7) not always possible to separate materials for sorting; (8) hospital waste is
regarded as ’infectious’ and not appropriate for recycling; (9) contaminated waste streams; (10) some-
times health care tasks demand a shift in priorities; (11) lack of time in a busy working environment;
(12) lack of space for sorting; and (13) lack of ﬁnancial incentives for sorting some materials. Based
on a survey conducted with eight different participants, these barriers were ranked in terms of their
perceived prevalence within each region. Here it was found that barriers 2, 6 and 7 were perceived
to be the most prevalent. However, some barriers were observed as having more wide range effects
than others, which should thus be prioritized in terms of eliminations. This could for example be seen
regarding some barrier being more responsible for amplifying a greater extent of the barriers than
others. This was especially found to be the case for many of the regulatory barriers, which focus on
restricting the production of many products and packaging materials, such that they become easier to
recycle.
8. Propose possible methods of barrier elimination.
Lastly, this study has outlined several different methods of barrier elimination. Due to the fact that
the hospitals were found to have such different approaches to waste management, barrier elimination
strategies will depend very much on the local conditions of the given hospital. Thus, this study has
provided a number of examples of different initiatives which are currently being carried out and im-
plemented within the different regions. These include implementing space maximizing waste trolleys,
producing online platforms with publicly available results from research and pilot projects, conducting
research regarding medical SUPs and their material composition, and so on. However, this study
argues that the lack of centralized decision making regarding waste management and communica-
tion across regions, may very likely make it difﬁcult to implement nation wide initiatives to increase
resource efﬁciency and recycling rates. Thus, it proposes an alternative model for a more central-
ized approach to waste management, where a national-, regional-, hospital- and department level
of management are provided with different areas of responsibility. The purpose of this model is to
obtain cheaper costs, efﬁcient decision-making processes, smoother implementation of visions and
==PAGE:112==
99
increased transparency. It should be noted, that this study has not assessed the costs associated to
the transition to an alternative model, and thus no conclusion can be made in terms of whether or not
it would be able to sustain itself in practice. However, it is argued that such a system will provide a
more homogenous handling of waste, and provide all of the hospitals in Denmark with a uniﬁed voice,
when making requirements to multinational suppliers.
