==PAGE:119==
APPENDIX
==PAGE:120==
107
A
REGIONS
Section 4 describes the waste management systems of the ﬁve regions that are responsible for the
healthcare system in Denmark. However, due to the lack of information available regarding their waste
management systems, it was necessary to conduct interviews with representatives from each region.
The information gathered through these interviews has been summarised in this section.
A.1
CAPITAL REGION
Participants:
Thyge Morten Nielsen, Chief Consultant, Center for Real Estate, Unit for Energy
and Environment, Capital Region
Thomas Madsen, Specialist Consultant, Center for Real Estate,Unit for Energy
and Environment, Capital Region
Figure 21: Map of the municipalities in the Capital Region, and hospital locations.
Note:The white outlines deﬁne the municipalities, and the red dots display the hospital locations.
The Capital region has the largest population among the ﬁve regions, and also the smallest area,
giving it by far the largest population density of about 721 inhabitants per km2. The region is comprised
of 29 municipalities and manages seven different hospitals and two hospital units comprised of two
and three hospitals, see Figure 21.
In their Regional Growth and Development Strategy, the Capital Region have set forth their own waste
targets, stating that they aim for a recycling rate of 40% by 2025, 50% by 2030 and at least 80% by
2035 (Capital Region, 2020). All municipalities within the region have agreed to the targets. In total,
the region produces approximately 17,000 tonnes of waste per year, of which the majority is produced
by the region’s hospitals. Of this amount, around 20% were recycled in 2019.
==PAGE:121==
A.1
CAPITAL REGION
108
Figure 10 provides a visualization of the region’s overall organizational structure in terms of waste
management. The region takes a very centralized approach to managing waste with its joint facilities
management (FM)-system called, Center for Real Estate, which in danish translates to ’Center for
Ejendomme’ (CEJ). The CEJ was formed in 2015 and works at the regional level, reporting to the
Regional Council. It consists of an executive board and seven units, which together cover work areas
within building operation and maintenance. The CEJ are also responsible for the overall ”external”
waste management system for all of the hospitals in the region, i.e. the collection and transport, and
purchasing agreements. The individual ”internal” waste management systems for each hospital are
managed on site, i.e. the sorting schemes in each department and the collection and transport of
waste from the departments to the hospital’s waste terminal. However, a standard set of guidelines
have been provided by the CEJ to all hospitals and departments, to secure consistency.
The hospitals in the capital region are required to sort the following waste fractions: batteries, elec-
tronics, glass, chemicals, food waste, medicine, metal, cardboard, paper, plastics, porcelain, clinical
hazardous waste, toner- and ink cartridges. Additional more technical fractions are also sorted, such
as pallets. However, these will not be discussed in this report, as they are not considered to be typical
hospital waste fractions. In Figure 11 a, b and c, the typical waste management setup in any given
department in the Capital Region has been presented.
As can be seen, the region uses different types of waste bins. Smaller bins are hung on walls for
waste types that are produced in small quantities (e.g. porcelain, electronics, glass and metal), and
waste trolleys are placed on the ground for waste types that are produced in larger quantities (e.g.
plastic, general waste and clinical hazardous waste). The waste trolleys are found as either single or
double models (containing either one or two bins per trolley respectively). According to Thyge Nielsen
and Thomas Madsen, the operating rooms are responsible for some of the most impressive recycling
rates, due to the large amount of planning that goes into an operation. Thus, the employees know
exactly what products will be used, and can therefore, prepare the bins accordingly. Thyge Nielsen
and Thomas Madsen explain that lack of space for bins is a challenge faced by many of their hospitals.
However, they believe that it is a matter of priority, and that waste trolleys and wall bins prove an ideal
solution to this challenge, as wall bins do not take up space on the ground, and waste trolleys can be
relocated.
Once bins are full or an operation or procedure has been completed, the bins are transported to the
department’s wash room, which in Danish translates to ’skyllerum’, see Figure 11 d, e and f. Here
the waste is transferred into larger bins that are collected by waste porters. Waste porters transport
the waste to a larger waste room, usually placed in the basement level of the hospital, where the
waste is transferred into appropriate waste containers. Waste is to be transported primarily at the
basement level, to the extent of which it is possible. The waste is then collected from the waste room,
and transported to the hospital’s waste terminal where it is prepared for collection.
As mentioned, the capital region has a centralized waste management system, and thus, have one
primary purchasing agreement with STENA Recycling, to which they send all of their recyclables from
almost all of their hospitals. The reason why the region has made agreements with only one primary
purchaser of their recyclable waste, is to secure a consistent set of requirements for every given waste
fraction, and methods of sorting it across all of their hospitals. In addition it makes it easier for them
to collect their waste related data and monitor their progress. Their mixed waste on the other hand, is
sent to Vestforbrænding and Amager Ressourcecenter for incineration with energy recovery.
The Capital region is for the most part responsible for their own transport of waste, for which they
use a hooklift truck and distribution truck, depending on the fraction. The hospitals in the region
==PAGE:122==
A.2
REGION ZEALAND
109
use the same types of waste bins and containers, as shown in Figure 11, which have been marked
with a labelling system in which they have developed based on the color coding of Vesforbrænding’s
pictogram system. However, these have been replaced with the Common Pictogram System, as a
result of the new national requirements. According to the region, they are not entirely satisﬁed with the
new pictograms, as they feel that several fractions are missing from the common system based on the
fractions that are currently sorted by the region. These include small bulky waste, toner cartridges,
chemical waste, mercury-containing light sources, clinical hazardous waste and truck batteries. After
having inquired about the missing pictograms, the region have been told that they must pay for their
production if they are interested in including them in their system.
A.2
REGION ZEALAND
Participants:
Lærke Møller Toftlund, Consultant, Project leader of Project Circular Zealand,
Gate21
Jesper Lambert Nielsen, Development Consultant, Center for Regional Develop-
ment, Region Zealand
Figure 22: Map of the municipalities in Region Zealand, and hospital locations.
Note:The white outlines deﬁne the municipalities, and the red dots display the hospital locations.
Region Zealand has the second smallest population among the ﬁve regions, however, it has the sec-
ond largest population density of about 115 inhabitants per km2. It is comprised of 17 municipalities,
and manages seven hospitals divided into 4 units, see Figure 22.
==PAGE:123==
A.2
REGION ZEALAND
110
In the region’s Regional Development Strategy 2020-2030, they state their visions in terms of becom-
ing more resource efﬁcient. The region states that they will work towards increasing their recycling
rate, aiming for a recycling rate of 55% for all of their plastic waste (which is the same as the EU tar-
get for plastic packaging waste). However, no concrete targets have been set forth regarding general
waste, or an overall recycling rate. Every year the region produces roughly 2,500 tonnes of general
waste, of which the majority is produced by the region’s hospitals. Currently, the region does not
monitor their recycling rate. It has, therefore, not been possible to provide an estimate.
As opposed to the Central Region, Region Zealand has a less centralized approach to waste manage-
ment, with the exception of their purchasing agreements, which are made by a central organizational
body that is responsible for procurement, production and logistics. Thus, Region Zealand has one
primary purchasing agreement with Marius Pedersen A/S, who collects all of their waste (including
the general waste), prepare it for treatment and sell it to companies that treat it (Marius Pedersen
A/S, 2014). As the region has one primary purchasing agreement, all of the hospitals are required
to sort the same waste fractions. These include batteries, electronics, glass, chemicals, food waste,
medicine, metal, cardboard, paper, plastics, porcelain, clinical hazardous waste, toner- and ink car-
tridges. However, when it comes to the actual management of waste and sorting practices on site, it
is largely the responsibility of the individual hospitals.
All of the hospitals have their own service department, which is responsible for waste management
(at the hospital level) at the given hospital, along with a range of other work areas such as cleaning,
supply and logistics. Typically, the hospitals do not have employees that are ofﬁcially responsible for
waste management at the department level. Thus, it is up to the employees working in the given
department to secure that the right waste bins are available, and that everyone is sorting correctly. It
can, therefore, be difﬁcult for the region to monitor the sorting efﬁciencies and/or systems from the
individual departments. As with the Capital Region, the hospitals in the Zealand Region also have a
wash room in every department. Typically, it is in the wash room that all of the waste bins for sorting
are placed, and thus, the only place where sorting happens in the departments. However, some of the
hospitals, or individual departments may have installed their own additional sorting initiatives, such as
additional smaller sorting bins outside of the wash rooms, to increase the recycling rate.
In recent years, Region Zealand has collaborated with Gate21 on several different projects. Gate21 is
an organization that works together with municipalities, regions, companies and researchers, to test
and develop concrete solutions for creating a green transition (Gate21, nd). Between 2020 and 2023,
the region has collaborated with Gate21 on a project focusing on plastic products and plastic waste.
The project aims at reducing the amount of plastic products that are purchased by the region, along
with increasing the plastic recycling rates, so as to avoid that plastics end up in the general waste
fraction. In this regard, Lærke Toftlund explained that pilot projects are currently being conducted at
several different hospitals in the region, where they are assessing the plastic waste fraction in great
detail in collaboration with Roskilde University (RUC), along with testing the use of waste trolleys for
sorting different plastic fractions in the department wash rooms. The different types of plastics that are
found are assessed in a laboratory, to properly determine their composition and treatment options.
The waste trolleys that are being tested, are the same ones as those used by the Capital Region.
One preliminary ﬁnding, she explains, is that a relevant part of the plastic waste that is produced in
the departments, is disposed of before it reaches the wash room, and thus, ends up in the general
waste fraction. Therefore, a part of an ideal solution would be to place the bins at the point of use
(e.g. in the patient rooms, or operating rooms etc.). However, she mentions that they too face spatial
challenges which makes it difﬁcult to ﬁnd room for the bins outside the wash rooms.
==PAGE:124==
A.3
REGION OF SOUTHERN DENMARK
111
When asked about whether or not it could be beneﬁcial for Region Zealand to adopt a more centralized
approach to waste management, both Lærke and Jesper Nielsen, one of the region’s Development
Consultants in the Center for Regional Development, meant that knowledge sharing across regions
was essential, however, it could also be important to take the local conditions of each hospital into
account when planning the waste management system. Here, Lærke mentioned an example of one
hospital in the region that had made an agreement with a local ﬂamingo production company, to
which they brought all of their ﬂamingo waste. She stated that it could be important to look for local
opportunities such as this one, and that a central approach may oversee such opportunities.
In
addition, she mentioned that it could be important to take into consideration the maturity of the system
with respect to views and values among employees regarding waste sorting, which may differ greatly
from hospital to hospital. She mentioned that although some of the regions were working on similar
pilot projects, that it was necessary for each region to make their own observations in order to improve
their maturity, and knowledge of their own system.
A.3
REGION OF SOUTHERN DENMARK
Participants:
Birgitte Bagge, Energy and Environmental Coordinator, Buildings Department,
Region of Southern Denmark
Marianne Mortensen, Project Leader at the Lillebelt Hospital Unit, Region of
Southern Denmark
Anders Krabbe Nielsen, Energy Consultant, Buildings Department, Region of
Southern Denmark
Christina
Rasmussen,
Energy
and
Environmental
Coordinator,
Hospital
Sønderjylland, Region of Southern Denmark
The Region of Southern Denmark is comprised of 22 municipalities, spans over the second largest
area and has a population density of about 100 inhabitants per km2. It manages thirteen hospitals
that are divided into four somatic hospital units, see Figure 23.
Currently, the region produces about 4,852 tonnes of general waste and has a recycling rate of about
26% (Region of Southern Denmark, 2019). According to (Region of Southern Denmark, 2018), their
recycling rate was about 20% in 2018. In their Climate Strategy 2020 - 2023, the Region of Southern
Denmark (2020) have communicated their desire to work towards transitioning to a more circular
economic system. They state that they aim to reduce their total amount of annual waste production
by 10% by 2030, and thus, aim for a reduction of 1% per year over the ten year period. In addition,
they state that they wish to increase their recycling rate to 45%, also by 2030. The region aim at
meeting the national/EU targets in terms of waste reduction. However, they have not published their
own targets.
The Region of Southern Denmark has a very decentralized approach to waste management. This
means that every hospital is largely responsible for managing their own waste. The hospitals are in
charge of their individual purchasing agreements, along with the fractions they sort and how they sort
them. This results in very different waste management practices between hospital, and even from
department to department. This was seen in Aalborg Hospital, where the fractions that were sorted
by each department differed greatly among the seven departments that were observed, even though
many of them produced similar types and quantities of waste (Sund, 2020). According to Region of
Southern Denmark (2018), the majority of sorting that happened at the department level at Aalborg
==PAGE:125==
A.3
REGION OF SOUTHERN DENMARK
112
Figure 23: Map of the municipalities in the Region of Southern Denmark, and hospital locations.
Note:The white outlines deﬁne the municipalities, and the red dots display the hospital locations.
Hospital, happened in the wash rooms of the individual departments. Although the hospitals are
grouped in units, hospitals within a unit typically also differ greatly in terms of their approach to waste
management.
Similar to the other regions, the hospitals in the Region of Southern Denmark experience a lack
of space, preventing them from implementing extensive sorting opportunities outside of their wash
rooms, throughout the departments. To combat this issue, Sund (2020) proposed the implementation
of three different types of waste trolleys, equipped with multiple smaller bins for different waste frac-
tions. Two of the bins were based on models already existing on the market. The third trolley was
designed such that it was equipped with an adjustable lattice, for the attachment of different sized con-
tainers for sorting. The lattice was adjustable such that it could be moved up and down, and locked in
place to suit the needs of the given situation. The Region of Southern Denmark are currently carrying
out a pilot project where they are testing the effects of implementing such a waste trolley on their
recycling performance at a number of different departments and hospitals in the region.
The region does have a central group that works with matters related to environment. This group
consists of at least one representative from each hospital unit, who meet roughly once quarterly.
Under this group are multiple ad hoc groups, who are responsible for managing different ad hoc
projects all related to environment. One of these ad hoc projects currently deals with creating a more
uniform waste management system across all of the hospitals in the Region of Southern Denmark.
The overall goal of the project is to increase the region’s recycling rate. In order to monitor their
progress, they have set recycling targets for 2022, 2025 and 2030.
The Region of Southern Denmark are also in the process of evaluating whether or not they should
attempt to make one primary purchasing agreement for their waste. However, factors such as distance
between hospitals, ﬂuctuations in waste prices between buyers, along with the technical barriers
==PAGE:126==
A.4
CENTRAL DENMARK REGION
113
evident from local recyclers have an impact on this decision. However, prices have been found to
ﬂuctuate largely between hospitals for the same waste fraction, from the same buyer. According
to Birgitte Bagger, these ﬂuctuations could not only have been based on geographical differences.
Thus, she believes that it would be a good idea to create a primary purchasing agreement, where an
average price could be negotiated for the entire region and they can achieve economies of scale.
Currently, each hospital is responsible for marking their own waste bins and providing their employees
with educational information and guides regarding sorting. This was also seen at Aalborg Hospital,
where the amount of effort put into the sorting schemes in each department, largely depended on
the motivation among the employees (Sund, 2020). Here, some departments showed a great amount
of motivation to investigate different types of plastic wastes, to educate students prior to internships
and to provide fellow employees with thorough sorting guides including smaller waste bins for sorting
outside of the departments wash room. In terms of replacing the current bin marking systems with
the Common Pictogram, Marianne Mortensen, who is working on implementing a more uniform waste
management system in the Lillebelt Hospital Unit, agrees with the comments from the Capital Region
regarding some of the missing fractions.
Overall, the representatives from the region seemed hesitant as to whether or not they would be able
to achieve a recycling rate that was high enough to meet the EU and national targets. Here they
pointed out that a lack of targets regarding procurement and production had an impact on their hesi-
tation. Christina Rasmussen from Sønderborg Hospital explained that she had on several occasions
witnessed that products were procured based on their favourable price, even though they contained
more equipment and/or medicine than necessary. Thus, these excess items were typically discarded,
and ended up in the general waste fraction. She explained that it in many cases was more expensive
for the hospital to require that the producer remove the excess items, than it was for them to buy them
and discard what they did not need.
A.4
CENTRAL DENMARK REGION
Participants:
Maria Gaden, Chief Manager, Center for Sustainable Hospitals, Central Denmark
Region
The Central Denmark Region has the second largest population among the ﬁve regions. In terms of
its geography, however, it is the largest region in Denmark. It’s population density is very similar to that
of the Region of Southern Denmark, with about 101 inhabitants per km2. The region is comprised of
19 municipalities, and manages ten different somatic hospitals, where some of the smaller hospitals
are grouped in units, see Figure 24
According to the region’s climate accounting’s report from 2019, the hospitals in the Central Denmark
Region currently produce just under 5000 tonnes of general waste annually. Their recycling rate
in 2019 was found to be 21%, which was an increase of about 2% from the year before (Central
Denmark Region, 2019). However, this increase was found mainly to be caused by the production
of an increased amount of construction waste from a building project in the region (Central Denmark
Region, 2019). In their Strategy for Sustainability 2030, the region sets forth three ambitious targets
relating to circular economy. Here, they state that they aim for a recycling rate of 70% by 2030, a
30% decrease in waste production and a 30% reduction in resource consumption (measured in CO2
emissions) (Central Denmark Region, 2021).
==PAGE:127==
A.4
CENTRAL DENMARK REGION
114
Figure 24: Map of the municipalities in the Central Denmark Region, and hospital locations.
Note:The white outlines deﬁne the municipalities, and the red dots display the hospital locations.
Recently the region established a Center for Sustainable Hospitals, which in danish translates to Cen-
ter for Bæredygtige Hospitaler (CfBH). The aim behind establishing this center, has been to intensify
their transition to more circular economic practices, in the region’s hospitals. In this regard, the region
has worked towards creating a more centralized structure in terms of working with sustainability. Maria
Gaden, who is the chief manager of the center, states that the hospitals in the region have previously
worked on individual ad hoc sustainability projects. However, she states that they were interested
in gaining a more centralized authority in terms of decision making, when dealing with sustainability
issues especially (e.g., to implement a common sorting strategy at all of the hospitals in the region).
According to Maria, it is absolutely necessary that some form of authority has the power to make de-
cisions on behalf of the region’s roughly 30,000 employees when dealing with circular economy. The
CfBH consists of 3 project managers and coordidates the sustainability representatives from each
hospital, that meet at least once every two weeks to discuss matters related to sustainability and
circular economy, and secure that the hospitals are working efﬁciently towards meeting their goals
(i.e., that they are not conducting identical pilot projects at multiple hospitals, and so on). In addition,
Maria states that the Central Denmark Region are very interested in facilitating a national network for
sustainable hospitals.
The Central Denmark Region has also implemented a sustainability steering group at the regional
level. This group consists mainly of directors from across the region, such as from the Regional
Development Department, from the individual hospitals and from the highest regional level. Under
the steering group, a technical working group has been established, which consists of members that
have a more hands on or technical approach to sustainability, such as logistics or service managers,
procurement managers and including Maria herself. Local sustainability steering groups have also
been implemented at the hospital level, to collaborate with the sustainability representatives, and with
CfBH, on implementing the initiatives which have been agreed upon by the regional steering group.
==PAGE:128==
A.5
NORTH DENMARK REGION
115
As mentioned, the Central Denmark Region is in the process of transitioning from decentralized ap-
proach to waste management and circular economy, to a centralized one. This is also seen in their
purchasing agreements, where each hospital has been responsible for their own, with respect to recy-
cling facilities. This has resulted in many different purchasing agreements across the region. However,
the region is currently working on investigating the extent of which they can merge these agreements,
and ideally arrive at one primary agreement on the regional level.
In the process of preparing the hospitals in the region for a more uniform sorting system, Maria
mentioned that they had agreed to gather their sorted plastic polymers at two of the hospitals in the
region, where they would prepare the fractions for their collection. The Central Denmark Region are
responsible for their own transport vehicles, which they use to distribute goods. In this regard, they
had planned to streamline their use of vehicles to collect plastic waste from the hospitals in connection
with their deliveries. In this regard, the region could minimize the extent of which the vehicles would
have to drive with an empty load. However, they were prevented in carrying out this new initiative,
due to a tightening of a national law regarding transport of empty containers that have previously
been used to store ﬂammable liquids. According to Maria, the law states that these items can not
be transported in an ordinary waste bag or transport cage. They must instead be transported in a
liquid-tight approved container containing an inner bag plus absorbent material. However, this is not
possible for the region, if they are to use their own transport vehicles, as it would require that they
purchase hundreds of liquid-tight containers. In addition, it is not possible for them to compress the
fraction due to a ﬁre hazard. Alternatively, they are now assessing whether or not they can install a
shredder at one of the region’s hospitals, so that they can shred the fraction instead, and transport it
as ﬂakes.
The Central Denmark Region has put a lot of effort into investigating the types of wastes that they
produce, especially their plastic fraction. According to Maria, there are only around 2 - 3 suppliers
of most of their procured products. For this reason, she believes that there is only a small variation
in the types of products that are found in Danish hospitals, making many of the observations made
regarding hospital waste very relevant on the national level. In this regard, she states that the region is
currently in the process of creating an online open platform for their sustainability projects and results.
The aim behind this platform is to create transparency, and give all of the hospitals in the region,
including all of the other regions, the opportunity to learn from both their insights and blind ways, and
to share knowledge both ways.
A.5
NORTH DENMARK REGION
Participants:
Torben Kronborg Pihl, Development Consultant, Center for SustainableDevelop-
ment, North Denmark Region
The North Denmark Region consists of 11 municipalities. It has the smallest population out of all
of the regions, and a population density of about 74 inhabitants per km2. It manages eight somatic
hospitals that are divided into two units, see Figure 25. In 2010, the region entered into an agreement
with the Danish Nature Conservation Association on being a Climate Region5. Annually, the region
produces around 36,500 tonnes of waste of which 94% is collected for incineration.
In terms of its waste management system, like many of the other regions, the North Denmark Region
5Climate Regions commit to reducing their annual CO2 emissions by 2% every year until 2025.
==PAGE:129==
A.5
NORTH DENMARK REGION
116
Figure 25: Map of the municipalities in the North Denmark Region, and hospital locations.
Note:The white outlines deﬁne the municipalities, and the red dots display the hospital locations.
has a very decentralized approach.
All of the hospitals are expected to sort around 30 different
fractions of waste. However, each individual hospital is responsible for managing how they wish to
sort those fractions, and for making their purchasing agreements. This means that the hospitals in the
region are very likely to follow different standards for sorting, and that slightly different fractions are
regarded as general waste from hospital to hospital, based on the requirements of their purchasers.
The region has been becoming increasingly aware of the challenges that they face in terms of ob-
taining valid data for their annual climate accounts, and how this also affects their abilities in ﬁnding
appropriate circular waste solutions. In this regard, they have conducted a large assessment in col-
laboration with NIRAS, on the existing waste streams in the region, which they hope will allow them to
prepare an ofﬁcial waste strategy for the region by the summer of 2021. In connection with preparing
the waste strategy, the region has established a regional waste group with representatives, which
among others consists of technical managers, procurers and external consultants (including a con-
sultant from the Capital Region). This group meets roughly every other month and is responsible for
preparing the waste strategy, and for facilitating the implementation of its initiatives.
The waste group has also discussed the possibility of looking into making one joint purchasing agree-
ment for the region. However, Torben Pihl who is a Development Consultant in the region’s Center
for Sustainable Development, explained that there are several factors that may prevent them from
making one primary agreement. These include the geography aspect in terms of the distance from
the hospitals to the purchasers, the potential local alternative opportunities that may exist, and the
varying maturity among the municipalities in terms of the treatment options that they offer. He states
that with the implementation of the national Circular Economy Strategy, the municipalities are facing
requirements in terms of harmonizing their practices, which was also discussed in Section 3.3.1 in
further detail. However, he expects that this may take several years, and says that the region will have
==PAGE:130==
A.5
NORTH DENMARK REGION
117
to wait and see, what opportunities they are faced with.
In terms of pilot projects, Torben mentions that the region is currently assessing the implementation of
alternatives to SUP-cutlery at one of their hospitals, and the production of a biogrinder at another, with
the intention of processing food waste for biogas generation. However, he states that the elimination
of SUP-cutlery will not make a prominent difference in their total plastic waste production. Therefore,
they will be looking into other alternative products on the market, that can substitute more of the SUP-
medical equipment that they use to treat patients with, as these constitute the largest fraction of their
plastic waste stream. In addition, he mentions that another hospital has assessed the climate effects
of single use hospital equipment (e.g. lab coats, linens, etc.) with more permanent options which
needed cleansing between each use. According to Torben, they found the single use equipment to
be the more favorable option, resulting in signiﬁcant CO2 savings.

Question 1: Hvad er titlen p˚a dit speciale?
Jeg har kun en arbejdstitel lige nu: Waste audit on Holbæk Hospital – an investigation in plastics

Question 2: Hvad er den totale mængde affald du har sorteret?
605,27 kg affald indsamlet over 9 dage
Question 3: Hvilke afdelinger var det, som du analyserede affald fra?
Sterilcentralen, Klinisk biokemisk afdeling og Nefrologisk afdeling.

• Bio: 12,6%
• Papir: 4,3%
• Plast: 12,2%
• Andet: 26,8%
• Ukendt: 43,6%
• Pap: 0,06%
• Metal: 0,02%
• Elektronik: 0,25%
Question 5: Vil du give mig en generel beskrivelse af fraktionerne især plastfraktionen (hvilke
typer du har fundet – ogs˚a dem som du vurderer er problematiske ift. at oparbejde)?
• Bio har best˚aet primært af papir h˚andklæder som jeg har vurderet til at være bionedbrydelig.
• Ukendt har best˚aet af lukkede poser med biologisk affald eller større mængde væske. Sen-
geafdellingen havde mange lukkede affaldsposer og grunden til at denne fraktion er s˚a stor
skyldes bl.a. fyldte bleer.
• Andet har været ”madposer”, hvilket er poser fra f.eks. frokost hvor diverse produkter som er
involveret i en frokost p˚a et sygehus. Affaldet har været s˚a indsmurt i madrester at jeg ikke
kunne sortere i de udvalgte fraktion og valgte derfor at det kom i ”Andet” fraktionen. Jeg har dog
norteret de enkelte produkter som jeg fandt, s˚a man foretage beregne p˚a ubrugte produkter til
at sammenligne mængderne.
==PAGE:132==
B.1
E-MAIL CORRESPONDENCE LUCAS ABSALON
119
• Papir best˚ar af A4 papir og en del kasser som er for store til at komme igennem markuler-
ingsslisken som sidder p˚a de nuværende papir skraldespande.
• Plast har best˚aet af primæst blød plast, heriblandt poser, palle wrap, sm˚a lommer og plast/papir
produkter.
Fra min polymeranalyse kan jeg se at mange af produkter er lavet af ﬂere typer plast som er problema-
tisk af genanvende. En stor del af papir/plast produkterne er lavet af forskellige typer plast. Derudover
er de ﬂeste produkter ikke mærket med plast typen hvilket gøre det svært for forbrugeren at sortere
i ﬂere fraktioner. Til sidst, s˚a er der stor forskel p˚a genanvenderens ﬂeksibilitet n˚ar det kommer til
kontamineret plast eller bearbejdning af forskellige typer af plast.
Question 6: Vil du give mig en generel beskrivelse af din metode?
Jeg har indsamlet affald fra tre afdelinger, i tre dage, i tre uger. De tre dage er valgt fordi det svare til
den mænge renovationsvirksomheden modtager. Det har udmundet i 167 sorte skraldesække som
jeg har sorteret i 12 fraktioner, heriblandt plast. Jeg har brugt den nuværende sorteringsguideline
p˚a sygehusene til at sortere, s˚a jeg har prøvet at sætte mig i personalets sted og vurderet hvorvidt
”produktet” var f.eks. papir, pap eller plast. Der er enkelte fraktioner som ikke bliver sorteret p˚a
sygehusene som jeg har valgt at inkludere. De fraktioner er: Bio og plast, som er fraktioner som
de kommer til at sorterer i p˚a sigt. Til sortering i Bio har jeg brugt den guideline som bliver brugt i
Københavns kommune, jeg antager at den er sammenlignlig med guidlines for andre kommuner.
Til sortering i plast valgte jeg kun at vælge produkter som var ”rene” plast produkter, alts˚a produkter
som ikke indeholdte medicin rester, metal, organiske rester osv. Alts˚a kun produkter som var lavet af
ren plast og ikke kontamineret.
Question 7: Har du noteret nogle begrænsninger ved dit studie? F.eks. nogle faktorer som
har kunne p˚avirke dit resultat?
Der er ﬂere begrænsinger og usikkerheder i min analyse, jeg kan nok ikke g˚a igennem dem alle
sammen. I forhold til begræsninger s˚a har de lukkede affaldsposer fra Nefrologisk været den største
begrænsning. Det skal dog siges, at jeg ikke vurdere at en sortering af de poser ville ændre det
overordnede resultat betydeligt. Det bygger jeg p˚a at posernes vægt primært kommer fra fyldte bleer,
IV-udstyr, blod osv.
Den største usikkerhed ved min analyse er fejlsortering. Jeg har brugt den nuværende sorterings-
guide som pejlemærke, men guiden giver primært en overordnet beskrivelse af produkter og det kan
være svært at vurdere hvad et produkt er lavet af, hvad det var før det blev til affald og om det kan
genanvendes.
Question 8: I din ”anden” fraktion, var det primært produkter som var smurt ind i madrester,
eller har jeg misforst˚aet din forklaring? Og hvilke slags produkter var det du fandt?
Vægten fra ”anden” er i min vurdering primært produkter med madrester. Men fraktion er tænkt
som restaffald og produkter som engangshandsker, masker, v˚adservietter, tandbørster osv. Er ogs˚a
kommet i den fraktion. Ud over produkter med madrester, s˚a er engangshandsker sandsynligvis den
største bidrager til vægten.
Question 9: Ud fra de svar du har sendt mig, ser det ud som om, at du ikke har sorteret glas.

B.2
INTERVIEW WITH SUSANNE BACKER
121
B.2
INTERVIEW WITH SUSANNE BACKER
Participants:
Susanne Backer, Circular Economy Consultant, former Project Manager of the ’Mat-
erialestrømsprojekt1 project’ and author of www.GENTÆNK PLAST.dk, Central Den-
mark Region
1Direct English translation: ”Material Flow Project”
Susanne Backer conducted a judgemental sample waste audit in 2016, in connection with the GEN-
TÆNK PLAST project, where she and her team from from COWI and the clinical staff at AUH, as-
sessed the clean plastic waste fraction of 500kg general waste, from nine different departments (by
clean, she meant items ”that a nurse regards as being visibly clean”). Of this amount, 90kg clean
plastic waste was removed and assessed. The waste audit was conducted based on a similar, much
larger study, that was conducted by Stanford. She stated that despite it being an expensive project
to carry out, it could not ’stand alone’, due to the small sample size, that was assessed. However,
she explained that the results they found were very similar to those found by Stanford, and also to a
similar small project carried out by a hospital in the Netherlands.
According to Susanne, the dirty portion of plastic waste was not assessed, due to the vast amount of
contaminants, ranging from body ﬂuids, to medicinal remains. Thus, in order to prevent hospital staff
from exposing themselves to potentially dangerous or infections liquids, the items were not separated
and sorted for recycling. She stated that another factor that one must take into consideration, is the
cost associated to increased sorting. If too much time is spent among hospital staff on sorting waste,
it will be very expensive for the hospital. In addition, she explained that one of their main goals, has
been to ﬁnd local recyclers for their plastic waste, so as to avoid exporting their waste outside of
Denmark. She explained that it is very difﬁcult to be sure of what actually happens to the waste, once
it has been exported. Here, she mentioned Germany as an example (as much Danish plastic waste is
exported to Germany), where she refers to them as one of the largest exporters of plastic packaging
waste. She explained that Germany too, cannot know for sure, what happens to the waste once it
has been exported. In addition, she mentioned that there previously have been German regulations
preventing the import of ”dirty” plastic waste from hospitals. However, she was unsure of whether this
still was the case.
Nevertheless, in terms of handling the waste locally, she explained that a lot has changed over the
recent years, and that recyclers are gaining more and more interest in purchasing their plastic fraction.
However, she says that it is important to also consider, what is safe for the recyclers to handle. She
explained that it is possible to receive sorted plastic fractions of very high quality when dealing with
medical appliances. And if hospital staff is to spend time on sorting the plastic into these high quality
fractions, that they also expect to receive a price that can make up for this time spent. In addition,
she stated that it is important for them to know that the plastic is fed into a cycle that can maintain the
quality of the plastic to as high a degree as possible, in order to prevent cascading and downgrading
of their plastics. According to Susanne, the Central Denmark Region is currently working on using
their recycled plastics in playground items. She explained that if they can get their plastic accepted as
being applicable in such items, that it will provide a good indication of the high quality of their plastic.
On the European scale, Susanne explained that plastic is of much higher value, due to better infras-
tructure and recycling technologies, compared to what is currently available nationally, in Denmark.
Another factor that prevents them from making beneﬁcial purchasing agreements, is the small size of
their sorted fractions, due to a very fractured waste management system. Currently, every hospital
==PAGE:135==
B.2
INTERVIEW WITH SUSANNE BACKER
122
is responsible for its own purchasing agreements, however, they are thus, in the process of looking
to make more regional agreements. She stated that even though they make a regional agreement,
their fractions will still be quite small, and that it would be beneﬁcial to look into making more national
agreements.
On the topic of national agreements, Susanne explained that an increased level of centralization would
also prevent multiple regions from conducting similar research. Based on the ﬁndings of their waste
audit being largely similar to those from Stanford and the Netherlands, she anticipates that they will
be largely similar among all hospitals in Western countries. She explained that it would contribute to
an increased level of resource efﬁciency, to increase the level of national centralization in terms of
waste management, both in terms of minimizing confusion regarding sorting schemes, and ﬁnancially
regarding the total amounts of waste they produce.
When asked about the lack of PVC in their waste audit results, she explained that PVC is generally
not used in packaging, but instead in IV systems for example. However, these IV systems are not
landﬁlled, but sent to incineration instead, which in fact goes against national regulations. She ex-
plained that they are currently trying to deﬁne a set of requirements, along with the other regions and
Nordic countries, in terms of phasing out the use of PVC in all medical packaging. She also stated the
importance of thinking about waste management during the design process, and to create systems
that can be easily separated from one another, and sorted correctly. However, she was still unsure of
whether or not the IV systems would become recyclable, even if they hypothetically, no longer were
composed of PVC. She explained that the Central Denmark Region has discussed the matter with
Plastics Recyclers Europe, who have stated that they do not believe in recycling materials that have
been in contact with cytostatics, opiates or antibiotics.
When asked about whether or not there is a lack of recycling, and sorting technologies available for
hospital plastic waste, she explained that this is the case in Denmark, but at the international level
e.g. in Holland, Belgium and France, all markets served by Suez, there are better opportunities. In
this regard, she explained that there are international sorting and recycling options, that they would
be interested in making use of, such as SUEZ and Quality Circular Polymers. However, that the
low costs associated with incinerating waste in Denmark, prevent Suez from setting up their services
in Denmark. In addition, she stated that along with the low costs of incineration, lack of technical
knowledge, insufﬁcient technology and investment in the technical infrastructure in Denmark, and
also currently is no producer-responsibility scheme.
In terms of employee motivation regarding sorting waste, she stated that it absolutely is not an issue
at AUH. She explained that they have worked with circular economy and waste sorting for some years,
and that the staff take increased waste sorting very seriously.

B.4
SUMMARY OF THE COLLECTED DATA
B.4.1
HOSPITAL SØNDERJYLLAND
Sund (2020) conducted a waste audit, assessing the composition of ≈230 kg general waste from Hos-
pital Sønderjylland (which in Danish translates to ’Sygehus Sønderjylland’), in the Region of Southern
Denmark. Here general waste was collected from eight different departments, over the course of
four days, where the waste was collected over the course of 24 hours from each department. The
departments assessed in the audit include a kitchen / cafeteria, clinical biochemistry laboratory, an
operating department, cardiac outpatient clinic, department for medical diseases, department of or-
thopedic surgery and a department for day surgeries.
During the audit, waste bags were opened, and seven fractions were sorted out of the waste and
weighed. The fractions were selected based on the current recycling technologies available nationally,
but also internationally. For example, among the sorted fractions were beverage cartons. Currently
there are no recycling facilities that treat these in Denmark, however, several neighboring countries,
such as Sweden and Germany, do offer such treatment. In addition, both dirty and clean plastics were
sorted, even though previous knowledge had not been obtained regarding their actual recyclability.
An overview of the sorted material fractions has been provided in Table 8.
Hospital Sønderjylland encompasses three different hospitals; the emergency hospital in Aabenraa,
the specialist hospital in Sønderborg and the day hospital in Tønder.
The hospitals in Aabenraa
and Sønderborg were designated as pilot hospitals for the ﬁeld study, by the project leaders from
the Region of Southern Denmark, and have 270 and 79 hospital beds, respectively (which sums up
to a total of 349 beds) (Southern Jutland Hospital, 2021). The analysis was carried out for seven
departments at Aabenraa Hospital, and for one department at Sønderborg Hospital. The assessment
of the waste was carried out in collaboration with DTU, COWI A/S, Hospital Sønderjylland and the
Region of Southern Denmark. COWI A/S had been hired by the Region of Southern Denmark to
perform the analysis in October 2020. The results from the analysis have, thus, been gathered during
the COVID-19 pandemic.
Among the sorted fractions presented in Table 8, the total weight measured regarding bio waste,
ﬁbers, plastic, metal, glass, EPS and beverage cartons was considered as the hospital’s ’recycling
potential’. Out of the total amount of waste assessed, Sund (2020) measured an average recycling
potential of ≈27%. Of this total potential, the individual contributions of each fraction have been
presented in Figure 26.
As can be seen, the two fractions with the largest potential were plastic (≈12.1% of the total) and bio
waste (≈10.2%). As plastic is typically much lighter than food waste, a greater volumetric difference
between the two fractions is to be expected. The individual types of plastics were not investigated
further. However, according to Sund (2020), the main types of products that dominated this fraction
were surgical instrument baskets, intravenous (IV) bags (including tubing and additional parts), and
peel pouch / packs (otherwise known as sterilization pouches). According to Backer (2016), peel
pouches are bags which can be separated into two pieces, and consist of either two plastic parts
(alike or different), or a plastic part and a paper part (they are typically glued together, but can also
be welded).
In terms of bio waste, the larger part of the fraction was composed of food waste. Much of the food
was found inside compostable packaging (which were composed of a compostable food tray and
plastic lid), or inside beverage cartons. In such cases, the lids were removed from the packaging and
==PAGE:138==
B.4
SUMMARY OF THE COLLECTED DATA
125
Figure 26: The results from the waste audit conducted by Sund (2020), showing the composi-
tion of the general waste assessed from Hospital Sønderjylland.
Source: The ﬁgure has been created for the purpose of this thesis.
included in the plastic fraction, and the food was removed from the beverage cartons.
Paper and cardboard were sorted together, but separately from beverage cartons. A potential of
≈2.8% and ≈1.2% was measured of the total amount of measured waste, respectively. Products
dominating the ﬁber fraction were different types of cardboard packaging (e.g., for latex gloves).
Among the smaller fractions were glass, metal and polystyrene. The glass fraction was typically
composed of laboratory ﬂasks, medicine jars, and jam jars. Many medicine jars were attached to IV
systems. However, these were not detached during the audit, and were instead placed in the plastic
waste fraction. In terms of metal, the main products found were beverage cans. Polystyrene was the
material fraction with the smallest weight-based potential. However, quite a bit of polystyrene was
found considering its volume, in the form of packaging and packaging chips.
Finally, ≈73% of the measured waste was considered non-recyclable, and thus, regarded as general
waste. This fraction was mainly composed of latex gloves, face masks, diaper-like materials and
surgical linens and gowns.
B.4.2
HOLBÆK HOSPITAL
Absalon (2021) conducted a waste audit, assessing the composition of ≈600 kg of general waste
from Holbæk Hospital, in Region Zealand. The waste was collected from three different departments,
over the course of nine days (spanning over three weeks). The departments assessed in the audit
include a clinical biochemistry laboratory, central sterile services department and a nephrological
department. The fractions were sorted based on the common sorting instructions provided by Region
Zealand. The common sorting guidelines are placed in all wash rooms in the Region’s hospitals, to
aid the staff in sorting correctly. Thus, the items that were sorted by Absalon (2021), were items that
had been incorrectly sorted as general waste. The same fractions were sorted as the ones sorted by
Sund (2020) (see Table 8), with the exception of the following dissimilarities:
• electronic equipment was sorted;
• EPS was not sorted;
• glass was disregarded due to the minimal size of the fraction;
==PAGE:139==
B.4
SUMMARY OF THE COLLECTED DATA
126
• food waste was collected separately from bio waste, in an ’other’ fraction, also containing
products that were covered in food left overs to an inseparable degree;
• only clean plastic was sorted in the plastic fraction, i.e., products that did not contain
medicine residues, metal, organic residues, etc., thus to form a fraction that consisted
of pure plastic with little to no contamination; and
• cardboard had been collected separately from paper waste.
For the purpose of comparison, the bio waste and other fraction were merged, forming one bio waste
fraction. In addition, the paper and cardboard fractions were merged, forming one ﬁber fraction.
Holbæk Hospital has a total of 344 hospital beds (Region Sjælland, 2019). The assessment was
made in connection with a master’s thesis, and carried out in collaboration with Roskilde University
(RUC), Gate21, Holbæk Hospital and Region Zealand. The master thesis was written in the spring of
2021, and thus, results from the analysis have also been gathered during the COVID-19 pandemic.
Among the sorted fractions, the total weight measured regarding bio waste, ﬁbers, plastic, metal and
electronics was considered as the hospital’s recycling potential. Out of the total amount of waste
assessed, Absalon (2021) measured an average recycling potential of ≈44%. Of this total potential,
the individual material contributions found for each fraction have been presented in Figure 27.
Figure 27: The results from the waste audit conducted by Absalon (2021), showing the
composition of the general waste assessed from Holbæk Hospital.
Source: The ﬁgure is adapted from the one provided by Lucas Absalon, in Appendix B.1.
As can be seen, the largest fraction is the bio waste, with a potential of ≈39.6% of the total waste
assessed. According to Absalon (2021), the bio waste fraction was composed of paper towels, which
he deemed to be biodegradable.
As mentioned above, it was also composed of food and other
products. Originally, the bio waste and food waste fractions had measured potentials of ≈12.7% and
≈27.0% of the total measured waste, respectively. Moreover, according to Absalon (2021), merely
around 25%-33% of the food waste fraction was actually composed of food waste. However, this was
not taken into account in the ﬁgure.
The second largest fraction was the plastic fraction, with a potential of ≈12.3% of the total waste
measured. It consisted primarily of soft plastic, such as bags, peel pouches and pallet wrap. Absalon
(2021) also conducted a polymer analysis of different products found in the plastic fraction. From
the analysis, he observed that several types of plastics were problematic in terms of recycling. This
==PAGE:140==
B.4
SUMMARY OF THE COLLECTED DATA
127
was due to a large portion of the peel pouch waste being made of both paper and plastic, with many
different types of plastic. In addition, he mentioned that most of the products were not labelled in
terms of each of their speciﬁc plastic type, which would make it difﬁcult for the consumer to sort it
correctly.
The ﬁber fraction was primarily composed of A4 paper and boxes that were too large to be passed
through the chute on the paper bins. Originally, the paper and cardboard fractions had potentials of
≈4.3% and ≈0.04% of the total waste measured, respectively.
Finally, ≈43.5% of the measured waste was considered non-recyclable, and thus, as appropriately
sorted general waste. This fraction was primarily composed of the same types of materials as those
described by Sund (2020).
B.4.3
AARHUS UNIVERSITY HOSPITAL
Backer (2016) conducted a judgemental sample waste audit in 2016. Here, she and her team from
COWI and the clinical staff at AUH, assessed ≈500 kg of plastic waste disposed at AUH, in the Central
Denmark Region. The general waste was collected from nine different departments, over 48 hours for
each department. Only clean plastic waste was to be assessed in the audit, and thus, all used plastic
was disregarded. A total of ≈90 kg of clean plastic waste was found and assessed. The purpose of
the audit was to identify the types of polymers that were present in their clean plastic waste fraction,
including the amounts of each type.
The hospital has approximately 856 hospital beds (Central Denmark Region, 2020), making it the
largest hospital among the three studies. The waste audit was carried out in 2016, and thus, results
from the analysis were gathered prior to the COVID-19 pandemic. The methodology of the waste
audit was based on a similar survey conducted by Stanford (2013). The waste audit serves as part
of a larger project, as described in Section 4.2.5.4. The audit and the data obtained has, thus, been
described on the Central Denmark Region’s online platform, Gentænk Plast (2020), along with other
studies conducted in connection with the project.
Out of the 90 kg of clean plastic waste that was collected, Backer (2016) observed a number of
different types of plastic. Thus, she separated them into 17 different fractions, which will be described
below. The waste mainly consisted of plastic packaging waste, as equipment is typically used before it
is disposed of. According to Susanne, products that have passed their shelf life, are typically donated
to third world countries, and therefore, do not end up in the general waste. The results from the audit
have been illustrated in Figure 28, as a percentage of the 90 kg of assessed clean plastic waste.
As can be seen, almost all of the main types of thermoplastics were observed (PET, HDPE, LDPE, PP
and PS), with the exception of PVC. However, according to Susanne, PVC is found in many different
types of hospital equipment. Nevertheless, the assessed fraction was mainly composed of plastic
packaging waste, and not the equipment itself, as the equipment is typically regarded as dirty once it
has been used. And as the audit only assessed clean plastic, no PVC was observed.
Although all of the plastics that were collected in the waste audit were clean, they were not necessarily
recyclable. As mentioned in Section 3.1, different factors have an impact on whether or not a material
is recyclable. These include the environmental impacts associated to recycling a given material (e.g.,
whether the impacts of recycling the material are larger than the impacts associated to alternative
treatment options), and whether it is ﬁnancially feasible (e.g., whether the costs associated to recy-
cling are higher than alternative treatment options, and whether there is a demand for the recycled
==PAGE:141==
B.4
SUMMARY OF THE COLLECTED DATA
128
material) (Sørensen et al., 2019). In addition, some plastics are more difﬁcult to recycle than others,
based on their content of additives (i.e., substances that are added to the plastic to give it the desired
properties and appearance) (Dakofa, 2018).
Figure 28: The results from the waste audit conducted by Backer (2016), showing the com-
position of the plastic waste found over a 48 hour period at AUH.
Source: The ﬁgure has been adapted from Backer (2016).
Out of the total 90 kg of plastic assessed, ≈55% was regarded as recyclable. Of this amount, the vast
majority was ﬂexible plastics (≈88%), whereas foils only accounted for ≈3% and rigid plastic of ≈9%.
In Table 10, a list of all of the plastics assessed is presented, together with the the main items they
were found in, and their current recyclability according to Gitte from Aage Vestergaard Larsen A/S.
The two largest fractions observed include ’uknown plastics’ (≈39.6%) and LDPE (≈26.6%). The
unknown plastics fraction was composed of many different items such as gloves, syringes, miscel-
laneous tubes and hoses, form pressed plastics, lids and other small items. However, according to
Susanne, their composition was not possible to identify, even by experts. A laboratory assessment
of a random sample of the fraction, proved that it consisted mainly of laminates, that were made of
composite materials (e.g., different types of plastics or metalized plastics). Laminates are difﬁcult to
recycle as they consists of multiple layers of different materials which are demanding to separate.
The LDPE fraction, on the other hand, mainly consisted of transport ﬁlm around pallets and as the
plastic layer in much of the sterile packaging or peel pouch packaging. This fraction was for the most
part recyclable. According to Susanne, the transport plastic around pallets is relatively easy to collect,
and was already being collected at AUH to some extent, at the time of the waste audit. The packaging
on the other hand, is not always easy to collect. This is because, not all of the peel pouch packaging
was made of LDPE. In fact, much of the entire plastic fraction that was assessed, was in the form of
==PAGE:142==
B.4
SUMMARY OF THE COLLECTED DATA
129
peel pouch packaging. The peel pouch packaging was observed with both two plastic parts of the
same type of plastic, of multiple plastics, and of one side paper and one plastic. They were found to be
made of either HDPE or LDPE, a combination of PE and either PET or PA, of ethylene vinyl copolymer
(EVA), or of tyvek, which is a synthetic material made from spunbound HDPE ﬁbers. This means that
the staff must ﬁrst identify the type of material that the given packaging is composed of, where after
they must separate the individual parts from each other entirely, to ﬁnally be able to sort them in the
appropriate fraction. Some of the plastic combinations were not regarded as recyclable, resulting
in a contamination of the given waste stream, if the staff mistakenly misidentiﬁes a given packaging
material. In addition, the packaging materials are often not labelled with a resin identiﬁcation code,
making it difﬁcult for the staff to determine the type of materials it is composed of in the ﬁrst place.
The packaging is typically made so that it is easy for staff to quickly rip open, and remove the content.
However, it is typically only necessary to partially open them, before the content can be removed.
Thus, the vast majority of the packaging material that is disposed of, has not been fully separated.
This results in a large contamination of paper labels. In addition, there is often glue holding the
packaging parts together. The type of glue used can differ from item to item, which also makes it
difﬁcult for recycling facilities to treat the given fraction.

C.4
REPORT: Comparative Life Cycle Assessment of EOL treatment-and disposal
scenarios for aseptic cartons in Denmark
In this section, a list of assumptions regarding the recycling process for beverage cartons has been
presented. For more information see the attached ﬁle "Tetrapak_LCA.pdf".
List of assumptions:
• There are no losses of material from the waste stream.
• The fraction of paper and carton containers is sent for recycling, and the PE and aluminium
fraction is utilized in cement production.
==PAGE:150==
C.4
REPORT: Comparative Life Cycle Assessment of EOL treatment-and disposal scenarios for
aseptic cartons in Denmark
137
• cement production is based on a generic ecoinvent process from Portland, with a composition of
6-20% ﬂy ash (based on data obtained from Cementa’s production, which is a Swedish cement
production company).
• Emissions and consumption of electricity, heat and water are based on Fiskeby’s Environmental
Declaration.
• Dust emissions from Fiskeby’s Environmental Declaration are particulates ¡10um.
• 20% of the total electricity consumption and 80% of the total heat consumption is used for the
manufacture of pulp. The rest is used for the manufacture of cartonboard.
• Wastewater treatment efﬁciencies include 94.5% for COD, 84.9% for nitrogen, 67.3% for phos-
phorus, 83.4% for zinc, and 0% for TOC (as no data was available). These were based on
values obtained through a generic EASETECH wastewater treatment process.
• There is a 90% market response in terms of the substituted cardboard, and a 100% market
response in terms of the substituted cement aggregates.
Limitations:
• A major limitation is that the production of cement, is a database process, found on the ecoinvent
database.
• Generic EASETECH processes include the paper to cardboard , 2006 [with substitution]; the
utilization for neutralization of acid waste in Norway, 2006; and the MSWI BA aggregate in Road
100% leaching emissions to soil.
• The removal efﬁciencies for the water treatment were based on values obtained through a
generic EASETECH process.
• The distribution of electricity and heat consumption, and emissions between the pulping and
manufacture of cartonboard is unknown, as total values were obtained from Fiskeby.

D
BARRIERS
D.1
INTERVIEW REGARDING THE BARRIERS
Participants:
Thomas Dejbjerg Pedersen, Environmental Coordinator at Aarhus University Hospi-
tal (AUH), Central Denmark Region
Thomas Dejbjerg Pedersen conﬁrms that the Central Denmark Region are working towards an in-
creased level of centralization in terms of their organizational structure. In this regard, hospitals in the
region are communicating with each other more and more, and every hospital has an Environmental
Consultant or Environmental Coordinator. In addition, AUH are working on appointing department-
level Environmental Representatives. He explains that the Environmental Coordinators and Consul-
tants at the different hospitals have very different backgrounds. Some have clinical backgrounds and
know a lot about the daily practice, technical terms, and hygiene and safety guidelines, whereas he
has a chemical engineering background, and has experience working with more environmental mat-
ters. He explains that the insights regarding hygiene are very important, as hygiene is almost always
the top priority in all of their working areas. The Environmental Representatives on the department
level will typically be already existing employees, working in the given department, who will help to
make sure that sorting is being done correctly, that all the necessary bins and sorting instructions are
in place, and will serve as the link between the the Environmental Coordinators and Consultants and
their department.
Although he states that an increased level of centralization regarding their waste management, could
be beneﬁcial in several ways, he explains that it also is very important to take into consideration the
local conditions of each hospital, and department even. He explains that even just within AUH, many
of the departments have different ways of managing their waste, and that the bigger the hospital, the
more difﬁcult it is to make sure all departments manage their waste in the same manner. This can
be due to many different reasons. Thus, in his opinion it will be difﬁcult to create one general sorting
guideline, that will be applicable to all hospitals and departments. However, he guesses that around
75% - 90% will be the same, with small deviations. According to him, two areas where it makes a
lot of sense to increase centralization, is within procurement and in terms of purchasing agreements.
Here he states that the Central Denmark Region is already investigating the degree of which it will
be possible for them to merge their purchasing agreements. In addition, he conﬁrms that they are
currently working together with several of the other Nordic countries on putting forward a common set
of procurement requirements.
Thomas explains, that they have run into some issues in terms of collecting their plastic fractions. This
is because, a large portion of their plastic waste is represented by containers containing remains of
different substances such as spirits or cleaning detergents etc., and new regulations do not allow them
to transport these due to concerns regarding safety. In addition, safety regulations also prevent them
from compressing them, due to a ﬁre hazard. This makes it difﬁcult for them to merge their purchasing
agreements regarding plastics for example. Currently, they mainly collect PP and HDPE plastics in
their clinical departments, smaller amounts of PET from their surgery departments, and LDPE is
collected in their goods reception area and technical departments. The PP and HDPE plastic that
they currently, are only collected from nine or ten different departments. This is because, they are
currently investigating how well the collection works among the different departments. The fractions
collected are mainly composed of different types of containers. Peel pouches are not collected, as
==PAGE:152==
D.1
INTERVIEW REGARDING THE BARRIERS
139
they need to be separated completely in order to be sorted correctly. The excessive amounts of peel
pouches disposed of in one day, would make it very difﬁcult for staff to separate each and every
one, both in terms of avoiding injuries due to repetitive movements, inhaling dust (released when
separating some types of packagings), and the amount of time that it would take.
When asked about the list of barriers that have been presented within this report, Thomas provided
his views each. These will be presented in the following:
Barrier: Lack of EU and national targets regarding production and procurement
Thomas agreed that there were many targets that focused on increasing recycling rates and so on,
but not as many that focused on reducing the waste production in the ﬁrst place, through design for
example. He explains that this makes it very difﬁcult for them to make requirements to suppliers, as
there is no regulation supporting their requests. This requires for the hospitals to engage in collabo-
rations across hospitals and countries even so they then can put a noticeable amount of pressure on
the suppliers. As an individual hospital, they are not able to put the necessary amount of pressure on
large suppliers, for them to listen to their requests.
Barrier: Lack of restrictions on amount and type of polymers contained in SUPs
Thomas deﬁnitely sees this as a barrier, however, he does not expect that any international or national
requirements will be set forth regarding medical equipment, as the main priority is to secure patient
and worker safety. For example, there are many products containing PVC that you cannot manu-
facture in a different way with current technologies. In addition, they produce several smaller plastic
waste fractions, which typically are composed of different types of polymers, which simply does not
make sense for them to sort and recycle from an economic perspective.
Barrier: Insufﬁcient quantities of recyclable fractions due to a fragmented market
At the moment, Thomas explains that the fractured market poses a big barrier for them, as it prevents
them from making purchasing agreements. This is because they cannot fulﬁll the requirements of the
recycling facilities, in terms of sufﬁcient volumes, and a stable waste production.
Barrier: Lack of motivation among employees
Thomas explains that the level of motivation is very different from department to department. Some
factors that can demotivate employees include the difﬁculty it sometimes is to discern what material
the given item is made of, and thus, how to sort it. In addition, he explains that some employees
become frustrated regarding the large amount of plastic that is disposed of as general waste com-
pared to other smaller fractions which are recycled, which makes them feel that their efforts do not
make the biggest difference. Nevertheless, he does think that a few things could be done to motivate
employees. These include to increase accounting of progress, benchmarks, and delegate an annual
title to the most sustainable hospital (or the employee that has worked the hardest on sustainability
related tasks), so as to increase the level of competition among hospitals in reaching their targets.
He also feels that the management level should be held accountable on an annual basis, in terms of
whether or not they are meeting the given targets.
Barrier: Difﬁcult to discern how to sort different products
He agrees that this is deﬁnitely a barrier. The types of plastic for example, that they expect their staff
to recycle are all marked with its respective resin identiﬁcation code. He explains that they do not
require their staff to try to discern which type of plastic the given item is made of prior to its disposal,
==PAGE:153==
D.1
INTERVIEW REGARDING THE BARRIERS
140
if it is not stated. He hopes that the suppliers will begin to mark all of their products and packaging
materials in the coming years.
Barrier: Hospital waste is regarded as ’infectious’ and not appropriate for recycling
According to Thomas, he believes that this is becoming less and less of a barrier, as more recyclers
are gradually becoming increasingly open to recycling their waste fractions. Especially if the volume
of their fractions begin to increase, as the market becomes less fractured. He explains that many of
the materials that are used in their products are of some of the highest qualities, as they must ensure
sterility and patient and employee safety and so on. Thus, their products typically don’t contain many
additives. However, other factors such as labels or different types of glue used in different products,
can make it very difﬁcult for recyclers to recycle the material fractions whilst maintaining as high a
quality as possible.
Barrier: Contaminated waste streams
On the topic of contaminated waste streams, blood and other body ﬂuids also cause for contaminated
waste streams. However, unlike the labels and glue, which make it difﬁcult for recyclers to produce
high quality recycled material, items that have had patient contact create a different type of difﬁculty.
These items pose for health hazards among the employees who are required to handle them, both
internally (e.g., within the hospitals) and externally (e.g., within the sorting and recycling facilities).
Barrier: Sometimes health care tasks demand a shift in priorities
He agrees that this is deﬁnitely a barrier, however, it is not something that he imagines can be avoided.
Barrier: Lack of space for sorting
He deﬁnitely feels that the lack of space poses as a barrier, also in their newer buildings. Here they are
looking to increase the amount of vertical sorting happening in their departments. However, he feels
that some work is also needed in terms of re-organizing many of the hospital’s wash rooms as this
would clear up some amount of space. However, he mentions that along with lack of space around
the departments, hygiene is deﬁnitely also a barrier. For example, standard waste containers are not
allowed in the operating rooms due to reasons related to hygiene. He mentions that one area where it
would make sense to increase sorting, would be in the preparation rooms (where all of the equipment
needed for a given surgery are prepared). However, he explains that these rooms deﬁnitely have
a lack of space for sorting. In this regard, he does see the alternative waste trolleys proposed by
Sund (2020), as a possible method of minimizing this barrier. However, he explains that the costs
associated to substituting all of their current bins, with new trolleys would be very high. Currently, they
are in the process of implementing weights in their bins, so as to monitor the amounts of waste that
are produced by each department, so as to better design their waste management practices.
Barrier: Lack of ﬁnancial incentives for sorting some materials
He does not believe that waste management will ever provide a good business case for hospitals, due
to the amount of time it takes, and the equipment needed and so on. Although they do receive money
for many of their fractions, most of this is spent on internal waste management related activities, or
other fractions which they may need to pay to have picked up. In addition, he mentions that it would be
much easier for the hospitals to be able to sort all plastics together, for example, if sorting or recycling
facilities offered to sort and prepare the plastics for recycling. However, this is currently not possible,
if the purpose is to maintain a high quality material. He explains that if the market becomes more
harmonized, and hospitals are able to secure larger and more stable fractions, recycling facilities may
==PAGE:154==
D.2
BARRIER SURVEY
141
be able to invest in improved technology and research, so that centralized sorting may become a
realistic option. However, one factor that also contributes to this barrier, is that virgin plastic often is
cheaper than recycled plastic.

hvor:
• 1 indikerer at dette slet ikke udgør en barriere for Regionen
• 2 indikerer at dette delvist udgør en barriere for Regionen
• 3 indikerer at dette i høj grad er en barriere for Regionen

dem alle, men jeg vil sætte pris p˚a, hvis du gør det de steder hvor du ikke mener at der er tale om en
barriere.

Evt. kommentar:
Barriere 8: Sygehusaffald anses som værende smitsomt og ikke egnet til genanvendelse
Beskrivelse af barrieren: Genanvendere eller sorteringsvirksomheder vil ikke tage imod affaldet da
det har været i patientkontakt, selvom det m˚aske er lavet af højkvalitetsmateriale.

Barriere 9: Kontamineret affaldsstrømme
Beskrivelse af barrieren: Affaldsstrømme kan være kontamineret af mange forskellige ˚arsager. Dette
kan f.eks. være af blod eller andre kropsvæsker, men ogs˚a af forskellige slags lim brugt til at sammen-
sætte emballager, papir labels m.m. Det kan være svært for personale at undg˚a disse kontaminer-
inger.

Barriere 10: Affaldssortering kan ikke altid være den højeste prioritet
Beskrivelse af barrieren: Sygehuspersonale redder liv, og det er dermed ikke altid muligt at prioriterer
affaldssortering som det højeste. I et tilfælde af en akutoperation f.eks. vil det ikke være muligt
bruge tid p˚a at sortere. Derudover, er det ikke altid sikkert eller hygiejnisk for personalet at separerer
produkter eller emballage fra hinanden for at sortere dem.

skal sorteres korrekt, og hele det genanvendelige potentiale skal udnyttes.

E
EXCEL WORKSHEET
Excel sheet labellel, "Thesis_calculations.xlsx", was used for all of the calculations made in
connection with each research area. In addition, it was used to observe the results from the LCIA,
and for the interpretation. It is composed of the following sheets:
• Title page
• Regions: Presents all of the numerical data obtained for each of the regions (e.g., the recycling
rates, waste productions, populations, and so on)
• Waste data: Presents the different results from each of the different waste audits by Sund
(2020), Absalon (2021) and Backer (2016).
• Barrier survey: Presents all barrier survey results and calculations.
• LCA inputs: Presents all of the material fractions enterred into the EASETECH models.
• Results for each scenario: Presents the EASETECH LCIA results.
• LCA aggregated results: Presents the heatmap tables of the LCA results for both the Sund
(2020) data and the average Data, the process contribution results to the Climate Change impact
category, and the heatmap table of the percentage change in results from inputting the Sund
(2020) data to the average Data.
